Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
15 min
Definition of Usul al-Fiqh and the Term 'Asl'
Defining usul al-fiqh, analyzing 'asl', and showing the discipline's role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define usul al-fiqh as the general evidences for fiqh, identify the four technical meanings of 'asl', and explain how the major premise in fiqh argumentation is derived from usul.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh is the methodological evidence foundation; 'asl' when added to a science means evidence.

In this passage, the author of Fawatih al-Rahamut establishes the bedrock of Hanafi usul. He unpacks the compound term usul al-fiqh, focusing on the word 'asl' which carries four distinct technical meanings. He then clarifies that only one of these—evidence (dalil)—is intended when 'asl' is appended to a discipline like fiqh. Without this clarity, the entire science of usul would be misconstrued.

Guided Path
1
Parse the compound term
The definition of usul al-fiqh depends on knowing the two parts: 'asl' and 'fiqh'. Start by noting that the text first addresses the meaning of 'asl'.
The author uses a top-down approach: define the whole by its components.
2
Identify the four technical meanings of 'asl'
The text explicitly lists four usages: preponderant (rajih), continued (mustashab), rule (qa'ida), and evidence (dalil). Recognize each with its example.
These are technical, not linguistic; the linguistic meaning is 'that on which something is built'.
3
Determine the intended meaning when 'asl' is added to a science
The author argues, following al-Mufid, that when 'asl' is added to a science (like usul al-fiqh), it means 'evidence'. This is because the foundation of a science is its evidence.
He rejects the idea that it means 'rule' here, calling it remote.
4
Understand the definition of usul al-fiqh
After analyzing the parts, the text defines usul al-fiqh as 'the general evidences for fiqh needed when applying the detailed evidences to their rulings'.
This highlights the methodological role of usul.
5
See how usul supplies the major premise in fiqh reasoning
The passage illustrates with the zakat example: the major premise 'everything commanded is obligatory' is an usul principle. Without usul, the inference is incomplete.
This shows the practical utility of the science.

إليه إلا في معرفة كيفية الإنتاج، ولا توجد مقدمة دليلها من مسألة منطقية، وربما يشكل بمباحث القياس فإنها لا يحتاج إليها إلا في كيفية إنتاجه كيف وأن القياس مفيد للحكم بنفسه من غير ضم أمر آخر لكن ليس لك (بموادها وصورها من أفراد موضوع مسائل الأصول فإن الدليل التفصيلي لوجوب الزكاة آتوا الزكاة من إفراد الأمر، ولحرمة الربا ولا تَأْكُلُوا الرّبوا أَضْعَافًا مُّضَعَفَةٌ) [آل عمران : ١٣٠] من أفراد النهي (بخلاف المنطق الباحث عن المعقولات (الثانية فإن الدلائل «الفلسفية ليست بموادها معروضة معه
As for the introduction, it concerns the definition of usul al-fiqh – i.e., the comprehensive and exclusive definer. It can be left on its reality based on allowing that the mentioned is a real definition. And its subject is that which investigates its essential accidents that occur to the thing for itself or for what equals it. And its goal is what results from its acquisition. Then this name has a linguistic and a technical meaning, so he pointed to its interpretation in both respects. He said: 'As for its definition when added, it depends on knowing the definition of al-asl, which is the added term, and al-fiqh, which is the term to which it is added.' So al-asl linguistically: that on which something else is built, either as its material in reality like clay is the asl of a pot, or in common consideration like the reality is called the asl of a metaphor. And technically: al-rajih (the preponderant) as in 'the Book is asl relative to qiyas' i.e. preponderant; al-mustashab (the continued) as in 'the purity of water is asl'; al-qa'ida (rule) as in 'the doer is marfu' is an asl from the usul of grammar'; and al-dalil (evidence) as in 'establish prayer' is asl for the obligation of prayer. So the word asl is a technical homonym for these four. And the establishment of a lexical meaning requires evidence; rather, one might claim figurativeness in some of these meanings due to their understanding by context. It was benefited in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that when asl is added to a science, what is meant is its evidence, without doubt. But not because the word asl is used in the sense of evidence; how so? If that were the case, it would require double transfer. Rather, the word asl is used in its linguistic meaning, and when added to a science, the meaning becomes the foundation of the science, and its foundation is nothing but evidence. Thus, in this way, evidence is intended, not in the first way. This is the statement of al-Mufid and the author in the gloss. So whoever takes usul here as rules has ignored the apparent meaning of this asl, for the rules of a science are its issues, not its principles. If asl here meant rule, the meaning would be 'the issues of fiqh', which is a reversal. Know that there is no doubt about the remoteness of taking asl as rule, but it has some validity by making the addition for the least relation, i.e., issues that have a connection to fiqh. In that case, the objection is not raised. Then this science – i.e., the science of usul – [is] the general evidences for fiqh needed when applying the specific detailed evidences concerning each issue to their rulings. Because if the evidence is formulated in the form of the first figure, its major premise is taken from the usul, whether it is exactly a particular usul issue, or embedded in it, or taken from multiple issues. And if the evidence is formulated in the form of the exceptional syllogism, the entailment is taken from it, as in our saying: Zakat is obligatory because of His statement, 'and give zakat' (4:77). If we want to apply it to its ruling, we say: Zakat is commanded by Allah, and everything commanded by Him is obligatory (because the command indicates obligation). So this major premise is taken from an usul issue. Then, the universality of that major premise requires qualifications: every commanded thing by a command that is not abrogated, not opposed by a preponderant or equal, and not interpreted, is obligatory. So to complete this proposition, knowledge of issues of abrogation, conflict, and interpretation is required. Thus, this major premise is taken from multiple issues. Similarly, if formulated by the exceptional syllogism: If zakat were commanded, it would be obligatory, and the antecedent is true, so zakat is obligatory. The entailment is taken from our saying: the command indicates obligation. Hence it is clear that the science of usul has a special relationship with fiqh that it does not have with other disciplines. As for logic, its relation to philosophy, usul, and fiqh is the same; it is only needed for knowing the method of production, and no premise has its evidence from a logical issue. One might object with the topics of qiyas, for they are only needed for how to produce it, and qiyas gives a ruling by itself without adding another thing. But you cannot blunder, for qiyas does not give a Shari' ruling except by considering that the Lawgiver considered the predominant probability resulting from it; then a Shari' ruling is only established by that this ruling has been reached by qiyas, and everything reached by qiyas is from Allah, established. So the second proposition is taken from usul. As for mere qiyas without this proposition, it does not establish that this ruling is from Allah so that one must act by it. But this proposition requires qualifications, so knowledge of whether qiyas can be abrogated or not, etc., is needed. With what we mentioned, the apparent objection that some usul issues are not suitable as major premises – like our saying 'qiyas is not an abrogator nor abrogated' – is repelled, for we do not claim their occurrence exactly, but rather it is more general than it and what is taken individually or from other issues collectively. Thus it has become clear to you that fiqh's need for usul is greater, and its relation to fiqh is not like the relation of a balance to philosophy, as was mistakenly thought. That is apparent. And as for what the author mentioned in his saying: 'For the detailed fiqh evidences specific to each issue, in their matter and form, are individuals of the subject of usul issues' – for the detailed evidence for the obligation of zakat 'give zakat' is an individual of command, and for the prohibition of usury 'do not eat usury multiplied' (3:130) is an individual of prohibition. Unlike logic, which investigates secondary intelligibles, for the philosophical evidences are not presented with their matter to it.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a definition and exposition of usul al-fiqh. The author first discusses the definition of usul al-fiqh as a compound term. He notes that defining it requires knowing the meaning of 'asl' (foundation) and 'fiqh' (understanding). He then explains that 'asl' linguistically means that on which something else is built. Technically, it is used in four senses: (1) the preponderant (rajih), e.g. the Book is asl over qiyas; (2) the continued (mustashab), e.g. the original purity of water; (3) a rule (qa'ida), e.g. grammatical rules; and (4) evidence (dalil), e.g. the verse on prayer is asl for obligation. Thus 'asl' is a homonym. The author clarifies that when 'asl' is added to a science (e.g. usul al-fiqh), it means the evidence or foundation of that science. He criticizes those who take 'usul' here as rules, arguing that would mean 'the rules of fiqh' instead of 'the principles of fiqh', which is incorrect. He then defines usul al-fiqh itself as the general evidences for fiqh, needed when applying specific evidences to rulings. He illustrates with the example of zakat: the major premise 'everything commanded is obligatory' is taken from usul (command indicates obligation), and this premise needs qualifications from issues like abrogation, conflict, and interpretation. He contrasts usul with logic, emphasizing that usul has a unique relationship with fiqh. Finally, he explains that qiyas (analogy) only yields a Shari' ruling because the Lawgiver accepts the predominant probability, and that the second premise in the qiyas argument is from usul. The passage firmly establishes the necessity and special status of usul al-fiqh within Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
أصول الفقه (Usul al-Fiqh): The general evidences for fiqh, needed when applying specific detailed evidences to their rulings.
الأصل (al-Asl): A term that technically has four meanings: preponderant (rajih), continued (mustashab), rule (qa'ida), and evidence (dalil).
الدليل (al-Dalil): Evidence or proof that leads to a ruling. In usul, it is the fourth meaning of 'asl'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the word 'asl' is added to a branch of knowledge (like usul al-fiqh), what is the intended meaning?
A) Al-rajih (the preponderant)
B) Al-dalil (the evidence)
C) Al-qa'ida (the rule)
D) Al-mustashab (the continued)

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh is the general evidence and principles that inform the derivation of fiqh rulings; 'asl' when added to a science specifically means evidence foundation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
6 min
Definition of Usul al-Fiqh and its Subject Matter
Explaining the meaning of 'usul' and the subject of usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of usul al-fiqh as the science of general proofs (adilla ijmalia) and explain how a fiqh ruling is derived using a usul major premise, as illustrated by the obligation of zakat.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh provides the major premises that transform textual commands into legal rulings.

This passage lays the foundation for understanding what usul al-fiqh actually is. It is not simply a collection of rules but a science that supplies the general principles—like 'command indicates obligation'—without which no fiqh ruling can be derived from the Quran and Sunnah. The author uses the example of zakat to show how a single usul premise operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the meaning of 'usul'
The commentator explains that 'usul' in 'usul al-fiqh' does not mean the foundations of the science, but rather the rules (qawa'id) that serve as premises for deriving fiqh rulings.
This nuance is critical to avoid confusing usul with mabadi'.
2
Understand the formal definition
The passage defines usul al-fiqh as 'general proofs for fiqh that are needed when applying the detailed proofs to their rulings'. This means usul provides the overarching principles that give meaning to specific textual evidences.
The phrase 'adilla ijmalia' is key.
3
Trace the derivation example
Follow the text's reasoning: The Quranic verse (4:77) commands zakat. The usul principle 'al-amr li al-wujub' (command indicates obligation) is applied to produce the ruling that zakat is obligatory. This shows how a major premise from usul is used.
The conditions (no abrogation, etc.) also come from usul.
4
Distinguish usul from logic
The author contrasts usul al-fiqh with logic. Logic is a universal tool, whereas usul is specifically tied to fiqh because its premises are drawn from fiqh-related concepts and are directly applied to fiqh issues.
This removes the misconception that usul is just another theoretical science.
5
Appreciate the necessity of usul
By showing that even the example of zakat requires multiple usul sub-issues (abrogation, opposition, interpretation), the passage demonstrates that fiqh is inseparable from usul.
Fiqh's need for usul is stronger than philosophy's need for logic.

والتأويل، فهذه الكبرى مأخوذة من عدة تلك المسائل، وكذا إن حرر بالقياس الاستثنائي لو كانت الزكاة مأمورة لكانت واجبة والمقدم ،حق فالزكاة واجبة، فالملازمة مأخوذة من قولنا : الأمر للوجوب فقد بان بهذا أن لعلم الأصول خصوصية بالفقه ليس له تلك الخصوصية بغيره، أما المنطق فنسبته إلى «الفلسفة» و«الأصول» و«الفقه»، نسبة واحدة، ولا يحتاج إليه إلا في معرفة كيفية الإنتاج، ولا توجد مقدمة دليلها من مسألة منطقية، الحكم من الله تعالى حتى يجب العمل ،به لكن لا بد لصحة هذه القضية من قيود فلا بد من معرفة أن القياس هل يكون منسوخاً أولاً وغير ذلك، وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما يتراءى وروده من أن بعض مسائل الأصول لا يصلح للكبروية كقولنا : القياس لا يكون ناسخاً ولا منسوخاً لأنا لا ندعي وقوعها بعينها، بل أعم منه ومن المأخوذ به انفراداً أو منها من غيرها اجتماعاً فقد ظهر لك أن حاجة الفقه إلى الأصول أشد وليس نسبته إلى الفقه كنسبة الميزان إلى الفلسفة كما وهم
This principle is that the rules of a science are its issues, not its principles. If 'usul' here meant 'base', then the meaning would be 'issues of fiqh', which is a reversal. Know that there is no doubt in the distance of taking 'usul' to mean 'base', but it has a type of validity that makes the addition for the slightest connection, i.e. issues that have a relation to fiqh, and then the appendix is not rejected. Then this science, i.e. the science of usul, is 'general proofs for fiqh that are needed when applying the detailed proofs specific to each issue to its rulings'. Because if the proof is formulated in the form of the first figure, its major premise is taken from usul, whether it is a specific usul issue itself, or integrated in it, or taken from several issues. And if the proof is formulated in the form of exceptional syllogism, the implication is taken from them, like our saying: Zakat is obligatory because of His statement: 'And give zakah' [Al-Nisa 4:77]. So if we want to apply it to its ruling, we say: Zakat is commanded by Allah, and everything commanded by Him is obligatory (because the command indicates obligation). So this major premise is taken from a usul issue. Then, for the correctness of the universality of that major premise, there must be conditions: 'Every commanded thing with a command not abrogated, not opposed by a preponderant or equal, and not interpreted, is obligatory.' So to complete this proposition, knowledge of issues of abrogation, opposition, and interpretation is necessary. So this major premise is taken from several of those issues. Similarly, if formulated by exceptional syllogism: If zakat were commanded, it would be obligatory, and the antecedent is true, so zakat is obligatory. So the implication is taken from our statement: The command indicates obligation. Thus it is clear that the science of usul has a specificity with fiqh that it does not have with others. As for logic, its relation to philosophy, usul, and fiqh is one relation, and it is only needed to know the manner of production, and no premise of a proof is taken from a logical issue. Perhaps an objection arises regarding the topics of qiyas, for they are only needed for the manner of its production. How so, and qiyas gives a ruling by itself without combining another matter? But you cannot be careless, for qiyas does not give a shar'i ruling except by considering that the Lawgiver considered the predominant conjecture obtained from it. So then a shar'i ruling is not established except by saying: This ruling is reached by qiyas, and everything reached by qiyas is established from Allah. So the second proposition is taken from usul. As for mere qiyas without this proposition, it does not indicate that this ruling is from Allah such that action becomes obligatory. But for the correctness of this proposition, conditions are needed, so knowledge is needed whether qiyas can be abrogated or not, etc. By what we have mentioned, what appears to occur is repelled: that some usul issues are not suitable as major premises, like our saying: Qiyas cannot be abrogating nor abrogated. For we do not claim that they occur exactly, but general than it, and from what is taken singly or from them along with others. Thus it has become clear to you that fiqh's need for usul is more severe, and its relation to fiqh is not like the balance to philosophy, as was erroneously thought.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter and nature of usul al-fiqh as a science. The author begins by clarifying the meaning of 'usul' in the compound 'usul al-fiqh'—it does not mean 'foundations' (mabadi) but rather 'rules' (qawa'id) that serve as premises for fiqh. He then gives a precise definition: usul al-fiqh is the science of general proofs (adilla ijmalia) that are needed when applying the detailed proofs to particular rulings. The commentator illustrates this with the example of deriving the obligation of zakat from the Quranic command. He shows how the major premise 'every commanded thing is obligatory' is taken from a usul principle (that command indicates obligation), and further conditions (no abrogation, etc.) require additional usul sub-topics. The passage also contrasts usul al-fiqh with logic, arguing that fiqh's need for usul is more intimate than philosophy's need for logic, because the detailed proofs of fiqh are themselves particular instances of usul concepts, while logical premises are never derived from logic itself. This is a foundational definition of the science.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): Here it means 'rule' (qa'ida) or 'premise' that is used to derive fiqh rulings, not the foundation of the science (mabda).
أدلة إجمالية (adilla ijmalia): General proofs that are not specific to any particular fiqh issue but provide the major premises for deduction, e.g., 'command indicates obligation'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, how is the obligation of zakat derived using usul al-fiqh?
A) By direct text from the Quran without any intermediary principle.
B) By applying the major premise that every commanded act is obligatory to the command in the Quran.
C) By qiyas with other forms of worship.
D) By consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh is the science that supplies the general principles (adilla ijmalia) needed to transform detailed textual proofs into definitive fiqh rulings.

The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh and Exclusion of Taqlid
Defining fiqh and excluding the muqallid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of the Hanafi definition of fiqh and explain why the muqallid's knowledge is excluded from that definition.

Core Idea
Fiqh is evidence-based knowledge of shar'i rulings; blind following without evidence is not fiqh.

This passage clarifies the technical meaning of 'fiqh' in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It goes beyond a simple definition to explain why the knowledge of a muqallid (one who follows without understanding the evidence) does not qualify as fiqh. The commentator also engages with a terminological debate about the scope of fiqh—whether it is limited to physical actions or also includes actions of the heart.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the definition's components
Identify the genus (knowledge) and differentiae (shar'i rulings, detailed proofs) that constitute the definition of fiqh.
The passage says fiqh is knowledge of shar'i rulings derived from their specific proofs.
2
Locate the exclusion clause
Find where the text states that the muqallid's knowledge is not called fiqh and note the reason given.
The muqallid is excluded because he falls short of capability and does not derive rulings from evidence.
3
Analyze the supporting proof
Identify the dalil used to justify excluding the muqallid: the Prophetic praise of fiqh.
Since the Prophet praised fiqh, it must be a praiseworthy knowledge; that which does not deserve praise (taqlid) cannot be fiqh.
4
Assess the terminological expansion
Examine the commentator's argument for including actions of the heart in the definition of fiqh.
The commentator argues that tasawwuf is a later discipline, so it is more appropriate to keep the original broad usage of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفقه حكمة) أي أمر واقعي (فرعية) متفرعة على الإيمان بالذات والصفات والثواب والمعاد (شرعية ثابتة بأدلة شرعية (فلا يقال على) فقه (المقلد لتقصيره عن (الطاقة فلا يكون في تقليده مستحقاً للمدح، والفقه قد مدح في كلام الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وصحبه وسلم، فإذا ما حصل له تقليد إلا يسمى فقهاً، وحينئذ سقط ما يظن في بادىء الرأي أنه لا دخل لحديث التقصير وأنه إن أخذ في مفهومه الأدلة العلم من فيخرج، وإلا لا والتخصيص بالحسبات التي هي العمليات المتعلقة بالجوارح احترازاً عن التصوف الباحث عن أفعال القلوب كوجوب التوبة وحرمة البخل والكبر ووجوب الرضا بقضاء الله تعالى وتقديره حديث (محدث) لم يكن هذا في عصر الصحابة والتابعين ولا غاية في تغيير الإصطلاح أيضاً، فالأليق أن يكون الفقه عاماً لأعمال الجوارح والقلب
And fiqh is wisdom, i.e. a real matter, subsidiary, branching off from faith in the essence, attributes, reward, and resurrection, shar'i, established by shar'i proofs. So it is not said of the fiqh of the muqallid (blind follower), because of his falling short of capability, so in his taqlid he is not deserving of praise. And fiqh has been praised in the speech of the Messenger (peace and blessings upon him and his family). So if he obtains taqlid, it is not called fiqh. Thus falls what is initially thought that the hadith of falling short has no entry, and that if the concepts of evidence are taken, the people of knowledge would be included, otherwise not. And the specification by acts (al-hasbat) which are practical matters related to limbs is a precaution against tasawwuf, which investigates actions of the heart like the obligation of repentance, prohibition of miserliness and pride, and obligation of satisfaction with Allah's decree. (Muhdath) This [tasawwuf] did not exist in the era of the Companions and Successors, nor is there any benefit in changing the terminology. So it is more appropriate that fiqh be general for actions of the limbs and the heart.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines fiqh as knowledge of shar'i rulings derived from their specific proofs (adilla tafseeliyya). It excludes the knowledge of a muqallid (one who blindly follows), because such knowledge does not involve understanding of the evidence and thus does not deserve the commendable title of 'fiqh' that the Prophet praised. The commentator also notes that the traditional definition restricts fiqh to rulings concerning physical actions (acts of the limbs), but argues that it would be more appropriate to include actions of the heart as well, since disciplines like tasawwuf are relatively new and the early usage was broader.

Key Terms
فقه (fiqh): Knowledge of shar'i rulings derived from their detailed evidences; understanding that requires evidence-based reasoning, excluding taqlid.
مقلد (muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowing the evidence; such knowledge is not called fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he does not act on his knowledge.
B) Because it does not involve knowledge of the detailed proofs (adilla tafseeliyya).
C) Because the Prophet did not praise taqlid.
D) Because he lacks sincerity.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is not simply knowing rulings; it is knowing them with evidence—a distinction that elevates the scholar above the blind follower.

Defending the Definition of Fiqh: The Malaka Response
Critique and defense of the definition of fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi definition of fiqh as a malaka (firm capacity) resolves two key objections and distinguish between fiqh as knowledge and fiqh as a faculty.

Core Idea
Fiqh is a malaka – a stable capacity to derive all rulings; a faqih may say 'I don't know' yet still be a faqih.

In this passage, a classical commentator defends the standard definition of fiqh – 'knowledge of shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences' – by responding to two powerful objections. The first objection targets the completeness of the definition: if 'shar'i rulings' means all rulings, even great imams like Abu Hanifa admitted ignorance in some cases, so they would be excluded. The second objection targets exclusivity: if 'shar'i rulings' means any ruling, then a muqallid who knows a few rulings with evidence would be included. The defense hinges on the Hanafi concept of malaka (firm capacity) which is the core of being a faqih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections to the definition of fiqh
Read the first part of the passage that says 'If [shar'i rulings] means all, then it is not convertible... If [it] means absolute, then it is not restrictive...' Identify both conditions and what they would entail: exclusion of great jurists and inclusion of muqallids.
The objection is a classic 'constructive critique' – it tests the definition for being comprehensive and exclusive.
2
Locate the first answer: fiqh as malaka
The passage says 'the answer is given by choosing the first alternative, and that [fiqh] means the malaka to obtain knowledge of all rulings.' Understand that malaka is a capacity, not actual knowledge of every single rule. The imam who says 'I don't know' still has the capacity but may not have exerted it or faced a temporary obstacle.
This distinguishes between possession of the faculty (malaka) and its actual exercise.
3
Locate the second answer: distinction between fiqh and faqih
For the second objection, the answer chooses the second alternative and asserts that a muqallid's knowledge of some rulings with evidence is indeed fiqh (the knowledge), but he is not called a faqih because he lacks the malaka. The term faqih requires the stable capacity.
This is a terminological distinction within the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the reference to Sadr al-Shari'ah
The passage mentions Sadr al-Shari'ah's concept of 'remote capacity' (al-tahayyu' al-ba'id) vs 'near capacity' (al-qarib). The remote capacity is present in everyone, even the layman, but the near capacity is not well-defined. The passage interprets malaka as the power by which one can obtain knowledge of any ruling he desires – this is well-defined.
This refines the definition of malaka to be something real and measurable.
5
Synthesize the overall defense
Combine both answers: The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of shar'i rulings from detailed evidences' is saved by making 'knowledge' refer to the capacity (malaka) for all rulings, not actual exhaustive knowledge, and by reserving the title 'faqih' for those who possess that capacity. Thus the definition remains both comprehensive and restrictive.
The defense is successful within the Hanafi framework and shows the importance of usul concepts in protecting definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Yes, the exclusion of Kalam, even though it is also a later innovation, not existing among the Companions and Successors. For this reason, Imam [Abu Hanifa] called it 'the greater fiqh', and defined fiqh broadly to include it: 'knowledge of the self of what is for it and against it'. But it is a known construct among the later scholars, so there is no harm in excluding it. And they defined fiqh as 'knowledge of shar'i rulings'. Apparently it is intended that the relation occurs, not first its occurrence? Then 'rulings' guards against simple perceptions, and 'shar'i' guards against things like 'the sky is a sphere' and 'fire is burning', etc., so Kalam is excluded. It is also possible that 'shar'i ruling' means the effect of the discourse, not the discourse itself, as is known. Then the definition is 'from their detailed evidences', i.e. knowledge obtained from their detailed evidences specific to each issue. This guards against the knowledge of the muqallid, the knowledge of Jibril, and the knowledge of Allah (mighty and magnificent). So there is no need to add the condition of istidlal (deduction), except for further clarity. Then the definition is comprehensive and restrictive. An objection is raised on this definition: If shar'i rulings means 'all', then it is not convertible, because the fiqh of great jurists like Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Malik would be excluded – for 'I do not know' is known from them. If it means 'absolute' (any rulings, even few), then it is not restrictive, because it would include the knowledge of a muqallid who knows some issues with evidence. The answer is given by choosing the first alternative, and that [fiqh] means the malaka (faculty) to obtain knowledge of all rulings. And 'I do not know' does not harm, because the malaka does not entail actual possession; it may be delayed due to an obstacle. Or it is decided by choosing the second alternative, committing that the muqallid's knowledge of some rulings with evidence is fiqh, but he is not called a faqih, for the faqih is one for whom fiqh is a malaka. Consider. And what Sadr al-Shari'a said: 'The remote capacity is present in everyone, even the muqallid, even the layman, but the near capacity is not well-defined.' It is interpreted as the power by which a person can obtain knowledge of any ruling he desires, and this capacity is well-defined. So reflect. And also answered ...
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defends the standard definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences' by addressing two objections. First, if 'shar'i rulings' means all rulings, the definition would exclude even great imams like Abu Hanifa and Malik who admitted ignorance in some issues. The response is that fiqh is a malaka (capacity) to know all rulings, not actual knowledge of every single one. Second, if it means any ruling, it would include the muqallid's limited evidence-based knowledge. The response here is to distinguish between having fiqh (even one ruling) and being a faqih (which requires the malaka). The commentator also references Sadr al-Shari'a's discussion of 'remote capacity' vs. 'near capacity', affirming that the capacity to acquire knowledge is well-defined. The text ends with another answer beginning but cut off.

Key Terms
ملكة (malaka): A firm capacity (al-qawwa) through which a person can obtain knowledge of any shar'i ruling he desires. The faqih is one who possesses this malaka.
استدلال (istidlal): The act of deducing rulings from evidence; the definition includes this implicitly, as 'knowledge from detailed evidences'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does saying 'I do not know' not negate a jurist's status as a faqih?
A) Because jurists were known to be humble and actually knew all rulings.
B) Because fiqh is defined as malaka (capacity) to obtain knowledge, not actual possession of every ruling.
C) Because the definition of fiqh was later changed to exclude such statements.
D) Because the jurist's followers made up for his gaps.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is a malaka (firm capacity) to derive all shar'i rulings; a faqih may not know every ruling individually but possesses the faculty to acquire that knowledge when needed.

Fiqh as Certain Knowledge of Obligation: Defining the Hanafi Position
Defends a definition of fiqh against objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh as knowledge of the obligation to act, explain why it is considered certain despite conjectural rulings, and evaluate Sadr al-Shari'a's alternative definition.

Core Idea
Fiqh is certain knowledge of the obligation to act, not the rulings themselves.

This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut defends the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the obligation to act upon Shar'i rulings' against objections that most fiqh is conjectural. The author grounds the certainty in the definitive consensus (ijma' qati') that a mujtahid's conjecture is binding, distinguishing the mujtahid from the muqallid, and concluding that the knowledge of obligation is certain even if rulings are not. We will trace the argument and examine Sadr al-Shari'a's alternative definition focusing on certainties and the faculty of deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under discussion
Find the key phrase: 'knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings obtained from indicators'. Understand that 'indicators' (al-imarat) refer to proofs that lead to conjecture.
The definition emphasizes practical obligation (wujub al-'amal) rather than theoretical knowledge.
2
Grasp the role of ijma' in establishing certainty
Note the claim that the mujtahid's conjecture is binding by definitive consensus (ijma' qati'). This consensus makes the knowledge of the obligation to act certain, even if the substantive ruling itself is conjectural.
This is the key move: the obligation is certain because it is backed by consensus.
3
Distinguish the mujtahid from the muqallid
The definition excludes the muqallid because he does not obtain knowledge through his own reasoning. However, the passage addresses the 'discerning muqallid' who might have some direct knowledge; the response is that his conjecture does not obligate action in the same way.
The distinction preserves the definition's inclusiveness of the mujtahid.
4
Counter the objection that fiqh is mostly conjectural
The objection: if fiqh rulings are mostly dhanni, how can fiqh be called 'ilm? The answer: 'ilm here is the knowledge of the obligation to act, which is qat'i. The opponent's disagreement is after consensus and does not damage our certainty.
The author adds that certainty varies by person; the opponent's denial does not affect the truth.
5
Evaluate Sadr al-Shari'a's alternative definition
Sadr al-Shari'a defines fiqh as the certain rulings (qat'iyyat) and the faculty of deduction (malakat al-istinbat). This excludes conjectural rulings like those from qiyas and khabar wahid. The author notes this exclusion is a 'commitment without proof' and that the praised fiqh includes the faculty of deduction.
This shows the spectrum of positions: the author's preferred definition includes both certain and conjectural rulings via the certainty of obligation.

Source Text

بهذا الجواب أصلاً (نعم) يرد عليه أنه يلزم أن يكون) الفقه حينئذ (عبارة عن العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام لا العلم بها وبطلانه ضروري (إلا أن يقال إنه رسم فيجوز (باللوازم كما هو شأن الرسوم (وفيه ما فيه فإن الفقه من باب (الظنون أي أكثره فكيف يكون علماً فلا يصدق التعريف على أكثر أفراد المعرف وجه الاندفاع أن الفقه عبارة عن العلم بوجوب العمل، وهو قطعي لا ريب فيه ثابت بالإجماع القاطع بل ضروري في الدين، وإن كان معرفة الأحكام على سبيل الظن ولا يعبأ بالمخالف لأنه نشأ بعد الإجماع، وإنما لا يكفر لأنه لا يسلم الإجماع، ومنكر الإجماع إنما يكفر إذا أنكر بعد تسليم تحقق الإجماع، والقطع يختلف باختلاف الأشخاص كما سيجيء في بحث الأمر إن شاء الله تعالى فخلاف المخالف لا يضر القطع وللجواب تحرير آخر هو أن الفقه عبارة عن العلم بالأحكام الحاصل بالإمارات لأجل وجوب العمل بحسبه، والمقلد وإن حصل له العلم من الإمارة لكن لا يجب عليه العمل بهذا العلم فإن قلت المقلد يعلم وجوب العمل بقول المجتهد، وهذا حكم أيضاً، فقد علم بعض الأحكام لأجل العمل، قلت: لا يعلم هذا الحكم عن دليله التفصيلي، بل إما يعلمه ضرورة من الدين أو بالتقليد المحض، وعلى هذا التحرير لا يرد عليه ما أورد بقوله : نعم يرد لكن لا يندفع به إيراد ظنية الفقه، بل يحتاج في دفعه إلى العلاوة التي أشار إليها بقوله (على أن العلم حقيقة فيما ليس بتصور أيضاً فيتناول الظن واليقين، وهو المراد في خطبة الكتاب للشارح أن تعريف الفقه، فلا إيراد، ثم أن دعوى كون العلم حقيقة فيما يتناول الظن أيضاً لا يخلو عن لأنه مخالف لكتب اللغة والأحرى أن يقول : مستعمل فيه استعمالاً شائعاً، فلا بأس بإرادة هذا المعنى (وبعضهم) وهو الإمام صدر الشريعة (جعل الفقه عبارة عن الأحكام القطعية ملكة الاستنباط وعلى هذا يندفع الإيرادان، أما الأول : فلأنا نختار شقاً ثالثاً وهو: أن المراد البعض المعين الذي هو القطعيات فلا إيراد، وأما الثاني فلأن الفقه حينئذ علم قطعي ويلزم عليه خروج المسائل الثابتة بالأدلة الظنية كالقياس وخبر الواحد (وهي كثيرة، ألا ترى أن السنة المتواترة قليلة جداً وكذا الإجماعات، فالقطعيات أقل القليل، فإن قلت إنه يلتزم خروجها قال : ( والتزام ذلك التزام بلا لزوم من حجة، ولعل حجته أن الظن مذموم من الشارع لا كمال فيه وأقله أنه لا يصلح للمدح عليه، وإنما اعتبر ضرورة العمل، وإذ قد ثبت من الشارع والصحابة مدح الفقهاء علم أنه علم قطعي، ثم إنهم إذ لم يطلقوا الفقيه إلا على من له ملكة الاستنباط علم أن مقارنتها أيضاً معتبرة في الفقه الممدوح والكلام فيه
By choosing the second part and saying that by 'proofs' is meant indicators, and by 'knowledge of rulings' is meant knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings obtained from indicators. And here, knowledge of the obligation to act is through the medium of the conjecture obtained in the path, because the mujtahid's conjecture is obligatory to act upon by definitive consensus. And obtaining knowledge of the obligation to act through the medium of conjecture is a characteristic of the mujtahid by consensus, with no share for the muqallid in it. As for the muqallid, his basis is the statement of his mujtahid, so he acts upon what the mujtahid gives a fatwa, not his own conjecture, nor the mujtahid's conjecture. Then the definition is not disturbed because it excludes the muqallid's knowledge, since he does not have knowledge of the obligation to act. But I suspect that this does not repel the problem, because it will come that ijtihad is divisible, and we are discussing the muqallid who has obtained some issues from their proofs, so knowledge of the obligation to know through the medium of conjecture from the indicator applies to his knowledge, for acting upon his own conjecture is also obligatory. So know the difference between the mujtahid and his muqallid, so that you do not say like the one who said: just as the mujtahid's conjecture is obligatory to act upon, so too upon his muqallid, so both are equal, so the muqallid is not excluded. And in it is like what passed: the discussion is about the discerning muqallid, not the commoner, who says: the conjecture of my mujtahid which he obtained from such-and-such proof is binding upon me. So the discerning muqallid and the mujtahid are equal. So better to answer that by 'conjecture' is meant a conjecture that obligates action, and his conjecture obtained from the indicator does not obligate action, for that is the affair of the mujtahid. And the truth is that the partial mujtahid is not excluded by this answer at all. Yes, it is objected that fiqh then would be the knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, not knowledge of the rulings themselves, and its falsity is necessary. Unless it is said that it is a definition (rasm), so it is permissible to define by concomitants as is the way of definitions. But there is something in it: knowledge of the obligation to act, although it is concomitant to knowledge of rulings in existence, is not predicated upon it, so it is not permissible to define by it either, unless it is permissible to define by a different thing. And from here, from what has been clarified about the outcome of the definition, you know the repulsion of what was said: fiqh is from the category of conjectures, most of it, so how can it be knowledge? So the definition does not apply to most instances of the defined. The repulsion is that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act, and it is certain with no doubt, established by definitive consensus, rather necessary in the religion, even if knowledge of rulings is by way of conjecture. And no attention is paid to the opponent because he arose after consensus, and he is not deemed a disbeliever only because he does not acknowledge the consensus, and the denier of consensus is only deemed a disbeliever if he denies after acknowledging the realization of consensus. And certainty differs according to persons, as will come in the discussion of command, so the opponent's opposition does not harm certainty. And there is another formulation for the answer: fiqh is knowledge of rulings obtained from indicators for the sake of acting accordingly, and the muqallid, even if he obtains knowledge from the indicator, is not obligated to act upon this knowledge. If you say: the muqallid knows the obligation to act upon the mujtahid's statement, and this is also a ruling, so he has known some rulings for the sake of action. I say: he does not know this ruling from its detailed proof, but rather he knows it either necessarily from the religion or by pure taqlid. And upon this formulation, what was objected to by 'Yes, it is objected' does not apply to it, but does not repel the objection of the conjectural nature of fiqh, rather it needs the extra point indicated by his statement: 'that knowledge is truth in what is not a concept as well, so it includes conjecture and certainty, and that is intended in the preface of the book by the commentator: that the definition of fiqh, so no objection.' Then the claim that knowledge is truth in what includes conjecture as well is not free, because it contradicts the lexicon books, and it is better that he say: it is used in a common usage, so there is no harm in intending this meaning. And some, who is Imam Sadr al-Shari'a, made fiqh an expression for the certain rulings and the faculty of deduction. And upon this, both objections are repelled. As for the first: we choose a third part, that by it is meant the specific part which is certainties, so no objection. As for the second, because fiqh then is certain knowledge, and it follows that issues established by conjectural proofs like qiyas and solitary reports are excluded, and they are many. Do you not see that the mutawatir sunnah is very little, and likewise consensus, so certainties are the least of the little. If you say: he commits to their exclusion, he said: that commitment is a commitment without a binding proof. Perhaps his proof is that conjecture is blameworthy from the Lawgiver, no perfection in it, and at least it is not fit for praise upon it, and it is only considered due to the necessity of action. And since praise of fuqaha' is established from the Lawgiver and the Companions, it is known that it is certain knowledge. Then since they did not apply the term faqih except to one who has the faculty of deduction, it is known that its accompaniment is also considered in the praised fiqh. And the discussion is about it.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the definition of fiqh, specifically defending the definition that fiqh is the knowledge of the obligation to act upon Shar'i rulings derived from indicators. The author, as commentator on Musallam al-Thubut, addresses several objections. First, he clarifies that the term 'knowledge' here refers to certainty (qat') regarding the obligation to act, even though the substantive rulings themselves may be conjectural. This certainty is established by the definitive consensus (ijma' qati') that a mujtahid's conjecture is binding. He distinguishes the mujtahid, who obtains this knowledge through his own reasoning, from the muqallid, who merely follows without that knowledge. A problem arises with the discerning muqallid (al-muqallid al-mumayyiz) who may arrive at certainty through indicators for some issues; the author suggests that such a person's conjecture does not obligate action in the same way, and the definition still excludes him. The objection that fiqh is mostly conjectural is countered by noting that the knowledge of the obligation to act is itself certain, regardless of the conjectural nature of the specific rulings. The author then presents an alternative formulation (tahrir) that fiqh is knowledge of rulings for the purpose of acting, which further distinguishes the muqallid. Finally, he discusses Sadr al-Shari'a's definition of fiqh as certain rulings and the faculty of deduction (malakat al-istinbat), which solves the issues of the definition applying to all instances but leads to the problem that many fiqh issues based on conjecture would be excluded. The author notes that this exclusion is a commitment without proof, and that praise from the Shari'a and Companions indicates that true fiqh is certain knowledge, with the faculty of deduction being essential for the praised faqih.

Key Terms
الفقہ (al-fiqh): Knowledge of the obligation to act upon Shar'i rulings obtained from detailed proofs (according to one definition), or certain rulings with the faculty of deduction (according to Sadr al-Shari'a).
الظن (al-dhann): Conjecture or probable opinion, as opposed to certainty. In usul, it is the basis of most fiqh rulings and is considered binding for a mujtahid.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): One who exerts effort to derive rulings from detailed proofs; his conjecture is binding by consensus.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowing the detailed proofs; his basis is the mujtahid's opinion, not his own conjecture.
الإجماع القاطع (al-ijma' al-qati'): Definitive consensus of the mujtahidun that is certain beyond doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the obligation to act' not undermined by the fact that many fiqh rulings are conjectural?
A) Because the definition is merely conventional and does not need to match reality.
B) Because the obligation to act upon the conjecture of a mujtahid is established by definitive consensus, making that knowledge certain.
C) Because all fiqh rulings are actually certain through textual proofs.
D) Because the muqallid's knowledge is also taken into account.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is defined as certain knowledge of the obligation to act, grounded in definitive consensus, not as certain knowledge of the rulings themselves.

Action in the Definition of Fiqh and the Titular Definition as Knowledge of Principles
Evaluates the inclusion of action in the definition of fiqh and presents the title definition as knowledge of universal principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two definitions of fiqh (supplemental and titular), evaluate the dispute over including action, and apply the titular definition.

Core Idea
Fiqh as a science is knowledge of universal principles; action is required only for praiseworthy fiqh, not for the technical term.

The definition of fiqh is not merely a terminological exercise—it determines who counts as a faqih. This passage tackles a key dispute: should action (amal) be part of fiqh's definition? The author first critiques the view of Imam Fakhr al-Islam, then reconciles it by distinguishing technical from praiseworthy fiqh. Finally, he presents the accepted titular definition of fiqh as a science of universal principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of fiqh
Distinguish the supplemental meaning (al-ma'na al-idafi) already discussed from the titular meaning (al-ma'na al-laqabi) about to be introduced. The titular definition is the formal definition of fiqh as a science.
The source uses 'hadduhu laqaban' to signal the shift.
2
Analyse the dispute over including action
State the position of Imam Fakhr al-Islam and the author's initial objection. Then note that the discussion shifts from technical terminology to the praiseworthy fiqh of the Shari'a.
The example of al-Hajjaj illustrates that mere knowledge does not earn the title in the praiseworthy sense.
3
Trace the resolution through the threefold division
Follow the three aspects of fiqh (knowledge of law, mastery, action) and the confusion that arose. Understand al-Kashf's clarification: 'fiqh' is a restricted truth (haqiqah qasirah) that applies in degrees.
This resolves the paradox of the sinful faqih: he is a faqih in one sense but not in another.
4
Confirm the titular definition
The passage concludes with the formal definition: fiqh by title is 'knowledge of principles (qawa'id)' that allows derivation of particular rulings. Note that action is absent from this definition.
This definition aligns fiqh with other recorded sciences (al-ilm al-mudawwanah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعل العمل داخلاً في تحديد هذا العلم أي الفقه كما ذهب إليه بعض مشايخنا وهو الإمام فخر الإسلام رحمه الله تعالى بعيد جداً عن الصواب لأن الفقه أحد أنواع العلم المدوّنة، وأيضاً يلزم أن لا يكون الفقيه الفاسق فقيهاً. هذا واعلم أنه ليس الكلام في أن الفقه في الاصطلاح ما هو، فإنه قليل الجدوى، ولكل مصطلح أن يصطلح على ما شاء، فلا كلام على أحد، بل الكلام في الفقه الذي مدح في كلام الشارع والصحابة والتابعين ما هو وحينئذ الحق مع الإمام فخر الإسلام، فإن المدح لا يستحقه الفاسق، فلا بد من اعتبار العمل ولا شناعة في التزام كون الفاسق العارف بالأحكام بالأدلة غير فقيه، كيف ولم يعد أحد الحجاج فقيهاً مع كونه عارفاً للأحكام هذا واعلم أنه رضي الله تعالى عنه قال : والنوع الثاني علم الفروع، وهو وأنه ثلاثة أقسام علم المشروع بنفسه، والقسم الثاني : إتقان المعرفة به، وهو معرفة النصوص بمعانيها وضبط الأصول بفروعها والقسم الثالث: هو العمل به، حتى لا يصير نفس العلم مقصوداً، فإذا تمت هذه الأوجه كان فقيهاً مطلقاً، وإلا فهو فقيه من وجه دون وجه، فتحير المحصلون في فهمه، فإن أول كلامه يدل على أن الفقه المجموع، وآخره يدل على أن العلم فقط أيضاً فقه بل العمل وحده وحرر صاحب الكشف أن الفقه هو المجموع والعلم والعمل، كل جزء له فالفقه المستعمل فيه حقيقة قاصرة فهو فقه من وجه دون وجه، ويمكن أن يكون مراده أن الفقه عبارة عن القدر المشترك بين المجموع ،والعلم فالعلم المقارن للعمل على سبيل اليقين أو أعم فقه مطلق أي فرد كامل وإلا أي وإن لم يكن مقارناً له، بل يكون علماً فقط، ولم يكن العالم عاملاً به فهو فقه من وجه دون وجه أي فرد ناقص، وحينئذ لا يرد خروج فقه الفاسق الفقيه من الفقه، ولم يحتج إلى الالتزام المذكور ومعرفة أمثال الحجاج الأحكام من الأدلة غير ظاهر، فلا يقوم دليلاً، ولا بعد في ممدوحية الفاسق من جهة العلم، فإنه مدح من وجه فتأمل، ولما فرغ عن الحد باعتبار المعنى الإضافي أراد أن يشرع في حد المعنى اللقبي فقال ( وأما ) حده لقباً فهو علم بقواعد أي قضايا كلية يتعرف بها أحوال أفراد الفقه
And making action included in the definition of this knowledge, i.e., fiqh, as held by some of our teachers, namely Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him), is very far from correctness, because fiqh is one of the recorded types of knowledge (al-ilm al-mudawwanah), and also it would necessitate that the sinful faqih not be a faqih. This, and know that the discussion is not about what fiqh is in technical terminology, for that is of little benefit, and any terminologist may define as he wishes, so there is no argument. Rather, the discussion is about what is the fiqh that is praised in the speech of the Lawgiver, the Companions, and the Followers. And in this case, the truth is with Imam Fakhr al-Islam, for the sinner does not deserve praise, so it is necessary to consider action, and there is no shame in holding that the sinner who knows the rulings from proofs is not a faqih. How? Did anyone ever consider al-Hajjaj a faqih despite his knowledge of rulings? And know that he (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'The second type is the science of furu', and it comprises three divisions: knowledge of the law itself; the second is mastery of knowledge of it, which is knowledge of the texts with their meanings and connecting the principles to their branches; and the third is action upon it, so that knowledge itself does not become the goal.' So if these aspects are complete, he is a faqih absolutely; otherwise, he is a faqih in one sense and not another. Thus, the researchers were perplexed in understanding it, for the beginning of his statement indicates that fiqh is the composite (al-majmu'), while the end indicates that knowledge alone is also fiqh, indeed action alone. And the author of al-Kashf refined: fiqh is the composite, and knowledge and action; each part of it: the word fiqh used therein is a restricted truth (haqiqah qasirah), so it is fiqh in one sense and not another. And it is possible that his intent is that fiqh is the common element between the composite and knowledge; so knowledge accompanied by action with certainty, or more general, is absolute fiqh, i.e., a perfect individual; otherwise, even if not accompanied by it, being only knowledge and the knower not acting upon it, then it is fiqh in one sense and not another, i.e., an imperfect individual. And in that case, the exclusion of the sinful faqih from fiqh does not arise, and one does not need the aforementioned commitment. And the knowledge of people like al-Hajjaj of rulings from proofs is not apparent, so it does not stand as evidence. And there is no objection in the praiseworthiness of a sinner from the aspect of knowledge, for it is praise in one sense. So reflect. And when he finished with the definition in terms of the supplemental meaning (al-ma'na al-idafi), he wanted to begin the definition in terms of the titular meaning (al-ma'na al-laqabi), so he said: 'As for its definition by title, it is knowledge of principles (qawa'id), i.e., universal propositions by which the states of the individuals of fiqh are known.'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the definition of fiqh, focusing on two issues. First, it criticizes the view of Imam Fakhr al-Islam (Precious of Islam) that action ('amal) should be included in the definition of fiqh, arguing that fiqh is a type of recorded knowledge (al-ilm al-mudawwanah) and that including action would exclude a sinful faqih from being called a faqih. However, the author then clarifies that the real discussion is about the praiseworthy fiqh (al-fiqh al-mamduh) in the language of the Shari'a, and in that sense, action is indeed required, because a sinner does not deserve praise. Thus, al-Hajjaj, despite his knowledge of rulings, is not considered a faqih because he was a tyrant. The author then cites a statement from a scholar (possibly Imam Fakhr al-Islam) dividing fiqh into three aspects: knowledge of the law, mastery of connecting texts to branches, and action. If all three are present, he is an absolute faqih; otherwise, he is a faqih in one sense. This caused confusion among researchers. The author of al-Kashf clarified that the word 'fiqh' is used as a 'restricted truth' (haqiqah qasirah), so one can be a faqih in some respects but not others. Alternatively, fiqh could be the common element between the composite of knowledge and action; thus knowledge alone, when action is absent, yields an imperfect faqih. This resolves the problem of the sinful faqih. Finally, the author transitions to the 'title definition' (hadduhu laqaban), defining fiqh as 'knowledge of principles' (qawa'id), i.e., universal propositions (qadaya kulliyyah) by which the particular rulings of fiqh are known. This is the formal definition of fiqh as a science.

Key Terms
المعنى الإضافي (al-ma'na al-idafi): The supplemental meaning of fiqh, i.e., the definition relative to other sciences; the author just finished discussing that.
المعنى اللقبي (al-ma'na al-laqabi): The titular meaning, i.e., the definition of fiqh in itself as a science: knowledge of universal principles (al-qawa'id).
قواعد (qawa'id): Universal principles or maxims; here, the subject matter of fiqh as a science, i.e., the general legal propositions from which specific rulings are derived.
فقيه مطلق (faqih mutlaq): A complete faqih who possesses knowledge, mastery of derivation, and acts upon it.
حقيقة قاصرة (haqiqah qasirah): A restricted truth; a word used in a sense that applies partly or incompletely, so something can be called 'fiqh' in one respect but not fully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the 'title definition' (al-hadd al-laqabi) define fiqh?
A) As the composite of knowledge and action.
B) As the knowledge of universal principles from which the states of particular rulings are known.
C) As the faculty of deduction (malakat al-istinbat).
D) As acting upon one's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh as a science is defined as knowledge of universal principles; action is not part of its technical definition but is required for the praise-worthy fiqh mentioned in the Shari'a.

Definition of Fiqh: Certainty, Zann, and Action
Debate on the proper definition of fiqh and inclusion of action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why the Hanafi usul school defines praiseworthy fiqh as certain knowledge requiring action, and distinguish it from alternative definitions.

Core Idea
Praiseworthy fiqh is certain knowledge of rulings with the faculty of istinbat, and action is required for full praise.

This passage delves into two key debates in Hanafi usul: whether fiqh includes zanni rulings, and whether action is part of its definition. By examining the arguments, we understand why the Hanafi school insists on certainty and often action for the praised fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
The first objection is that 'ilm includes zann, so limiting fiqh to qat'i contradicts common usage. The second is that many rulings are based on zanni evidences like qiyas, which would be excluded. Both are raised against Sadr al-Shari'ah's definition.
2
Analyze Sadr al-Shari'ah's response
He chooses a third possibility: the intended fiqh is the specific part of rulings that are certain, so no contradiction. For the second, he argues that zanni rulings are not praiseworthy, and the praise of fuqaha indicates that fiqh is certain knowledge.
3
Examine the debate on including action
Imam Fakhr al-Islam holds that action is part of the definition, but the author critiques this because it would exclude sinful faqihs. However, the passage clarifies that the real issue is the praised fiqh, and the truth lies with Fakhr al-Islam because praise requires action.
4
Synthesize the final Hanafi position
The passage reconciles by allowing a deficient use of fiqh for knowledge alone, but the complete praiseworthy fiqh requires certainty, istinbat, and action.

أن تعريف الفقه، فلا إيراد، ثم أن دعوى كون العلم حقيقة فيما يتناول الظن أيضاً لا يخلو عن لأنه مخالف لكتب اللغة والأحرى أن يقول : مستعمل فيه استعمالاً شائعاً، فلا بأس بإرادة هذا المعنى (وبعضهم) وهو مشايخنا) وهو الإمام فخر الإسلام رحمه الله تعالى (بعيد جداً عن الصواب لأن الفقه أحد أنواع العلم المدوّنة، وأيضاً يلزم أن لا يكون الفقيه الفاسق فقيهاً. هذا واعلم أنه ليس الكلام في أن الفقه في الاصطلاح الفاسق، فلا بد من اعتبار العمل ولا شناعة في التزام كون الفاسق العارف بالأحكام بالأدلة غير فقيه، كيف ولم يعد أحد الحجاج فقيهاً مع كونه عارفاً للأحكام هذا واعلم أنه رضي الله تعالى عنه قال : والنوع الثاني علم الفروع، وهو وأنه ثلاثة أقسام علم المشروع بنفسه، والقسم الثاني : إتقان المعرفة به، وهو معرفة النصوص بمعانيها وضبط الأصول بفروعها والقسم الثالث: هو العمل به، حتى لا يصير نفس العلم مقصوداً، فإذا تمت هذه الأوجه كان فقيهاً مطلقاً، وإلا فهو فقيه من وجه دون وجه، فتحير المحصلون في فهمه، فإن أول كلامه يدل على أن الفقه المجموع، وآخره يدل على أن العلم فقط أيضاً فقه بل العمل وحده وحرر صاحب الكشف سبيل اليقين أو أعم فقه مطلق أي فرد كامل وإلا أي وإن لم يكن مقارناً له، بل يكون علماً فقط، ولم يكن العالم عاملاً به فهو فقه من وجه دون وجه أي فرد ناقص، وحينئذ لا يرد خروج فقه الفاسق الفقيه من الفقه، ولم يحتج إلى الالتزام المذكور ومعرفة أمثال الحجاج الأحكام من الأدلة غير ظاهر، فلا يقوم دليلاً، ولا بعد في ممدوحية الفاسق من جهة العلم، فإنه مدح من وجه فتأمل
That the definition of fiqh, so no objection. Then the claim that knowledge is a reality that includes zann as well is not free from contradiction because it contradicts language books. He should have said: it is used in a common usage, so no harm in intending this meaning. (And some) i.e. Imam Sadr al-Shari'ah (made fiqh an expression for certain rulings along with the faculty of istinbat. On this basis both objections are repelled. As for the first: we choose a third possibility: that the intended meaning is the specific part which is certain rulings, so no objection. As for the second: because fiqh then is certain knowledge and it necessitates the exclusion of matters established by zanni evidences like qiyas and khabar wahid, and they are many. Do you not see that mutawatir sunnah is very rare and likewise ijma', so certain rulings are the least of the little. If you say he is committed to their exclusion, he said: (That commitment is a commitment without proof. Perhaps his proof is that zann is blameworthy from the Lawgiver, no perfection in it, at least it is not worthy of praise. It is only considered out of necessity for action. Since praise of fuqaha is established from the Lawgiver and Companions, it is known that fiqh is certain knowledge. Moreover, since they did not call anyone a faqih except one who has the faculty of istinbat, it is known that its accompaniment is also considered in the praised fiqh. And the discussion about it: (And making action part of the definition of this knowledge i.e. fiqh as held by some of our teachers) i.e. Imam Fakhr al-Islam may Allah have mercy on him (is very far from correct because fiqh is one of the codified sciences, and also it would entail that a sinful faqih is not a faqih. Know that the discussion is not about what fiqh means in terminology, for that is of little benefit, and every terminologist can define as he wishes, so no dispute. Rather the discussion is about the fiqh that is praised in the speech of the Lawgiver, Companions, and Followers. In that case, the truth lies with Imam Fakhr al-Islam, because the sinful person does not deserve praise, so action must be considered. There is no shame in holding that a sinful person who knows rulings by evidences is not a faqih, because no one counts al-Hajjaj as a faqih despite his knowledge of rulings. Know that he (may Allah be pleased with him) said: The second type is knowledge of branches, and it is three divisions: knowledge of what is legislated in itself, the second: mastery of knowledge of it which is knowledge of texts with their meanings and grasping the principles with their branches, and the third: acting upon it, so that knowledge itself does not become the objective. When these aspects are complete, he is a faqih absolutely; otherwise he is a faqih in one aspect and not in another. The verifiers were confused in understanding him, for his first statement indicates that fiqh is the composite, and his last indicates that knowledge alone is also fiqh, rather action alone. The author of al-Kashf clarified that fiqh is the composite, action, and knowledge, each part belonging to it, so fiqh as used here is a deficient reality, so it is fiqh in one aspect and not in another. It is possible that his intent is that fiqh is an expression for the common factor between the composite and knowledge, so knowledge accompanied by action in a certain manner or more general is absolute fiqh i.e. a complete individual; otherwise i.e. if it is not accompanied by it but is knowledge only and the person does not act, then it is fiqh in one aspect and not in another i.e. a deficient individual. On this basis, the exclusion of the fiqh of the sinful faqih from fiqh does not arise, and there is no need for the aforementioned commitment. The knowledge of someone like al-Hajjaj of rulings from evidences is not apparent, so it does not stand as a proof. And there is no difficulty in the praiseworthiness of the sinful person from the perspective of knowledge, for he is praised in one aspect. So reflect.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed discussion on the definition of fiqh. It begins by addressing two objections to the definition of fiqh as certain knowledge (qati) with the faculty of istinbat. The first objection is that the word 'ilm includes zann, but the author responds by choosing a third possibility: the intended meaning is certain rulings only. The second objection is that limiting fiqh to certain rulings excludes many issues established by zanni evidences like qiyas and khabar wahid. The response is that such rulings are not worthy of praise, and the praise of fuqaha by the Lawgiver and Companions indicates that fiqh is certain knowledge. Then the passage discusses whether action (amal) is part of fiqh. Imam Fakhr al-Islam holds that it is, but the author notes that this would exclude sinful faqihs. However, the author clarifies that the real question is about the fiqh praised in shari'ah, not the technical term. He leans toward the position that action is necessary for praiseworthy fiqh. The passage then cites a statement from an authority that divides the science into three elements: knowledge of the law, mastery of its evidence, and action. If all three are present, one is a faqih absolutely; otherwise only partially. The author reconciles conflicting statements by saying that fiqh can be used in a deficient sense. The final conclusion is that a sinful person can be praised for knowledge alone, but not as a complete faqih. This nuanced discussion reflects the Hanafi concern with integrating action into the definition of fiqh without excluding the knowledgeable but sinful from all praiseworthiness.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of certain practical legal rulings derived from detailed evidences, with the faculty of istinbat; praised fiqh also requires action
الظن (al-zann): Probable knowledge, opposed to certainty; in this passage it is considered blameworthy and not worthy of praise in matters of fiqh
القطعية (al-qat'iyya): Certain rulings established by mutawatir or definitive evidences

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the debate in this passage, why does Imam Sadr al-Shari'ah define fiqh as 'certain rulings along with the faculty of istinbat'?
A) Because it excludes zanni rulings which are blameworthy and not praised by the Lawgiver.
B) Because it includes all types of rulings including zanni ones.
C) Because it excludes the requirement of action.
D) Because it makes fiqh a composite of knowledge and action.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view on praiseworthy fiqh requires certainty (qat'i) in rulings, the faculty of istinbat, and for full praise, action upon that knowledge.

Definition of 'Ilm: Rusm and Composition from Non-Predicable Parts
Explains definition of 'ilm as composite of non-predicable parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a composite of non-predicable parts cannot have a true definition (hadd) and must be described by rusm, and analyze the objection regarding the dual cognition of masā'il.

Core Idea
A body of knowledge ('ilm) has no genus or species because its parts are non-predicable – only a description (rasm) is possible.

This passage from the sharh of Musallam al-Thubut examines the logical structure of a science ('ilm). It argues that since the masā'il (topics) that compose a science are non-predicable parts—like the units of the number ten—the science as a whole has no genus (jins) or species (fasl). Consequently, it cannot be defined in a strict sense (hadd) but only described (rasm). The author then engages with objections concerning the use of 'ilm as a premise in its own definition and the relationship between conceptual (taṣawwur) and assertoric (taṣdīq) cognition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the composition type
Identify that 'ilm is a composite of masā'il, which are non-predicable parts. Like the units of the number ten, these parts are not predicated on the whole. Therefore, the composite has no genus or species.
Non-predicable means the parts cannot be used as genus or differentia for the whole.
2
Contrast hadd and rusm
True definitions (hadd) require predicable parts: a genus (jins) and a differentia (fasl). Since 'ilm lacks these, it cannot be defined by hadd; only a rusm (description) is possible, which refers to the whole through its non-predicable parts.
Rusm is a looser form of definition accepted in Hanafi usul for composites of non-predicable parts.
3
Trace the objection
Some scholars object that 'ilm could serve as a premise because its parts (masā'il) have two types of cognition: taṣawwur (conceptual) and taṣdīq (assertoric). As taṣawwur, it could be a premise; as taṣdīq, it could be the goal. The author responds that this objection depends on the earlier premise about composition from non-predicable parts.
The objection attempts to avoid circularity by splitting 'ilm into two aspects.
4
Analyze the deeper problem
Even if the objection were accepted, a deeper issue arises: if taṣawwur and taṣdīq both attach to the same object (masā'il), then they would be unified in reality, contradicting their distinct types. The author notes that this problem is only resolvable by denying the identity of knowledge and known—a metaphysical issue beyond this text.
This shows that the objection does not rescue the possibility of a true definition; it only leads to further complications.
5
Apply to usul al-fiqh
Recognize that the definitions of Islamic sciences (like usul al-fiqh itself) are rusūm, not ḥudūd. This affects how we use definitions in proofs: they are descriptive, not constitutive of the essence.
In Hanafi usul, the definition of usul al-fiqh is a rusm: 'knowledge of the principles of ijtihad and its rules'.

فالمفهومات الكلية التي تذكر في المقدمات لأجل البصيرة رسوم لا حدود بناء على أن المركب من أجزاء غير محمولة كالعشرة لا جنس له ولا فصل وإلا لزم تعدد الذاتي بل تعدد حقيقة المركب، وفي المشهور أنه لا يلتئم الحد إلا من الأجزاء المحمولة، وبعض المحققين قرر الكلام بأن حد العلم لا يصلح أن يكون مقدمة لأن حده عبارة عن العلم بالمسائل، فلو كان مقدمة لزم خروجه ودخوله وتوقف الشيء على نفسه، وهذا أيضاً موقوف على عدم كونه مركباً من الجنس والفصل، ثم لقائل أن يقول: للمسائل إدراكان تصوري وتصديقي فإن التصور يتعلق بكل شيء وتصديقي فيجوز أن يكون باعتبار العلم التصوري مقدمة وموقوفاً عليه، وباعتبار العلم التصديقي مقصوداً متوقفاً فلا إشكال وفيه في هذا المبنى عليه نظر أشرت إليه في السلم من أن الأجزاء المحمولة مغايرة بالاعتبار لغير المحمولة، فلا تعدد في الحقيقة وتفصيله فيه واعلم أن هذا المبنى عليه وإن كان فاسداً، لكن العلوم لكونها حقائق اعتبارية لا تركيب فيها إلا من المسائل الغير المحمولة، وليس لها جنس ولا فصل بالضرورة الوجدانية، ثم المفهومات المذكورة في المقدمة ليست مأخوذة من المسائل بأن تكون إذا أخذت لا بشرط بشرط شيء كانت عين تلك المفهومات بالضرورة الوجدانية الغير المكذوبة (نعم: يلزم على هذا التقدير (اتحاد) التصور والتصديق حقيقة لأن العلم بالحد علم تصوري، والإذعان بها تصديقي، وقد تعلقا بشيء واحد وهو المسائل (مع أنهما نوعان متباينان (تحقيقاً) عندهم (فتفكر) اعلم أن هذا الإيراد لم ينشأ من هذا بل وارد على كل تقدير مبناه أن التصور يتعلق بكل شيء فيتعلق بما يتعلق به التصديق والعلم والمعلوم متحدان بالذات، فيلزم الاستحالة قطعاً ولا يمكن الجواب عن هذا إلا بعد إنكار الاتحاد بين العلم والمعلوم، وليس كشف أمثال هذه الإشكالات
So the universal concepts mentioned in the premises for insight are definitions (rusūm), not true definitions (ḥudūd), based on the premise that a compound of non-predicable parts, like ten, has no genus and no species. Otherwise, there would be multiplicity of the essential (dhātī) and even multiplicity of the reality of the compound. According to the well-known view, a true definition is only formed from predicable parts. Some verifying scholars established that the definition of a science cannot be a premise because its definition is knowledge of the masā’il; if it were a premise, it would entail its exclusion and inclusion, and the thing depending on itself. This also depends on it not being composed of genus and species. Then it may be said: the masā’il have two cognitions: conceptual (taṣawwur) and assertoric (taṣdīq). Conceptual cognition relates to everything, and assertoric [relates to judgments]. So it is possible that, in terms of conceptual science, it is a premise and a condition, and in terms of assertoric science, it is an objective and dependent, so no problem. There is in this premise a consideration I pointed out in [my commentary on] al-Sullam: that predicable parts are different in consideration from non-predicable parts, so there is no multiplicity in reality. Its details are there. Know that this premise, though unsound, [still stands because] sciences, being mental constructs, have no composition except from non-predicable parts, and necessarily have no genus or species. Then the concepts mentioned in the premise are not taken from the masā’il such that if taken without condition or with a condition they would be identical to those concepts necessarily. Yes, according to this estimation there is unification of conceptual and assertoric cognition in reality, because knowledge of the definition is conceptual, and assent to it is assertoric, and they have attached to one thing—the masā’il—even though they are two distinct types according to them. Reflect. Know that this objection did not arise from this; rather it applies to every estimation. Its basis is that conceptual cognition relates to everything, so it relates to what assertoric relates to, and knowledge and known are essentially one, so impossibility necessarily follows. This cannot be answered except after denying the Unity of knowledge and known. This is not an exposure of such difficulties.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Musallam al-Thubut defines 'ilm (a body of knowledge) as a composite of non-predicable parts—the masā'il (topics). Since these parts are not predicated on each other (like the units of the number ten, which are not predicated on the whole), the composite has no genus or species. True definitions (ḥudūd) require predicable parts (jins and fasl). Thus, 'ilm cannot be strictly defined; only a rusm (description) is possible. The author then addresses a possible objection: maybe 'ilm can be a premise because the masā'il have both conceptual cognition (taṣawwur) and assertoric cognition (taṣdīq); as taṣawwur it could be a premise. He responds that this depends on the earlier premise about composition. He also counters a deeper objection that unifying taṣawwur and taṣdīq on one object leads to impossibility, but notes that this is only resolved by denying the identity of knowledge and known—a metaphysical issue beyond this text.

Key Terms
الرسوم (al-rusūm): Descriptive definitions lacking genus and species, suitable for composites of non-predicable parts.
التصور والتصديق (al-taṣawwur wa al-taṣdīq): Two types of cognition: taṣawwur is conceptual (grasping meaning), taṣdīq is assertoric (affirming truth).
الحد (al-ḥadd): A true definition composed of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl), applicable only to composites of predicable parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that 'ilm (a science) has no genus and no species?
A) Because it is a simple concept without parts.
B) Because it is composed of non-predicable parts (masā'il).
C) Because it is a proper noun ('alam).
D) Because it is a mental construct without reality.

Key Takeaway
A composite of non-predicable parts cannot have a true definition; it can only be described (rasm).
