Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
Rational Impossibility of Lying and the Maturidi Position on Divine Attributes
Usul al-fiqh dispute: second argument on lying and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Mu'tazila's second argument (that without rational good/evil, lying becomes possible for Allah) and state the Hanafi response that lying is a deficiency rationally impossible for Allah, and that punishing the obedient is also impossible.

Core Idea
Lying is a deficiency that is rationally impossible for Allah, regardless of one's theory of good and evil.

The Mu'tazila present a second, more fundamental argument: if rulings are not rational, then there is no rational basis to declare lying as ugly, so it would be possible for Allah to lie, thereby destroying the proof of prophethood. The Hanafis respond by asserting that lying is a deficiency that is rationally impossible for Allah by consensus of rational people. They further affirm the Maturidi position that punishing the obedient (i.e., injustice) is also impossible for Allah, against the Ash'ari view that it is possible but prevented by His promise.

Guided Path
1
State the Mu'tazila's second argument
They claim that if good/evil are not rational, then lying is not rationally impossible for Allah. If lying is possible, false miracles could occur, undermining prophethood.
This is a direct challenge to the epistemology of prophethood.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafis deny the implication. They argue that lying is a deficiency (naqs), and deficiency is rationally impossible for Allah by universal agreement of rational people, regardless of the theory of good and evil.
This response is based on the necessity of divine perfection.
3
Examine the objection from al-Mawaqif
The objection says that deficiency in actions depends on rational ugliness which is disputed. The Hanafis reply that what contradicts essential necessity—like lying—is a deficiency that is rationally impossible, not dependent on contested ethical theory.
This distinction is crucial.
4
Connect to the Maturidi position on punishment
The text adds that punishing the obedient (an innocent person) is also a deficiency impossible for Allah. This is the Maturidi view, contrasting with the Ash'ari view that it is possible but prevented by divine promise.
This shows the systematic nature of the Maturidi position on divine attributes.
5
Conclude the significance
The Hanafi/Maturidi position on rational impossibility of certain acts provides a firm foundation for prophethood without relying on contested ethical theories.
This settles the debate in favor of the Ahl al-Sunnah view.

فخصصوا الآية بعذاب الدنيا بدلالة السياق وهو قوله عزّ من قائل: ﴿وَإِذَا أَرَدْنَا أَن تُهْلِكَ قَرْيَةٌ أَمَرْنَا مُتْرَفِيهَا فَفَسَقُوا فِيهَا فَحَقَّ عَلَيْهَا الْقَوْلُ فَدَمَرْنَهَا تَدْمِيرًا﴾ [الإسراء : ١٦] ولعل هلاك القرى وتخريبها مسبب عن وقوع العباد المصطفين في الغم ودعائهم به عليها، وهو مسبب عن فسقهم، ولهذا يتأخر هلاك القرى عن الفسق إلى زمان إرسال الرسل، وليس شأن كل قبيح تسبيبه إلى هلاك القرى حتى يقال: ما الفرق بين عذاب الدنيا وعذاب الآخرة؟ حتى جوزوا الثاني بذنوب قبل البعثة دون الأول وأولوا أيضاً الرسول بالعقل، فإنه رسول باطن في تنبيه القلب، فالمعنى إذا والله أعلم وليس شأننا التعذيب من غير إعطاء العقل الذي به تنبيه الإنسان إلى غير ذلك من التأويلات، وها هنا جواب آخر هو أنه ليس زمان لم يبعث فيه نبي أصلاً البعثة مطلقاً، في الواقع، بل لم يترك الإنسان سدى، فتقدير وجود زمان خال ووقع الأعمال القبيحة تقدير محال فبعد فرضه يلتزم صحة العقاب، فمعنى الآية الكريمة والله أعلم: وليس شأننا العقاب من دون البعثة، فإنها لازمة لوجود الإنسان من لدن آدم إلى القيامة، ولم يخل زمان عنها فافهم المعتزلة قالوا: أولاً: لو كان الحكم شرعياً لزم إفحام الرسل عند أمرهم المكلف بالنظر في المعجزات ليعلم أنهم رسل فيقول هذا المكلف لا أنظر في معجزتك ما لم يجب النظر عليَّ لأن للإنسان أن يكف عما ليس واجباً عليه ولا يجب عليَّ ما لم يصدر عن لسان نبي ولا نبوة إلا بالمعجزة ولا تعلم المعجزة إلا بالنظر، فحينئذ لا يجب النظر ما لم أنظر فلزم إفحام الرسل، أي إسكاتهم، وهو محال، لأنه حينئذ يفوت الغرض من الرسالة، فإن قيل: يلزم عليهم هذا قالوا: ولا يلزم علينا لأن وجوب النظر عندنا من القضايا الفطرية القياس فإنكارهم وجوب النظر من المكابرة، فلا إفحام وفيه ما فيه لأن وجوب النظر موقوف على إفادته للعلم مطلقاً، وفي الإلهيات خاصة، وفيه خلاف الرياضيين، وعلى أن معرفة الله واجبة، وفيه خلاف الحشوية وأن المعرفة لا تتم إلا بالنظر، ونسب الإباء عنه إلى المتصوفة، وهو غير ثابت عنهم، بل هم مصرحون بخلافه، إلا عن الملاحدة المدعين للتصوف؛ وعلى أن مقدمة الواجب واجبة وسيجيء الخلاف فيه ولا تثبت هذه المقدمات إلا بنظر أدق، والموقوف على ما لا يثبت إلا بالنظر الدقيق كيف يكون فطرياً، كذا قيل، وفيه: أن هذه مؤاخذة لفظية، فإن لهم أن يقولوا إن وجوب النظر وإن كان نظرياً لكن لا يتوقف على الشرع، فيمكن أن يقول الرسول: إن هذا واجب عليك مع قطع النظر عن إخباري فامتثل، فلا يتمشى منه لا يجب النظر ما لم أنظر لأنه حينئذ له أن يقول: لا أنظر، فإنه غير واجب، فإذا قال الرسول: النظر واجب يقول المكلف هذا نظري لا يدرك إلا بالنظر، فليجز عدم صدقه وإني لا أضيع وقتي فيما لا أعلم وجوبه فحينئذ الإفحام لازم قطعاً، فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا، فإن قلت للرسول أن يقول: أدعي قضية إن كنت صادقاً فيها يضرك الإباء فاسمع، وليس للعاقل الإباء عن سماع مثلها فلا إفحام، قلت: هذا ينقلب على أصل الدليل، فإنه لو كان الوجوب بالشرع كان له أن يقول: إني أدعي قضية إن كنت صادقاً فيها لزم الإحكام بقولي فاسمع، فتدبر فإنه سانح عزيز: والجواب: أنا لا نسلم أن الوجوب بالشرع يتوقف على النظر فإنه أي الوجوب بالشرع نظر أو لم ينظر فحينئذ لا قوله: لا يجب النظر ما لم أنظر، يصح بل للرسول أن يقول: قد وجب عليك النظر بقولي تنظر أولاً، فإن قلت: على هذا الجواب يلزم تكليف الغافل، فإنه غافل عن الرسالة قال وليس ذلك أي التكليف بالنظر قبل العلم بالرسالة من تكليف الغافل فإنه أي المدعو إلى النظر يفهم الخطاب والغافل الذي يمتنع تكليفه هو الذي لا يفهم الخطاب كالنائم والمجنون بالجنون المطبق، فافهم أقول في دفع الجواب لو قال المكلف حين قال له الرسول انظر لا أمتثل ما لم أعلم وجوب الامتثال، إذ له أن يمتنع عما لم يعلم بوجوبه ولا يضيع وقته فيه ولا أعلم الوجوب ما لم أمتثل أمرك بالنظر لكان المكلف بمحل من المساغ فيلزم الإفحام والجواب عنه: أن للرسول أن يقول: إن الحسن والقبح في الأشياء ثابت وأنا أعلم المضرة في بعض الأشياء، فاصغ إليَّ، إذ ليس شأن العاقل أن لا يصغي إلى ناصح مدع للمضرات الشديدة بل شأنه أن يفحص حقيقة الحال، فإن ظهر الصدق فيطيع، وإن ظهر الكذب فلا يطيع هذا كما هو جواب على تقدير عقلية الحسن والقبح، كذلك يتأتى على تقدير كونهما شرعيين فإن للرسول أن يقول: القول قولي، وإني أثبت في بعض الأشياء ضرراً لا خلاص لك عنه، وليس شأن العاقل أن لا يفحص عن صدقه كما قرر، فما وقع عن واقف الأسرار أبي قدس سره أنه لا يتم عن الأشعرية لم يصل إلى فهمه ذهن هذا العبد قال المصنف: والحق في الجواب أن إراءة المعجزات واجبة على الله تعالى لطفاً بعباده عقلاً عند المعتزلة، فإنهم قالوا بالوجوب العقلي أو واجب عادة عندنا، فإن الله تعالى كريم جرت عادته بإراءة المعجزات وإذا كانت الإراءة واجبة عقلاً أو عادة فيرى المكلف المعجزة بالضرورة عند إراءة الرسول ويقع العلم بنبوته، ولا تتأتى هذه الأسئلة والأجوبة وهو متم نوره ولو كره الكافرون، والمعتزلة قالوا ثانياً: أنه لولاه أي كون الحكم عقلياً لم يمتنع الكذب منه تعالى عقلاً، إذ لا حكم للعقل بقبح، وإذا جاز الكذب عليه فلا يمتنع إظهار المعجزة على يد الكاذب ولو اكتفی به لکفی فینسد باب النبوة وهو مفتوح والجواب أنه أي المذكور نقص فيجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه، كيف وقد مرَّ أنه لا نزاع فيه فإنه عقلي باتفاق العقلاء فالملازمة ممنوعة وما في المواقف في إثبات الملازمة أن النقص في الأفعال يرجع إلى القبح العقلي المتنازع فيه، ولا يليق تفسيره باستحقاق العقاب، فإنه لا ثواب ولا عقاب على الباري، بل بما به يستحق أن يذم لكن هذا الاستحقاق في أفعال العباد يكون باستحقاق العقاب فشرعية القبح توجب شرعية النقص، فحينئذ جاز عقلاً الكذب، وفيه الفساد فممنوع، لأن ما ينافي الوجوب الذاتي كيفاً كان أو فعلاً من جملة النقص في حق الباري، ومن الاستحالات العقلية عليه سبحانه ولهذا أي لكونه من الاستحالات العقلية أثبته الحكماء أي أثبت كونه نقصاً مستحيلاً اتصافه تعالی به مع كونهم لا يسندون أقوالهم إلى نبي من الأنبياء، فلا لزوم بين النقص والقبح لكن يلزم على الأشاعرة التابعين للشيخ الأشعري امتناع تعذيب الطائع الله تعالى في الأعمال كما هو مذهبنا معشر الماتريدية ومذهب المعتزلة فإنه أي تعذيب الطائع نقص يستحيل عليه
They specified the verse [to refer to] punishment of this world by evidence of context, which is His statement: 'And when We intend to destroy a city, We command its affluent ones, and they transgress therein, so the word becomes justified against it, and We destroy it completely' (Quran 17:16). Perhaps the destruction of cities and their ruin is caused by the occurrence of chosen servants in grief and their supplication against them, and it is caused by their transgression; thus the destruction of cities is delayed after transgression until the time of sending messengers. And it is not the case that every ugly act results in destruction of cities such that one could ask: what is the difference between punishment of this world and the Hereafter? So they allowed the latter for sins before the mission without the former, and they also interpreted 'the messenger' as reason, for it is an inner messenger in awakening the heart. So the meaning—and God knows best—is 'It is not Our way to punish without giving reason which awakens man', among other interpretations. And here is another answer: that there is no time in which no prophet was sent at all in reality; rather, man was not left neglected. So assuming a time devoid [of prophets] with occurrence of evil deeds is an impossible assumption; after hypothesizing it, the validity of punishment is conceded. So the meaning of the noble verse—God knows best—is: 'It is not Our way to punish without the mission, for it [mission] is necessary for human existence from Adam until the Resurrection, and no time is free from it.' So understand. The Mu'tazila said: First: If ruling were Shar'i, it would necessitate silencing the messengers when they command the obligated person to consider miracles to know that they are messengers. This obligated person says: 'I will not consider your miracle unless consideration is obligatory upon me, because a person may abstain from what is not obligatory upon him. And what is not issued from a prophet's tongue is not obligatory on me; prophethood is only established by miracles, and miracles are only known by consideration. So consideration is not obligatory unless I already consider, thereby necessitating the silencing of messengers—i.e., rendering them speechless—which is impossible, because then the purpose of messengership is lost.' If it is said: This [problem] applies to them too? They said: 'It does not apply to us because the obligation of consideration is for us among the innate axiomatic propositions, so their denial of the obligation of consideration is obstinacy, hence no silencing.' In this there is much to discuss, because the obligation of consideration depends on its yielding knowledge absolutely, and especially in divine matters; and there is disagreement among mathematicians, and on the point that knowledge of God is obligatory—there is disagreement among the Hashwiyya, and that knowledge is not complete except through consideration—a stance attributed to Sufis but not confirmed from them; rather they explicitly contradict it except for the heretics claiming Sufism; and that the introduction to an obligation is obligatory—disagreement will come on this. These premises are only established by subtle consideration. How can something that depends on what is only established by subtle consideration be innate? So it is said. In it: this is a verbal objection, because they can say that the obligation of consideration, even if theoretical, does not depend on revelation; so the messenger could say: 'This is obligatory upon you regardless of my information, so comply.' Then his claim 'I will not consider what I have not considered' does not hold, because then he could say 'I will not consider, for it is not obligatory.' When the messenger says 'consideration is obligatory', the obligated person says 'this is theoretical, only known by consideration; it is possible it is false, and I will not waste my time on something whose obligation I do not know.' Then silencing is absolutely necessary. So whatever your answer is, it is our answer. If you say: The messenger could say: 'I claim a proposition—if you are truthful in it, abstention harms you, so listen; and it is not for a rational person to abstain from hearing such, so no silencing.' I say: This turns against the original proof, because if obligation were by revelation, he would have said: 'I claim a proposition—if you are truthful in it, confirmation is required by my word, so listen.' Reflect, for it is a precious occurrence. The answer: We do not concede that obligation by revelation depends on consideration; for obligation by revelation applies whether one considers or not. So then his statement 'consideration is not obligatory unless I consider' is not valid. Rather, the messenger can say: 'Consideration has become obligatory upon you by my word; consider first.' If you say: This answer entails burdening the heedless, for he is heedless of the mission. He said: That—i.e., burdening with consideration before knowledge of the mission—is not burdening the heedless, because the one invited to consideration understands the address; the heedless who cannot be burdened is one who does not understand the address, like a sleeper or a continuously insane person. So understand. I say in refuting the answer: If the obligated person, when the messenger says 'consider', says 'I will not comply until I know the obligation of compliance, for he may abstain from what he does not know to be obligatory and not waste his time on it; and I do not know the obligation until I comply with your command to consider'—then the obligated person is at liberty, and silencing results. The answer to this: The messenger can say: 'Goodness and badness in things are established, and I know harm in some things, so listen to me; for it is not the way of a rational person to not listen to a sincere adviser claiming severe harms; rather he should investigate the reality of the situation; if truth appears, he obeys; if falsehood appears, he does not obey.' This, just as it is an answer assuming the rationality of good and evil, similarly applies assuming they are Shar'i; for the messenger can say: 'The word is my word, and I affirm harm in some things from which you cannot escape; and it is not the way of a rational person to not investigate his truthfulness as established.' So what occurred about Waqif al-Asrar (Abu Quddis) that it does not stand regarding the Ash'ariyya did not reach the understanding of this servant's mind. The author said: The correct answer is that showing miracles is obligatory upon God as a grace to His servants rationally according to the Mu'tazila, for they held rational obligation; or it is obligatory by custom according to us, for God the Generous's custom is to show miracles. When showing is obligatory rationally or customarily, the obligated person sees the miracle necessarily at the messenger's showing and gains knowledge of prophethood, and these questions and answers do not arise. The Mu'tazila said second: If it were not so—i.e., ruling being rational—then lying from Him would not be impossible rationally, since the mind has no judgment of ugliness. If lying upon Him is possible, then showing a miracle at the hand of a liar is not impossible, and if that suffices, it would suffice; thus the door of prophethood would be closed, while it is open. The answer: That mentioned (lying) is a deficiency, so it is necessary to exalt Him above it; how can it be otherwise, when it has passed that there is no dispute about it—it is rationally [impossible] by consensus of rational people? So the implication is rejected. And what is in al-Mawaqif in establishing the implication: That deficiency in actions returns to rational ugliness which is disputed, and it is not fitting to interpret it with deserving punishment, for there is no reward or punishment on the Creator; rather, it is that by which He deserves blame. But this deserving in the actions of servants is by deserving punishment; so the Shar'i nature of ugliness necessitates the Shar'i nature of deficiency; then lying becomes rationally possible, and corruption results. So it is rejected, because what contradicts essential necessity—whether in quality or action—is part of deficiency regarding the Creator, and is among rational impossibilities upon Him. For this reason—i.e., being among rational impossibilities—the philosophers affirmed it, i.e., they affirmed it as a deficiency impossible for Him to be described with, despite their not attributing their statements to any prophet. So there is no necessary connection between deficiency and ugliness. But it applies to the Ash'ariyya (followers of Shaykh al-Ash'ari) the impossibility of punishing the obedient [person]—as is our school, the Maturidiyya, and the school of the Mu'tazila—for punishing the obedient is a deficiency impossible upon Him.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Mu'tazila and the Ahl al-Sunnah (specifically the Maturidi/Hanafi school) on whether rulings (ahkam) are known by reason independently of revelation or are purely Shar'i. The Mu'tazila present two main arguments: (1) the 'ifham' (silencing) argument — if rulings are only known through revelation, then a person approached by a prophet cannot be obliged to examine miracles because that obligation itself would require prior revelation, leading to an infinite regress and the prophet's silencing; (2) the impossibility of lying — if rulings are not rational, then lying by Allah is not rationally impossible, so miracles could be performed at the hands of false prophets, destroying the proof of prophethood. The Hanafi/Maturidi response: (1) Showing miracles is obligatory upon Allah as grace (lutf), either rationally (Mu'tazila) or by custom (Ahl al-Sunnah); thus the obligated person necessarily perceives the miracle and knows the prophet's truth without prior obligation. (2) Lying is rationally impossible for Allah because it entails deficiency, which is universally recognized as impossible regardless of one's theory of good and evil. The passage also affirms the Maturidi position that punishing the obedient is impossible for Allah, contrasting with the Ash'ari view that it is rationally possible but prevented by divine promise.

Key Terms
الإفحام (al-ifham): Rendering the prophet speechless — a situation where the addressee can always refuse to comply because no prior obligation exists without revelation, creating an infinite regress that prevents the prophet from establishing his mission.
الوجوب بالشرع (al-wujub bi al-shar‘): Obligation that arises solely from revealed texts (Quran and Sunnah), not from independent reason. The Hanafi position that rulings are established by revelation.
اللطف (al-lutf): Grace — an act of Allah that brings the obligated person closer to obedience and away from sin. Here, showing miracles is lutf, obligatory upon Allah rationally (Mu'tazila) or by custom (Hanafis) to establish prophethood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi basis for denying the possibility of lying by Allah?
A) The Quran explicitly says Allah does not lie.
B) Lying is a deficiency, and deficiency is rationally impossible for Allah.
C) The Mu'tazila's argument is logically flawed.
D) Allah's custom prevents lying.

Key Takeaway
Lying is impossible for Allah because it is a deficiency, and deficiency in the Perfect Being is rationally impossible, irrespective of one's theory of good and evil.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
The Obligation of Reflection (Nazar) and the Rebuttal of Taklif al-Ghafil
Defending the obligation of reflection against the objection of obligating the negligent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi argument for the obligation of nazar before knowledge of revelation and evaluate the opponent's objection of taklif al-ghafil.

Core Idea
The call to reflection is understood by the addressee; obligating the truly negligent (unconscious) is not the same as obligating a rational hearer.

In this passage, the author defends the obligation of intellectual reflection (nazar) to verify prophethood before one has explicit knowledge of that obligation. The opponent objects that such an obligation amounts to 'taklif al-ghafil' (obligating the negligent), which is impossible. The Hanafi response hinges on a precise definition of the 'ghafil': only those who cannot understand the address (e.g., sleeping, insane) are excused. A rational person who hears the call is obliged to investigate, and the messenger can reinforce this by pointing to apparent harm or to miracles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
The opponent claims that obligating reflection (nazar) before one knows that reflection is obligatory is impossible because it obligates a heedless person (ghafil). Locate this objection in the text and paraphrase it in your own words.
The opponent says: 'لا يجب النظر ما لم أنظر' (reflection is not obligatory as long as I do not reflect).
2
Analyze the Hanafi distinction between types of heedlessness
The author argues that the one called to reflection 'يفهم الخطاب' (understands the address). True negligence applies only to those who cannot understand, like the sleeping or insane. Explain why this distinction avoids the charge of taklif al-ghafil.
The text explicitly says: 'والغافل الذي يمتنع تكليفه هو الذي لا يفهم الخطاب كالنائم والمجنون'.
3
Examine the messenger's rational arguments
If the addressee still refuses, the messenger can appeal to known harms (rational or legal) or to the display of miracles. Find the two strategies mentioned and describe how each compels investigation.
The first strategy: 'إني أثبت في بعض الأشياء ضررًا...' (I establish harm in some things). The second: 'إراءة المعجزات' (showing miracles).
4
Understand the broader theological implications
The passage briefly touches on the debate about rational vs. customary obligation of miracles and the nature of divine punishment. Note how these discussions support the main argument that the initial call to reflection is valid.
The author sides with the Maturidi position that miracles are obligatory by custom, not rationally, but still effectively compel knowledge.

المقالة الثانية في الأحكام فإنه) أي الوجوب (بالشرع نظر أو لم ينظر) فحينئذ لا قوله : لا يجب النظر ما لم أنظر، يصح بل للرسول أن يقول : قد وجب عليك النظر بقولي تنظر أولاً، فإن قلت: على هذا الجواب يلزم تكليف الغافل، فإنه غافل عن الرسالة قال وليس ذلك أي التكليف بالنظر قبل العلم بالرسالة (من تكليف الغافل (فإنه أي المدعو إلى النظر ( يفهم الخطاب) والغافل الذي يمتنع تكليفه هو الذي لا يفهم الخطاب كالنائم والمجنون بالجنون المطبق، فافهم (أقول) في دفع الجواب لو قال المكلف حين قال له الرسول انظر (لا) أمتثل ما لم أعلم وجوب الامتثال، إذ له أن يمتنع عما لم يعلم بوجوبه ولا يضيع وقته فيه ولا أعلم الوجوب ما لم أمتثل أمرك بالنظر لكان) المكلف بمحل من المساغ فيلزم الإفحام والجواب عنه : أن للرسول أن يقول : إن الحسن والقبح في الأشياء ،ثابت وأنا أعلم المضرة في بعض الأشياء، فاصغ إليَّ، إذ ليس شأن العاقل أن لا يصغي إلى ناصح مدع للمضرات الشديدة بل شأنه أن يفحص حقيقة من الحال، فإن ظهر الصدق فيطيع، وإن ظهر الكذب فلا يطيع هذا كما هو جواب على تقدير عقلية الحسن والقبح، كذلك يتأتى على تقدير كونهما شرعيين فإن للرسول أن يقول : القول قولي، وإني أثبت في بعض الأشياء ضرراً لا خلاص لك عنه، وليس شأن العاقل أن لا يفحص عن صدقه كما قرر، فما وقع عن واقف الأسرار أبي قدس سره أنه لا يتم عن الأشعرية لم يصل إلى فهمه ذهن هذا العبد قال المصنف : (والحق) في الجواب أن إراءة المعجزات واجبة على الله تعالى لطفاً بعباده عقلاً) عند المعتزلة، فإنهم قالوا بالوجوب العقلي (أو) واجب (عادة) عندنا، فإن الله تعالى ،کریم جرت عادته بإراءة المعجزات وإذا كانت الإراءة واجبة عقلاً أو عادة فيرى المكلف المعجزة بالضرورة عند إراءة الرسول ويقع العلم بنبوته، ولا تتأتى هذه الأسئلة والأجوبة (وهو متم نوره ولو كره الكافرون، و) المعتزلة قالوا (ثانياً : أنه لولاه) أي كون الحكم عقلياً (لم يمتنع الكذب منه (تعالى عقلاً، إذ لا حكم للعقل بقبح، وإذا جاز الكذب عليه فلا يمتنع إظهار المعجزة على يد (الكاذب ولو اكتفی به لکفی فینسد باب النبوة) وهو مفتوح (والجواب أنه أي المذكور (نقص) فيجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه، كيف (وقد مرَّ أنه لا نزاع فيه فإنه عقلي باتفاق العقلاء فالملازمة ممنوعة (وما) في المواقف في إثبات الملازمة أن النقص في الأفعال يرجع إلى القبح العقلي المتنازع فيه، ولا يليق تفسيره باستحقاق العقاب، فإنه لا ثواب ولا عقاب على الباري، بل بما به يستحق أن يذم لكن هذا الاستحقاق في أفعال العباد يكون باستحقاق العقاب فشرعية القبح توجب شرعية النقص، فحينئذ جاز عقلاً الكذب، وفيه الفساد فممنوع، لأن ما ينافي الوجوب الذاتي كيفاً كان أو فعلاً) من جملة النقص في حق الباري، ومن الاستحالات العقلية عليه سبحانه (ولهذا) أي لكونه من الاستحالات العقلية أثبته الحكماء) أي أثبت كونه نقصاً مستحيلاً اتصافه تعالی به مع كونهم لا يسندون أقوالهم إلى نبي من الأنبياء، فلا لزوم بين النقص والقبح (لكن يلزم على الأشاعرة) التابعين للشيخ الأشعري (امتناع تعذيب الطائع) الله تعالى في الأعمال (كما هو مذهبنا معشر الماتريدية ومذهب المعتزلة فإنه أي تعذيب الطائع (نقص يستحيل عليه سبحانه عقلاً، فلا يتأتى هذا الجواب من قبلهم، ثم إنه يرد عليهم أن لا يصح تعذيب العاصي أيضاً، فإنه ما صار عاصياً باختياره بل يجعل الله سبحانه كما هو رأينا، ومعشر أهل السنة والجماعة، وجعل شخص عاصياً ثم التعذيب عليه بأنه لم عصيت نقص، فيستحيل عليه سبحانه، ولا يلزمنا هذا أيضاً، لأنا نقول إن بعض الأفعال من شأنها لحوق العقاب، فتعقب العقاب على العصيان كتعقب الحمى على التخمة ولا نقص فيه لأن إعطاء ملائمات الشيء لا قبح فيه وإن كان مؤلماً، فإن قلت: فلم خلق هذه الأفعال في ذوات العاصين حتى وصل هذا النحو من الألم الشديد؟ قلنا : التحقيق أنه كما أن في الأفعال استحقاق أن يتعقبه الألم أو الراحة، كذلك في الذوات أيضاً استحقاق، لأن يتصف بأفعال فيصرف قدرته إلى العزم فيتم هذا الاستحقاق فيخلق المفيض فيه الفعل فيتصف به، فإن قلت؛ فحينئذ لا يصح العفو لأنه خلاف ما يستحق به الفعل قلت كلا بل البعض يستحق جواز العفو وجواز العقوبة، وكذلك الذوات بعضها يستحق العفو لاستحقاقه اتصاف فعل حسن يتم به استحقاق العفو فيتصف به فیعفی عنه، ولذا لا يعفى الكفر، ولا يجعل الكافر معذوراً بوجه، لأن الكفر يستحق العقوبة فقط، على أن عفو المستحق للألم صفة كمال لا نقص فيها، فلا إيراد، ولا نعني بالاستحقاق أن هناك صفة يعبر عنها بالاستحقاق والاستعداد كما في «عرف الفلاسفة»، بل الاستحقاق صلوحها له، وهذا الصلوح هو الاستعداد وتفصيل أمثال هذه المباحث في شروح نصوص الحكم».
The second article on rulings: The obligation is by law whether one reflects or not. So then his statement 'Reflection is not obligatory as long as I do not reflect' is not valid. Rather the messenger can say: 'Reflection has become obligatory upon you by my statement; reflect first.' If you say: This answer necessitates obligating the negligent, for he is heedless of the message. He said: That (obligating reflection before knowledge of the message) is not from obligating the negligent, for the one called to reflection understands the address. The negligent whose obligation is precluded is the one who does not understand the address, like the sleeping and the insane with continuous insanity, so understand. (I say) In repelling the answer: If the obligated person said when the messenger told him 'reflect': 'I will not comply as long as I do not know the obligation of compliance, for he may refrain from what he does not know its obligation and not waste his time in it, and I do not know the obligation unless I comply with your command to reflect' then the obligated person would have room, leading to silencing (ifham). The answer to that: The messenger can say: 'Good and evil in things are established, and I know harm in some things, so listen to me, for it is not the nature of a rational person not to listen to a sincere adviser claiming severe harms; rather his nature is to investigate the reality of the situation. If truth appears, he obeys; if falsehood appears, he does not obey.' This is an answer on the assumption that good and evil are rational. Similarly, it applies on the assumption that they are legal, for the messenger can say: 'My word is the word, and I establish harm in some things from which you have no escape, and it is not the nature of a rational person not to investigate his truthfulness as established.' So what was said by the knower of secrets, Abu Quddis ([؟] meaning), that it does not hold against the Ash'aris, the mind of this servant did not reach his understanding. The author said: 'The truth' in the answer is that showing miracles is obligatory upon God as a kindness to His servants rationally according to the Mu'tazila (for they affirm rational obligation) or customarily according to us (for God the Generous has the custom of showing miracles). When the showing is obligatory rationally or customarily, the obligated person necessarily sees the miracle at the showing and acquires knowledge of prophethood, and these questions and answers do not arise. (And it is complete light even if the disbelievers hate). And the Mu'tazila said: 'Secondly, without it (i.e., the judgment being rational) lying from Him would not be rationally impossible, for there is no rational judgment of its evil. If lying upon Him is permissible, then showing a miracle at the hand of a liar is not impossible, and if this suffices, it would suffice, and the door of prophethood would be closed while it is open. The answer: That (i.e., what was mentioned) is a deficiency, so it is necessary to declare Him exalted above it. How (since it has passed that there is no dispute about it, for it is rational by consensus of rational people, so the necessary connection is denied). And what is in al-Mawaqif in establishing the necessary connection: deficiency in actions is traceable to rational evil which is disputed, and it is not appropriate to interpret it as deserving punishment, for there is no reward or punishment upon the Divine, but rather by what deserves blame. But this deserving in the actions of servants is by deserving punishment, so the legality of evil necessitates the legality of deficiency. Then lying would be rationally permissible, and corruption is in it. It is denied, because what contradicts essential necessity, whether in quality or act, is part of deficiency in the right of the Divine, and from rational impossibilities upon Him, may He be exalted. (For this reason) i.e., because it is from rational impossibilities, the philosophers affirmed it, i.e., they affirmed it as an impossible deficiency for Him to be described with, despite their not attributing their statements to any prophet. So there is no necessary connection between deficiency and evil. (But it entails upon the Ash'aris) followers of al-Ash'ari (the impossibility of punishing the obedient) by God in actions (as is our school, the Maturidis, and the school of the Mu'tazila), for punishing the obedient is a deficiency rationally impossible for Him. So this answer cannot come from them. Moreover, it is refuted against them: punishing the disobedient also cannot be valid, for he did not become disobedient by his own choice; rather God, may He be exalted, as is our view and the view of the Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, made a person disobedient, then punishing him for it ('why did you disobey?') is a deficiency, so it is impossible for Him. This does not entail us, for we say: Some actions are such that punishment follows them. So punishment following disobedience is like fever following overeating; there is no deficiency in it, because giving what a thing is suited for is not evil even if it is painful. If you say: Then why did He create these actions in the essences of the disobedient until this kind of severe pain occurs? We say: The verification is that just as in actions there is a deserving that pain or comfort follows them, similarly in essences there is a deserving to be qualified with actions, so He directs His power to resolve, thus this deserving is completed, and the Bestower creates the act in it, so it is qualified by it. If you say: Then forgiveness is not valid, because it contradicts what the action deserves. I say: No, rather some (actions) deserve both the permissibility of forgiveness and the permissibility of punishment. Similarly, some essences deserve forgiveness because they deserve to be qualified with a good act by which the deserving of forgiveness is completed, so they are qualified by it and forgiven. Therefore, disbelief is not forgiven, and the disbeliever is not excused in any way, because disbelief deserves only punishment. Moreover, forgiving one who deserves pain is a quality of perfection, not deficiency, so no objection. And we do not mean by 'deserving' that there is a quality expressed as 'deserving' and 'preparedness' as in the terminology of the philosophers; rather deserving is its suitability for it, and this suitability is the preparedness. Details of such discussions are in the commentaries on the texts of wisdom.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a rational dispute over the basis of obligation, specifically the obligation of reflective thought (nazar) to recognize the truth of revelation. The author defends the Hanafi-Maturidi position that the call to reflection is inherently understood by any rational person who hears the address, thereby avoiding the charge of obligating the truly negligent (the sleeping, insane). The response to the defiant opponent who refuses to reflect until he knows the obligation is that the messenger can appeal to apparent harm or to the display of miracles, which compels rational investigation. The discussion then shifts to the necessity of miracles: the Mu'tazila hold them rationally obligatory, while the Maturidis say they are customary (not rationally necessary but God's consistent practice). The text then engages with the Ash'ari position on divine punishment, arguing that punishing the obedient is rationally impossible (admitted by Ash'aris) but also showing that punishing the disobedient is not a deficiency because actions have inherent suitability for pain. The author concludes that deserving punishment or forgiveness is based on suitability, not a pre-existing quality, aligning with Maturidi theology.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar): Intellectual reflection to arrive at knowledge of God and prophethood.
تكليف الغافل (taklīf al-ghāfil): Obligating someone who is unaware of the obligation and does not understand the address.
إفحام (ifḥām): Silencing the opponent by proving their argument leads to absurdity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the call to reflection not considered 'obligating the negligent'?
A) Because reflection is only obligatory after seeing a miracle.
B) Because the one called understands the address, unlike the sleeping or insane.
C) Because reflection is not obligatory at all until one knows the obligation.
D) Because the negligent person is not a rational being.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of reflection (nazar) before knowledge of revelation is not taklif al-ghafil because the addressee understands the call and is rationally compelled to investigate.

The Rational Obligation of Gratitude to the Benefactor
Argument against the Ash'ari denial of rational obligation of shukr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi-Maturidi position on the rational obligation of shukr al-mun'im and evaluate the Ash'ari counter-argument using tanazzul.

Core Idea
Gratitude to the Benefactor is rationally obligatory; the Ash'ari objection, if sound, would negate all rational obligation.

This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafi (Maturidi) school and the Ash'aris on whether gratitude to God is obligatory by reason alone. The Ash'aris concede rational obligation in general (tanazzul) but deny it specifically for shukr. The author refutes this by showing the concession undermines their argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine the exact question: Is shukr al-mun'im rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqlan) or only known through revelation?
2
Understand the Ash'ari position on tanazzul
The Ash'aris, on the premise of concession, argue that it is not rationally obligatory. They accept rational judgment in general but deny it for this specific case.
Tanazzul means temporarily adopting the opponent's premise.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Many Hanafis hold that good and evil are of two types: one known by reason, including gratitude. Accountability for abandoning gratitude is rational.
Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly states this is the Hanafi view.
4
Examine the Ash'ari counter-proof
They argue that rational obligation requires a benefit; none benefits God (exalted) and for the servant worldly benefit is hardship and the afterlife is beyond reason.
This is a classic Ash'ari argument against rational obligation.
5
Follow the Hanafi refutation
The author points out that the Ash'ari concession already admits rational judgment in general. If their argument were sound, it would negate all rational obligation, which even Ash'aris accept. The discussion is about the specific after conceding the general.
The refutation uses a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [شكر المنعم] قال الأشعرية : (على التنزل شكر المنعم ليس بواجب عقلاً، خلافاً للمعتزلة) ومعظم مشايخنا، وقد نص صدر الشريعة على أن شكر المنعم واجب عقلاً عندنا، وفي الكشف نقلاً عن القواطع، وذهب طائفة من أصحابنا إلى أن الحسن والقبح ضربان ضرب يعلم بالعقل كحسن العدل والصدق النافع، وشكر النعمة وقبح الظلم والكذب الضار، ثم قال : وإليه ذهب كثير من أصحاب الإمام أبي حنيفة، خصوصاً العراقيين منهم، وهو مذهب المعتزلة بأسرهم، ومعرفة الحسن هو الوجوب، أو لازمه إذ الغرض أن المؤاخذة في ترك الشكر عقلية تعرف بالعقل، والمراد بالشكرها هنا صرف العبد جميع ما أعطي إلى ما خلق لأجله كالعين لمشاهدة ما تحل مشاهدته ليستدل به علی عجيب صنعة الحق تعالى ولعلهم أرادوا بالصرف الصرف الذي يدرك بالعقل لا الصرف مطلقاً، وإلا فلا معنى لدعوى العقلية واستدل بأنه لو وجب شكر المنعم عقلاً لوجب لفائدة وإلا كان عبثاً ولا فائدة له تعالى لتعاليه عنها) إذ ليس له كمال منتظر (ولا) للعبد لأنه لو كان فإما في الدنيا أو الآخرة وهما منتفيان (أما في الدنيا، فلأنه مشقة وهي بلاء لا يصلح فائدة (وأما في الآخرة، فلأنه لا مجال للعقل في ذلك، أقول) في ردّه أنه (بعد تسليم ما ادعاه المعتزلة من كون الحكم عقلياً في الجملة (كما هو معنى التنزل القول بأنه لا مجال للعقل مشكل) فإنه قد سلم المجال على أنه لو تم هذا الاستدلال لاستلزم عدم الوجوب مطلقاً، والظاهر) من التنزل ( أن الكلام في الخاص بعد تسليم المطلق مع أن فيه
Issue [Gratitude to the Benefactor]. The Ash'aris said: (On the assumption of concession) gratitude to the Benefactor is not rationally obligatory, contrary to the Mu'tazila and most of our masters. Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly stated that gratitude to the Benefactor is rationally obligatory according to us. In al-Kashf, quoting from al-Qawati', a group of our associates said: Good and evil are of two types: one known by reason, such as the goodness of justice and beneficial truth, and gratitude for blessing, and the evil of injustice and harmful lies. Then he said: Many of the associates of Imam Abu Hanifa, especially the Iraqis among them, held this view, and it is the school of all the Mu'tazila. Knowledge of goodness is obligation or its necessary consequence, for the purpose is that accountability for abandoning gratitude is rational, known by reason. What is meant by gratitude here is the servant's directing all that he has been given to that for which it was created, like the eye for observing what is permissible to observe so that he may deduce from it the wondrous work of the Truth, may He be exalted. Perhaps they meant by 'directing' the directing that is perceived by reason, not directing absolutely; otherwise, there would be no meaning to the claim of rationality. He argued that if gratitude to the Benefactor were rationally obligatory, it would have to be for a benefit; otherwise it would be futile. There is no benefit for Him, may He be exalted, for it is beyond such things, since He has no awaited perfection. Nor for the servant, because if there is a benefit, it would be either in this world or in the Hereafter, both of which are negated. As for this world, because it is hardship and a trial, not a benefit. As for the Hereafter, because reason has no scope in that. I say in refutation: After conceding what the Mu'tazila claimed that the judgment is rational in general (as is the meaning of the assumption), the statement that reason has no scope is problematic, for he has conceded scope. Moreover, if this argument were sound, it would entail non-obligation absolutely. And it is apparent from the assumption that the discussion is about the specific after conceding the general, although in it...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This is a rational and theological dispute about whether gratitude to God (shukr al-mun'im) is obligatory by reason alone, prior to revelation. The Ash'aris, on a conceding premise (tanazzul), argue that it is not rationally obligatory, contrary to the Mu'tazila and many Hanafis (especially the Iraqis). The author notes that Sadr al-Shari'ah and most Maturidi scholars affirm rational obligation, distinguishing between good and evil known by reason. The Ash'ari proof: If gratitude were rationally obligatory, it would need a benefit; none benefits God, and for the servant, worldly benefit is hardship and no benefit, and the Hereafter is beyond reason. The author refutes by pointing out that the Ash'ari argument, if sound, would negate all rational obligation, which even Ash'aris admit in some cases. The passage cuts off but continues the defense of rational obligation.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم (shukr al-mun'im): The servant's directing all gifts to their intended purpose, as recognized by reason, leading to gratitude to the Giver.
التنزل (al-tanazzul): A concessive argument where the disputant temporarily adopts the opponent's premise to show the flaw in their conclusion.
حسن وقبح (ḥusn wa qubḥ): Goodness and evil of actions; some are known by reason, others by revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because revelation commands it.
B) Because reason knows the goodness of gratitude and the evil of ingratitude.
C) Because the Mu'tazila say so.
D) Because it benefits God.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Maturidi school affirms that gratitude to the Benefactor is rationally obligatory; the Ash'ari objection based on benefit is refuted by showing it would undermine all rational obligation.

The Intellectual Obligation of Gratitude to the Benefactor
Usul dispute: intellectual obligation of gratitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three main positions on shukr al-mun'im and analyze the arguments and counterarguments presented in the Hanafi-Ash'ari dispute.

Core Idea
Whether gratitude to God is rationally obligatory is a foundational usul dispute about the role of reason in determining obligation.

The question of whether we must thank God by using His gifts as intended, even without revelation, is not merely a theological curiosity — it is a key usul dispute that determines the scope of rational obligation (wujub 'aqli). The passage you are about to read presents the Ash'ari position (denying rational obligation), the Mu'tazila and many Hanafi positions (affirming it), and a series of objections and replies that sharpen your ability to evaluate legal proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Positions
Extract the Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Hanafi stances on the intellectual obligation of shukr al-mun'im. Note that some Hanafis (including Sadr al-Shari'ah) agree with the Mu'tazila, while others side with the Ash'aris.
The Ash'ari argument is presented 'on concession' (على التنزل) of the Mu'tazila premise that reason can judge obligation in principle.
2
Analyze the Core Proof and Rebuttal
The Ash'aris argue that obligation must yield a benefit, which is impossible for God (perfect) or the servant (worldly hardship, hereafter beyond reason). The Hanafi/Mu'tazila response: hardship can include benefits, citing Quran 29:69.
The rebuttal shifts the debate to whether the servant's 'security from punishment' is a valid rational benefit.
3
Examine the Two Objections to Obligation
Objection 1: Gratitude uses God's property without permission, making it haram. Reply: rational permission exists, analogous to taking shade or light. Objection 2: Gratitude resembles mockery. Reply: sincerity matters; the Shari'ah would not obligate mockery.
These objections are external critiques, not from the Ash'ari argument itself.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: For someone before prophethood, denying rational obligation means no sin for ingratitude; affirming it means sin. This affects the status of non-Muslims before revelation.
The passage does not give a final tarjih, but the dispute remains alive in usul discussions.

Issue: [Gratitude to the Benefactor]
The Ash'aris said (on the assumption of concession): Gratitude to the benefactor is not intellectually obligatory, contrary to the Mu'tazila and most of our mashayikh. And Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly stated that gratitude to the benefactor is intellectually obligatory according to us. In al-Kashf, quoting from al-Qawati', a group of our companions held that good and evil are of two types: one type known by reason, like the goodness of justice, beneficial truthfulness, and gratitude for blessing, and the evil of injustice and harmful lying. Then he said: This is the view of many of the companions of Imam Abu Hanifa, especially the Iraqis among them, and it is the position of the Mu'tazila entirely. And knowledge of the good means obligation or its necessary consequence, because the purpose is that the penalty for abandoning gratitude is rational, known by reason. The intended meaning of gratitude here is the servant spending all that he has been given for that for which it was created, like the eye for observing what is permissible to observe to infer from it the wondrous work of the Truth Most High. Perhaps they meant by 'spending' the spending that is perceived by reason, not spending absolutely, otherwise the claim of rationality would be meaningless. And they argued that if gratitude to the benefactor were intellectually obligatory, it would be for a benefit, otherwise it would be futile, and there is no benefit for Him Most High because He is exalted above benefits, since He has no anticipated perfection, nor for the servant, because if there were a benefit, it would be either in this world or the Hereafter, both of which are negated. As for this world, it is hardship which is a trial, not a suitable benefit. As for the Hereafter, reason has no access to that. I say in response to it that after conceding what the Mu'tazila claimed of the ruling being rational in general (as is the meaning of the concession), saying that reason has no access is problematic, for he has already conceded access. And even if this argument were sound, it would entail the negation of obligation entirely. And the apparent meaning of the concession is that the discussion is about the specific after conceding the general, though there is another confusion in it, for hardship does not negate benefit; rather hardship may encompass countless benefits. 'Indeed, gifts lie upon trials.' Allah Most High said: 'And those who strive for Us – We will surely guide them to Our ways' (Quran 29:69). The Mu'tazila said: It (gratitude) entails security from the possibility of punishment for abandoning it, and everything that is like that is obligatory. So gratitude to the benefactor is obligatory. But the major premise may be denied rationally; rather, doing such an act is preferable, and if it is aided by the Shari'ah, the obligation is not rational but becomes legal. Sadr al-Shari'ah said: How can a rational person think that I am given by the Sovereign Bestower that which sustains my life and I take pleasure in foods, drinks, and clothing, and I am immersed in seas of mercy and covered every moment with types of blessings that cannot be counted or enumerated, then after that I show ingratitude for those blessings with various kinds of ingratitude and deny Him with various foul denials, while the Sovereign is capable of severe punishment? With all this, how would He not seize me with a kind of punishment, nor blame me for any of that? This is no more than a claim of necessity. And their argument was objected to, first: that it is a disposition in the property of someone else without his permission, because the servant with all his faculties is in the possession of the Lord, and gratitude cannot be except by exhausting them and spending them, so it is a disposition in another's property without his command, which is forbidden, so gratitude is forbidden. And it is answered: We do not accept that it is a disposition without the owner's permission; rather it is by rational permission from Him, based on the example of seeking shade and seeking light, for reason holds that the owner of the wall and the lamp is pleased with them. And it was objected, secondly: that gratitude resembles mockery, and everything that resembles mockery is forbidden. And it resembles mockery because the proportion of what is given to what is in the possession of the Benefactor is less than the proportion of a morsel given by one who owns the treasures of the East and West, and if one who gave that morsel takes it and in gatherings mentions his gift and gratitude, he is considered a jester and mocker. And it is very weak, for what matters with Allah is sincerity in intention. Also, if it resembled mockery, it would be forbidden by the Shari'ah, so how can it be said that the Shari'ah came with the obligation of mockery? So ponder.
The blame, rather he is pardoned from
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents a classical usul dispute (usul_dispute) concerning the intellectual obligation of gratitude to the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im). The author first states the Ash'ari position (on the concession of the Mu'tazila's premise) that gratitude is not intellectually obligatory, contrary to the Mu'tazila and most Hanafi scholars (most of our mashayikh). However, Sadr al-Shari'ah explicitly asserts that gratitude is intellectually obligatory in the Hanafi school. A group of Hanafis, especially the Iraqis, hold that good and evil are rationally knowable, and gratitude falls under that. The Mu'tazila argue that gratitude leads to security from punishment, so it is wajib. The author then provides the Ash'ari proof: if gratitude were obligatory, it would be for a benefit, but no benefit exists for God (who is perfect) nor for the servant (since worldly benefit is hardship and the Hereafter is beyond reason). The author rebuts this by noting that hardship can encompass benefits, citing the Quranic verse about striving. The Mu'tazila respond that gratitude entails safety from punishment. The author then presents two objections to the view of obligation: (1) gratitude involves using God's property without permission, making it haram, but the reply is that rational permission exists, like taking shade or light from another's property. (2) Gratitude resembles mockery, but the reply is that sincerity is what matters, and the Shari'ah would not obligate mockery. The passage concludes with a line about blame and pardon, possibly transitioning to the next issue. The author does not settle the dispute here but presents the arguments and responses.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم (shukr al-mun'im): Gratitude to the Benefactor (God) by using His gifts for their intended purposes.
حسن وقبح (husn wa qubh): Goodness and evil of actions, some rationally knowable.
وجوب عقلي (wujub 'aqli): Obligation established by reason alone, without revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position presented in the passage, why is gratitude to the Benefactor not intellectually obligatory?
A) Because gratitude is impossible without revelation.
B) Because it would entail a benefit for God or the servant, which is impossible.
C) Because God does not need gratitude.
D) Because the servant owns nothing.

Key Takeaway
The intellectual obligation of gratitude is a key usul dispute that hinges on whether reason alone can determine obligation without revelation, with the Hanafi/Mu'tazila affirming and Ash'aris denying.

Knowledge of Rulings Before Prophethood in Hanafi Usul
Usul dispute on knowledge of rulings before prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether rulings are known before prophethood and identify the key points of disagreement with the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris.

Core Idea
The ruling is eternal in God's speech, but its specific knowledge before prophethood is not necessary; reason can discover some rulings after reflection.

One of the foundational debates in usul al-fiqh is whether God's rulings must be known through prophets or can be known through reason alone. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses the Hanafi position, asserting that while the ruling (hukm) is eternal as God's attribute, its specific application may not be known before a prophet is sent. However, reason can still discern certain prohibitions and obligations after reflection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core agreement
Recognize that all schools agree the ruling is eternal (qadim) as God's attribute of speech, but there is disagreement on whether it can be known before prophethood.
The passage states 'no disagreement' on the eternality of the ruling.
2
Distinguish the Mu'tazili and Ash'ari positions
The Mu'tazila hold that the ruling is essential (dhati) but not all aspects are rationally knowable. The Ash'aris treat the issue as a concession, implying no ruling before prophethood.
The author criticizes the Ash'ari approach as 'corrupted'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis initially state there is no restriction (haraj) before prophethood, but their later mashayikh refine this: reason can, after reflection, discover some rulings like the prohibition of shirk and obligation of faith.
This refinement shows that 'no restriction' does not mean all actions are permissible.
4
Connect to the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether actions before prophethood are considered sinful. Hanafis hold that some actions (like kufr) can be known as sinful by reason after reflection, but not all are determinable.
This contrasts with the Ash'ari view that no ruling exists at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [معرفة الحكم قبل البعثة]
لا خلاف في أن الحكم وإن كان في كل فعل قديماً عندنا لأنه الخطاب القديم (لكن يجوز أن لا يعلم قبل البعثة بعض منه اتفاقاً (بخصوصه، أما عند المعتزلة فلأنه أي الحكم (وإن كان ذاتياً) لا يتوقف على الشرع لكن منه ما لا يدرك بالعقل علة الحسن والقبح فيه فلا يحكم عليه هناك عقلاً وأما عند غيرهم من أهل الحق فلأن الموجب وإن كان الكلام النفسي القديم، لكن ربما كان ظهوره بالتعلق الحادث بحدوث البعثة فلا حكم مشخص قبلها ومن ها هنا ظهر فساد ما اعتاده الأشعرية من جعل هذه المسألة تنزلية (فلا حرج عندنا) في شيء من الفعل والترك، حتى الكفر والشرك ومشايخنا لا يعرجون عليه ويقولون : قد يظهر بعض ما يوجبه الكلام النفسي بالعقل بعد مضي مدة التأمل من حرمة الشرك ووجوب الإيمان كما قد مر، فإن قيل : فعلى ما ذكر، كيف يصح الخلاف بين أهل السنة من أن الأصل الإباحة أو التحريم ؟ أجاب بقوله : (وأما الخلاف المنقول بين أهل الحق أن أصل الأفعال للإباحة كما هو
Issue: [Knowledge of the ruling before the prophetic mission]
There is no disagreement that the ruling, although it is eternal in every action according to us because it is the eternal speech, it is possible that some of it is not known before the prophetic mission, by agreement in its specifics. As for the Mu'tazila, because the ruling (although it is essential) does not depend on the law, but some of it is not perceived by reason as the cause of goodness or evil, so no ruling is made there rationally. As for others among the people of truth, because the obligator, although it is the eternal inner speech, perhaps its manifestation occurs through a contingent connection with the occurrence of the prophetic mission, so there is no specific ruling before it. From here appears the corruption of what the Ash'aris are accustomed to of making this issue concessional. So there is no restriction according to us in any act or abandonment, even unbelief and polytheism. And our mashayikh do not rely on that and say: Some of what the inner speech necessitates may appear by reason after a period of reflection, like the prohibition of polytheism and the obligation of faith, as has passed. For if it is said: On what has been mentioned, how can the disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah be valid that the original state is permissibility or prohibition? He answered by saying: As for the transmitted disagreement among the people of truth that the original state of actions is permissibility, as it is
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage addresses the usul issue of whether rulings can be known before the sending of prophets (qabl al-bi'tha). The author states that there is agreement that the ruling (hukm) is eternal (qadim) as God's attribute of speech, but it may not be known in its specific details before the mission. For the Mu'tazila, the ruling is essential (dhati) but some aspects are not rationally knowable as causes of good/evil. For the Ash'aris and others, the obligation is the eternal speech but its appearance is contingent on the prophet's coming, so no determinate ruling exists before. The author criticizes the Ash'ari practice of treating this as a concessional issue. The Hanafi position is that there is no restriction (haraj) before prophethood—meaning no specific prohibition or obligation—so even kufr and shirk are not yet legally prohibited? However, the author notes that the Hanafi mashayikh do not rely on that (i.e., they do not say it is permissible), because reason may discern some rulings after reflection, such as the prohibition of shirk and obligation of iman. The passage then transitions to the famous khilaf among Ahl al-Sunnah: whether the original state of actions is permissibility (ibaha) or prohibition (tahrim). The author seems to be defending the Hanafi view that the original state is ibaha, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): The divine ruling (line of address) concerning actions, which is eternal as God's speech.
بعثة (bi'tha): The sending of a prophet to convey divine commands.
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): The eternal inner speech of God, as distinct from the created words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi view regarding rulings before the prophetic mission?
A) All actions are permissible until a prophet comes.
B) There is no specific ruling, but some may be known by reason after reflection.
C) All actions are forbidden until revelation.
D) Rulings are known by consensus before prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that while specific rulings are not known before prophethood, reason can discern some prohibitions after reflection, such as the prohibition of shirk.

The Rational Obligation of Gratitude: Hanafi vs Mu'tazila
Refutation of Mu'tazila claim that gratitude is rationally obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that gratitude to the Benefactor is rationally obligatory, and apply the distinction between rational and legal obligation.

Core Idea
Gratitude to Allah is a legal duty, not a rational one — reason alone cannot bind.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut addresses a foundational usul dispute: Is gratitude to the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im) obligatory by reason alone, or only by divine command? The Mu'tazila argue for rational obligation; the Hanafis reject this, maintaining that obligation stems exclusively from the Law (shar'). The author presents and refutes the Mu'tazila evidence, clarifying that reason only indicates preferability, not necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim and their proof
The Mu'tazila claim that gratitude is rationally obligatory because leaving it entails the possibility of punishment, and everything with that property is obligatory. This is their major premise.
The major premise is critical: 'whatever entails security from punishment by leaving it is obligatory'.
2
Analyze the Hanafi denial of the major premise
The Hanafi response denies the major premise: even if leaving gratitude could lead to punishment, reason does not impose obligation; it only indicates that performing the act is preferable (awla). If the Law is invoked to establish obligation, then it becomes legal (shar'i), not rational.
This is the core Hanafi position: obligation requires a divine command.
3
Examine the first objection (disposal of property) and the Hanafi response
Objection: Gratitude uses powers owned by Allah, so it would be disposal of another's property without permission, hence forbidden. Response: There is rational permission (al-idhn al-'aqli), analogous to using someone's wall for shade or lamp for light, where reason presumes the owner's consent.
The analogy shows that rational permission is sufficient to remove prohibition, but not to impose obligation.
4
Examine the second objection (resemblance to mockery) and the rebuttal
Objection: Gratitude by a servant compared to Allah's blessings is like a paltry gift being publicly thanked, resembling mockery, which is forbidden. Rebuttal: What matters is sincerity (ikhlas); the Law would not command mockery, so the objection is weak.
External resemblance does not determine ruling; intention is decisive.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If gratitude were rationally obligatory, those before prophethood who did not show gratitude would be punishable. The Hanafi position holds that they are not punished because no divine command reached them. This highlights that obligation depends on revelation.
This practical difference is a key application in usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتزلة قالوا إنه يستلزم الأمن من احتمال العقاب بتركه وكل ما كان كذلك فهو واجب) فشكر المنعم واجب، وقد تمنع الكبرى عقلاً، بل ما كان كذلك ففعله أولى، وإن استعين بالشرع لم يكن الوجوب عقلياً، بل صار شرعياً قال صدر الشريعة، كيف يجوز عاقل أن أعطي من الملك الوهاب ما تقوم به حياته ويستلذ به من المأكولات والمشروبات والملبوسات، وأغرق في بحار الرحمة وغطى كل لحظة بأنواع النعم التي لا يمكن تعدادها وإحصاؤها، ثم بعد ذلك يكفر تلك النعم بأنواع الكفران ويكذبه بأنواع التكذيبات الشنيعة، والملك قادر على الأخذ الشديد، فمع هذا كله كيف لا يأخذه بنوع من أنواع التعذيب، ولا يذمه بشيء من ذلك كله، ولا يزيد هذا على دعوى الضرورة (وعورض) دليلهم (أولاً: بأنه تصرف في ملك الغير بغير إذنه لأن العبد مع جميع القوى في ملك الرب، والشكر لا يكون إلا بإتعابها وصرفها فيكون تصرفاً في ملك الغير بغير أمره، وهو حرام، فالشكر حرام (ويجاب) بأنا لا نسلم أنه تصرف من غير إذن المالك (بل) بالإذن العقلي) من جهته بناء على أنه مثال الاستظلال والاستصباح) فإن العقل يحكم أن صاحب الجدار والمصباح راضيان بهما (و) عورض (ثانياً بأنه أي الشكر يشبه الاستهزاء وكل ما يشبه الاستهزاء فهو حرام، وشبه بالاستهزاء لأنه نسبة ما أعطي إلى ما في ملك المنعم أقل من نسبة لقمة أعطاها الذي ملك خزائن المشرق والمغرب، وأن أخذ من أعطى تلك اللقمة في المحافل يذكر عطاءه وشكره عدّ لاعباً ومستهزئاً (وهو ضعيف جداً فإن المعتبر عند الله تعالى الإخلاص) في النية (وأيضاً) لو كان يشبه الاستهزاء لكان حراماً بالشرع وكيف يقال : إن الشرع ورد بوجوب الاستهزاء) فتدبر
The Mu'tazila said: It (the obligation) entails security from the possibility of punishment by leaving it, and everything that is like that is obligatory. So gratitude to the Benefactor is obligatory. But the major premise may be refuted rationally; rather, if something is like that, performing it is preferable. If help is sought from the Law, then the obligation is not rational, but becomes legal. Sadr al-Shari'ah said: How can a rational person [think] that I am given by the King, the Bestower, what sustains my life and I enjoy of foods, drinks, and clothes, and I am submerged in oceans of mercy and covered every moment with kinds of blessings that cannot be enumerated and counted, then after that I disbelieve those blessings with kinds of ingratitude and deny Him with kinds of ugly denials, while the King is capable of severe punishment? Despite all this, how would He not seize him with some kind of punishment, nor blame him for any of that? This is nothing more than a claim of necessity. Their evidence was objected to: First: because it is a disposal in the property of another without his permission, for the servant with all his powers is in the ownership of the Lord, and gratitude is only by exhausting and using them, so it is a disposal in the property of another without his command, and that is forbidden, so gratitude is forbidden. It is answered: We do not accept that it is a disposal without the owner's permission; rather, it is with rational permission from Him, based on the example of seeking shade and seeking light, for reason judges that the owner of the wall and the lamp is pleased with them. Second objection: Gratitude resembles mockery, and everything that resembles mockery is forbidden. It resembles mockery because the proportion of what is given to what is in the Benefactor's possession is less than the proportion of a mouthful given by one who owns the treasures of the east and west. And if one who gave that mouthful takes him to assemblies and mentions his gift and thanks, he is considered a jester and mocker. This is very weak, for what matters to Allah is sincerity in intention. Also, if it resembled mockery, it would be forbidden by the Law; how can it be said that the Law commands the obligation of mockery? So reflect.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) concerning the obligation of gratitude to the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im). The Mu'tazila argue that gratitude is rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqli) because failing to perform it would entail the possibility of punishment, and anything with that property is obligatory. The author refutes this by first denying the major premise: even if something has that property, it does not necessitate obligation; rather, performing it is merely preferable (awla). If one relies on revelation to establish obligation, then the obligation becomes legal (shar'i), not rational. Sadr al-Shari'ah then presents a rhetorical argument: despite Allah's immense blessings, ingratitude is possible, yet He does not necessarily punish; thus, the Mu'tazili claim that punishment is rationally necessary is unfounded. The passage then provides two objections to the Mu'tazili position. The first objection: gratitude requires using one's limbs and faculties, which are owned by Allah, so it would be a disposal of another's property without permission, hence forbidden. But the answer is that there is rational permission, analogous to using someone's wall for shade or lamp for light, where reason presumes the owner's consent. The second objection: gratitude resembles mockery because the puny act of a servant compared to Allah's blessings is like a pittance given by a wealthy man being publicly thanked—it seems like mockery. But this is weak because sincerity is what matters; if it truly resembled mockery, the Law would not command it. The author concludes that the Mu'tazili claim is unfounded.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم (shukr al-mun'im): Gratitude expressed to the One who bestows blessings, specifically through acts of obedience and use of faculties in a manner pleasing to Allah.
الوجوب العقلي (al-wujub al-'aqli): Obligation deduced by reason independently of revelation; according to the author, such obligation does not exist because reason only indicates preferability, not necessity.
الاستزاء (al-istizha'): Mockery; resemblance to mockery in the act of gratitude is dismissed because divine consideration is for sincerity, not outward resemblance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the objection that gratitude is a disposal in another's property without permission?
A) It is forbidden unconditionally.
B) There is rational permission, like using a wall for shade.
C) It is only forbidden if done publicly.
D) It is always permissible because the servant owns his actions.

Key Takeaway
Reason alone does not create obligation; only divine command makes an act obligatory — a foundational Hanafi usul principle.

8 min
Rulings Before Prophethood
Dispute on rulings before prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on rulings before prophethood and evaluate the Mu'tazili counter-argument.

Core Idea
No blame before revelation — pre-eternal rulings attach only at prophecy.

This lesson explores a foundational usul question: do specific rulings exist and obligate humans before Allah sends a prophet? The Hanafi school affirms the pre-eternal nature of Allah's speech but denies that humans are accountable for specific rulings until revelation comes. This position has profound implications for the status of actions like disbelief before prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The core question: Are specific legal obligations or prohibitions known and applicable to humans before the mission of a prophet?
This is a dispute between Hanafis, Mu'tazila, and others.
2
Highlight the Hanafi Position
Hanafis hold that the ruling (hukm) is pre-eternal in Allah's attribute of speech, but its attachment (ta'alluq) to specific acts occurs only with revelation. Hence, no specific ruling is applicable before prophethood.
The ruling exists but is not 'mushakhkhas' (specified) before the prophet.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazili Position
Mu'tazila argue that rulings are inherent in actions (dhāti), and reason can perceive some obligations and prohibitions. They claim that some acts are blameworthy even without revelation.
This relies on the capability of reason to discern good and evil.
4
Understand the Hanafi Response
Reason cannot fully discern the cause of good and evil; even if it perceives something, it does not create legal obligation or prohibition. Legal obligation depends on divine communication, not reason alone.
The Hanafi response preserves the necessity of prophecy for taklif.
5
Apply the Conclusion
The practical conclusion: 'la haraj' (no blame) for any action or omission before prophethood, even disbelief and polytheism. Some Hanafi masters note that after reflection, reason can recognize the wrongness of shirk, but that is not a pre-existing legal obligation.
This distinguishes between rational discernment and legal obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [معرفة الحكم قبل البعثة] ما يشبه لا خلاف في أن الحكم وإن كان في كل فعل قديماً عندنا لأنه الخطاب القديم (لكن يجوز أن لا يعلم قبل البعثة بعض منه اتفاقاً (بخصوصه، أما عند المعتزلة فلأنه أي الحكم (وإن كان ذاتياً) لا يتوقف على الشرع لكن منه ما لا يدرك بالعقل علة الحسن والقبح فيه فلا يحكم عليه هناك عقلاً وأما عند غيرهم من أهل الحق فلأن الموجب وإن كان الكلام النفسي القديم، لكن ربما كان ظهوره بالتعلق الحادث بحدوث البعثة فلا حكم مشخص قبلها ومن ها هنا ظهر فساد ما اعتاده الأشعرية من جعل هذه المسألة تنزلية (فلا حرج عندنا) في شيء من الفعل والترك، حتى الكفر والشرك ومشايخنا لا يعرجون عليه ويقولون : قد يظهر بعض ما يوجبه الكلام النفسي بالعقل بعد مضي مدة التأمل من حرمة الشرك ووجوب الإيمان كما قد مر
Issue [Knowing the Ruling Before the Mission]. Something similar: There is no difference that the ruling, although it is pre-eternal in every act according to us because it is the pre-eternal address, it is possible that some of it is not known before the mission unanimously [in its specific details]. As for the Mu'tazila, because the ruling, although it is inherent, does not depend on the Law, but among it there is that whose cause of goodness and evil is not perceived by reason, so no ruling is made by reason there. As for others among Ahl al-Haqq, because the obligator, although it is the pre-eternal inner speech, perhaps its manifestation is through the temporal attachment occurring with the occurrence of the mission, so there is no specific ruling before it. Hence appears the corruption of the habit of the Ash'aris in considering this issue a corollary. So there is no restriction [blame] according to us in any action or abandonment, even disbelief and polytheism. And our masters do not pay attention to it and say: Some of what the inner speech necessitates may appear by reason after a period of reflection, such as the prohibition of polytheism and the obligation of faith, as has passed.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological status of rulings before the advent of prophets (qabl al-ba'tha). The author asserts that there is agreement (except in specifics) that although rulings are pre-eternal (qadim) as the divine attribute of speech, their specific details may not be known before prophethood. The Mu'tazila hold that rulings are inherent in actions and reason can perceive some but not all; thus, reason cannot judge actions where it cannot discern the cause of goodness or evil. The Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox, including the Hanafi school) maintain that the obligating factor is the eternal inner speech (kalam nafsi), but its manifestation occurs through the temporal act of revelation (ta'alluq hadith) when a prophet is sent; hence, before prophethood, there is no specific ruling (hukm mushakhkhas). The author criticizes the Ash'ari habit of treating this as a corollary issue (tanzi'iyya). The practical conclusion is that before prophethood, there is no blame (haraj) for any act or omission, even disbelief and polytheism. Some Hanafi masters add that reason can still deduce the prohibition of polytheism and obligation of faith after reflection, but this is a later intellective recognition, not a pre-existing obligation.

Key Terms
الحكم القديم (al-hukm al-qadim): The pre-eternal ruling existing in Allah's attribute of speech, not created in time.
البعثة (al-ba'tha): The mission of a prophet commissioned by Allah to convey divine commands.
الحرج (al-haraj): Restriction or blame; 'la haraj' means no legal or moral culpability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the status of actions like disbelief before the mission of a prophet?
A) They are punishable because reason dictates their evil.
B) There is no blame (haraj) for them.
C) They are forbidden by default.
D) They are obligatory to avoid.

Key Takeaway
Before prophethood, there is no legal blame for any action; specific rulings attach only with revelation.
