Original Ruling of Actions and the Period of Fatra
Resolving the dispute on asl al-af'al by restricting it to zaman al-fatra

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the context of the dispute on asl al-af'al and explain how the concept of zaman al-fatra resolves it.

Core Idea
The dispute on original permissibility vs. prohibition is confined to the fatra period when divine law is unknown.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul debate: what is the default ruling for a human action before revelation? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools generally hold that the original is permissibility, while others hold prohibition. The author clarifies that this dispute is meaningless if applied to all of history, because every era has had a prophet and a law. Instead, the dispute applies only to the period of fatra (interregnum) when a previous law has been lost. Understanding this limitation is crucial for correct application of usul principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Recognize that the dispute is between those who say the original ruling of actions is permissibility (ibahah) and those who say it is prohibition (hazr). Note that the Hanafi/Shafi'i school favors permissibility, while others favor prohibition.
These positions are about the period before any specific divine law is known.
2
Understand the problem with universal application
If the dispute applied to all times, it would contradict the Quranic evidence that every nation received a prophet. Thus, the dispute must be restricted.
The author uses the Quran to establish that no era has been without divine guidance.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Surah Fatir (35:24) and Surah al-Qiyamah (75:36) establish that no era has been without a warner. This is the key premise that refutes the universal scope of the dispute.
These verses are qat'i in transmission and meaning.
4
Restrict the dispute to zaman al-fatra
The only period where the law is unknown is the fatra, after a previous law has been lost due to negligence. The dispute is meaningful only in that context.
Zaman al-fatra is the interregnum between prophets when revealed law is absent.
5
Analyze the two views within fatra
If ignorance is an excuse, actions are not blameworthy (Hanafi view, called ibahah asliyyah). If ignorance is not excused, caution dictates prohibition (opposing view). Note the exception for harming lives (Sadr al-Islam).
The practical consequence: in fatra, a person eating is not sinful per the Hanafi view, but would be sinful per the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعلى ما ذكر، كيف يصح الخلاف من أن الأصل الإباحة أو التحريم ؟ أجاب بقوله : (وأما الخلاف المنقول بين أهل الحق أن أصل الأفعال للإباحة كما هو مختار أكثر الحنفية والشافعية (أو أصلها الحظر، كما ذهب إليه غيرهم، وقال صدر الإسلام) الأصل (الإباحة في الأموال والحظر في الأنفس) فقتل النفس، وقطع العضو وإيلامه بالضرب، والتصرف على الفروج بقيت على الحرمة ، إلا ما خص منها بدليل كالقصاص والنكاح (فقبل) هذا الخلاف وقع بعد الشرع بالأدلة السمعية أي دلت تلك الأدلة على أن ما لم يقم فيه دليل مأذون فيه بدلالة دليل آخر، كما عند أكثر الحنفية والشافعية (أو ممنوع) عنه بدلالة دليل آخر كما عند غيرهم فلا ينافي هذا عدم الحرج قبل البعثة وفيه ما فيه إذ يظهر من تتبع أن الخلاف قبل ورود الشرع، ومن ثم لم يجعلوا رفع الإباحة الأصلية نسخاً لعدم التحريم كلامهم، أنه (، (هي خطاب الشرع، فتدبر، كذا في «الحاشية». ولنقل في تقرير الحق، فلنمهد مقدمة: أولاً: لم يمر على إنسان زمان لم يبعث إليه فيه الله رسولاً مع دين، لأن شرع آدم عليه السلام كان باقياً إلى مجيء نوح وشريعته إلى إبراهيم وكانت شريعته عامة للكل، فمن انتسخت في حقه فقد قام شرع غیره ،مقامها كشرع موسى وعيسى في حق بني إسرائيل وبقي في حق غيره، كما كان إلى ورود شريعتنا الحقة الباقية إلى يوم القيامة، ويدل عليه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إلا خلا فيها نذير دار این [فاطر: ٢٤] وقوله تعالى : أَيَحْسَبُ الإِنسَنُ أَن يُتْرَكَ سُدى ) [القيامة : ٣٦] وإذا تمهد هذا فنقول : فحينئذ لا يتأتى خلاف في زمان من أزمنة وجود الإنسان أصلاً، ولا يتأتى الحكم بالإباحة مطلقاً، ولا بالتحريم مطلقاً كيف وفي كل زمان شريعة فيها تحريم بعض الأشياء وإيجابه وإباحته وغير ذلك، فإذن ليس الخلاف إلا في زمان الفترة الذي اندرست فيه الشريعة بتقصير من قبلهم وحاصله أن الذين جاؤوا بعد اندراس الشريعة وجهل الأحكام، فإما جهلهم هذا يكون عذراً فيعامل مع الأفعال كلها معاملة المباح، أعني لا يؤاخذ بالفعل ولا بالترك كما في «المباح ، وذهب إليه أكثر الحنفية والشافعية وسموه إباحة أصلية، وهذا هو مراد الإمام فخر الإسلام بقوله : ولسنا نقول بهذا الأصل أي بكون التحريم ناسخاً للإباحة الأصلية بوضع أن البشر لم يتركوا سدى في شيء من الأزمان وإنما هذا أي القول بالإباحة الأصلية بناء على زمان الفترة قبل شريعتنا، يعني إذ لا إباحة حقيقة، بل بمعنى نفي الحرج، ولعل المراد الأفعال ما عدا الكفر ونحوه، فإن حرمتهما في كل شرع بين ظهوراً تاماً، وإما لا يكون عذراً، فحينئذ لا بد من القول بتحريم الأشياء كلها لاختلاط الحلال بالحرام، للجهل بالتعيين، فحرمت احتياطاً، فصار الأصل التحريم كما عند غيرهم، ولعلهم أرادوا ما سوى الأشياء الضرورية، ومزعوم صدر الإسلام أن تحريم الأنفس أصل ثابت في كل شرع لم ينسخ قط فحكم به، وأما غيرها فقد جهلت وهذا الجهل عذر، ولذا فصل، ولعل هذا تفسير منه لقول الحنفية والشافعية وفي كلام المصنف إشارة إليه أيضاً هذا ما عند هذا العبد، ولعل الله يحدث بعد ذلك أمراً (أما المعتزلة فقسموا الأفعال الاختيارية وهي التي يمكن البقاء والتعيش بدونها، كأكل الفاكهة (مثلاً) والاضطرارية التي سواها واجبة أو مباحة عندهم (إلى ما يدرك فيه جهة محسنة حسناً شديداً يورث تركه قبحاً ، وذماً ، أو ضعيفاً بحيث يثاب على الفعل ولا يعاقب بالترك، أو أضعف منه بحيث يأمن العقاب بالفعل والترك (أو مقبحة) قبحاً شديداً، بحيث
If it is said: According to what was mentioned, how can the dispute that the original [ruling] is permissibility or prohibition be valid? He answered: As for the transmitted dispute among Ahl al-Haqq that the original of actions is permissibility, as is the choice of most Hanafis and Shafi'is, or its original is prohibition, as held by others, and Sadr al-Islam said: The original is permissibility in wealth and prohibition in lives. So taking life, cutting a limb, causing pain by beating, and engaging in private parts remain on prohibition, except what is excluded by evidence like retaliation and marriage. Rather, this dispute occurred after the Law by textual evidence; those evidences indicated that what does not have evidence [of prohibition] is permitted by the indication of another evidence, as according to most Hanafis and Shafi'is, or prohibited by the indication of another evidence, as according to others. This does not contradict the absence of restriction before prophethood. But there is something in it, for it appears from investigation that the dispute is before the advent of the Law. Hence they did not consider the lifting of original permissibility as abrogation due to the absence of prohibition in their statement. It is the address of the Law, so reflect. This is in the gloss. Let us convey in establishing the truth; let us lay down a preface: First, there has not passed upon a human a time in which Allah did not send a messenger to him with a religion, because the Law of Adam (peace be upon him) remained until the coming of Noah, and his law until Abraham, and his law was general for all. Whose law was abrogated, another law took its place, like the law of Moses and Jesus for the Children of Israel, and it remained for others, until the arrival of our true law that remains until the Day of Judgment. This is indicated by His saying: 'And there was no nation except that a warner passed within it' (Surah Fatir: 24) and His saying: 'Does man think that he will be left neglected?' (Surah al-Qiyamah: 36). When this is established, we say: Then, no dispute occurs at any time of human existence at all, nor does the judgment of absolute permissibility or absolute prohibition arise. How can it, when in every time there is a law containing prohibition of some things, obligation of some, permission of some, etc.? So the dispute is only about the period of fatra (gap) in which the law was obliterated due to negligence from their predecessors. The summary: those who came after the obliteration of the law and ignorance of rulings, either their ignorance is an excuse; then all actions are treated as permissible, meaning they are not taken to task for action or omission, as in the concept of permissible (mubah). This is the view of most Hanafis and Shafi'is, and they called it original permissibility (ibahah asliyyah). This is what Imam Fakhr al-Islam meant by his saying: 'We do not say this principle' meaning that prohibition abrogates original permissibility, considering that humans were not left neglected in any age; rather, this saying of original permissibility is based on the period of fatra before our law, meaning there is no real permissibility, rather it is in the sense of negation of restriction. Perhaps the intended actions are except disbelief and the like, for their prohibition in every law is clearly manifest. Or ignorance is not an excuse; then it is necessary to say that all things are prohibited due to the mixing of lawful and unlawful, due to ignorance of specification, so they are declared prohibited as a precaution. Hence the original becomes prohibition, as according to others. Perhaps they intended other than the necessary things. The claim of Sadr al-Islam that the prohibition of lives is an established principle in every law, never abrogated, so he ruled accordingly. As for other than that, they are ignorant, and this ignorance is an excuse, so he made a distinction. Perhaps this is his interpretation of the saying of the Hanafis and Shafi'is; and in the author's words there is an indication of this. This is what is with this servant; perhaps Allah will bring about after that an affair. As for the Mu'tazila, they divided voluntary actions, which are those without which one can survive, like eating fruit, for example, and necessary actions which are other than these, which are obligatory or permissible according to them, into: those in which a beneficial aspect is perceived, either severe goodness causing its omission to be ugly and blameworthy, or weak such that one is rewarded for the act but not punished for omission, or weaker such that one is secure from punishment whether one does it or leaves it; or a harmful aspect causing severe evil, such that...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage resolves the well-known dispute among Ahl al-Haqq (Sunni schools) whether the original ruling for human actions is permissibility (ibahah) or prohibition (hazr). The author first clarifies that this dispute is not about all times, but specifically about the period of fatra — the interval when prophetic teachings have been lost. He introduces a crucial premise: no epoch of human history has been without a prophet and divine law, citing the continuity from Adam through Noah, Abraham, Moses, Jesus, and finally the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon them all). This is supported by Quranic verses: Surah Fatir (35:24) and Surah al-Qiyamah (75:36). Therefore, the dispute cannot be about human existence overall, because every era had a law. It can only be about the fatra, when a previously revealed law has been forgotten or corrupted. After this, he explains: those living in fatra who are ignorant of the law — if their ignorance is excusable, all actions are treated as permissible in the sense that there is no blame (la haraj); this is called 'original permissibility' (ibahah asliyyah), the view of most Hanafis and Shafi'is. If their ignorance is not excusable (because they should have deduced the law by reason), then all actions are presumptively prohibited as a precaution (ihtiyatan) due to the confusion of the lawful and unlawful. Sadr al-Islam's differentiation: prohibition of harming lives is an eternal principle never abrogated; for other matters, the original may be permissibility. Finally, the passage presents the Mu'tazilite classification of actions into voluntary vs. necessary, and then within voluntary, they divide based on the strength of the perceived benefit or harm: actions with severe goodness (obligatory), weak goodness (recommended or permissible), or severe evil (prohibited). The text cuts off while explaining the latter category.

Key Terms
الإباحة الأصلية (al-ibahah al-asliyyah): The absence of any legal ruling of prohibition or obligation; it means no blame attaches to action or omission, used for the fatra period.
زمن الفترة (zaman al-fatra): The period between two prophets when the previous law has been lost or corrupted and no new prophet has appeared.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation; here it is stated that lifting original permissibility is not considered naskh because there was no prior prohibition to remove.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the dispute between those who say original permissibility and those who say original prohibition?
A) It is a dispute about the time before any prophet was sent to humanity.
B) It is about the period of fatra when a previous law has been lost.
C) It is about actions that reason can judge.
D) It is about the actions of prophets themselves.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on asl al-af'al is resolved by restricting its application to the period of fatra, where ignorance may be an excuse.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
The Status of Actions Before Revelation: Three Opinions Analysed
Presenting and analyzing three opinions on pre-revelation rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on pre-revelation rulings (ibaha, hazr, tawaqquf), evaluate the objections against the first two, and explain the Hanafi reasoning that favours tawaqquf.

Core Idea
Before revelation, actions have a ruling known only to Allah; we cannot assume a blanket permission or prohibition.

In this passage, the commentator presents a classic usul debate on the status of human actions before divine law was revealed. Three views—permissibility (ibaha), prohibition (hazr), and suspension (tawaqquf)—each have distinct epistemological foundations. The text dissects the weaknesses of the first two, especially the risk of contradicting an unknown actual ruling, and ultimately defends tawaqquf as the most logically coherent position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the text to find the three opinions (ibaha, hazr, tawaqquf) and note the justification given for each. The text says: 'لهم فيه قبل الشرع ثلاثة أقوال : الإباحة ... الحظر ... التوقف'.
Each position is based on a rational argument about the purpose of creation and divine permission.
2
Understand the objection against ibaha/hazr
The commentator argues that claiming a blanket ruling (ibaha or hazr) for all actions leads to a logical contradiction: if the actual ruling in divine knowledge for a particular act is different (e.g., obligation), then the act would be characterized by two opposite rulings simultaneously.
The key phrase: 'يلزم جواز اتصاف الفعل بحكمين متضادين'.
3
Analyse the defence of tawaqquf
Tawaqquf avoids this contradiction because it does not claim knowledge of the specific ruling; it only acknowledges that a specific ruling exists. The text distinguishes between general knowledge (of a cause) and specific knowledge (of the exact ruling for each act).
The commentator clarifies that suspension in specificity does not negate the possibility of a general ruling (like ijtihad) that is tentative.
4
Trace the Hanafi response to objections
The text indicates that the advocates of ibaha/hazr might appeal to a general evidence (dalil ijmaili) that yields a blanket ruling, which they treat as a ijtihadi ruling—subject to error—until revelation clarifies. However, the commentator finds this insufficient because it still allows for two contradictory rulings in reality.
The final reconciliation: the real assumption is lack of specific 'illa (cause) for each act, which does not preclude deriving a specific ruling for an act via deduction from a universal principle.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under ibaha, one can freely act before revelation; under hazr, one must refrain; under tawaqquf, one suspends judgment and avoids acting, but may act based on general principles. This affects how we view the accountability of people before Islam.
The text does not explicitly state the thamarah, but you can infer it from the logical stances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأحكام (٤٣) يعاقب على الفعل، أو ضعيفاً لا يوجب الحرج، بل ترك الأولوية (فينقسم إلى الأقسام الخمسة المشهورة من الوجوب والندب والإباحة والتحريم والكراهة وإلى ما ليس كذلك أي لم يدرك فيه جهة محسنة أو مقبحة ولهم فيه قبل الشرع ثلاثة أقوال : الإباحة تحصيلاً لحكمة الخلق دفعاً للعبث) يعني لو لم يكن مباحاً فات فائدة الخلق التي هي انتفاع العبد فصار عبثاً (وربما يمنع الاستلزام) أي استلزام عدم الإباحة فوات فائدة الخلق لجواز أن تكون الفائدة الابتلاء بإيجاب الاجتناب والحظر لئلا يلزم التصرف في ملك الغير وهو الخالق (من غير إذنه وقد مر ) مع ما فيه ولا يرد عليهما أنه كيف يقال بالإباحة والحظر العقليين، وقد فرض أن لا حكم له أي للعقل (فيه) فالقول بهما مع هذا الفرض جمع بين المتنافيين، وذلك لأن الفرض أن لا علم بعلة الحكم تفصيلاً) أي في فعل فعل ولا ينافي ذلك العلم إجمالاً) لعلة شاملة لجملة الأفعال (أقول يرد عليهما أنه يلزم حينئذٍ (جواز اتصاف الفعل بحكمين متضادين في نفس الأمر فإن فرض عدم العلم بالحكم في فعل يجوز أن يكون الوجوب مثلاً، والآن أثبتم الإباحة أو الحظر (ولا ينفع حينئذ الإجمال) والتفصيل اللذان ذكرا في الجواب (لأن اختلاف العلة لا يرفع التناقض وها هنا الإجمال في علة معرفة الحكم لا في محل الحكم (فتأمل) فإنه يمكن الجواب بأن هذا الحكم الإجمالي كالحكم الاجتهادي الخطأ فيجب العمل به إلى أن تطلع شمس الحقيقة بتحقق البعثة فلا يلزم جواز الاتصاف أصلاً كذا في «الحاشية». ويمكن توجيه الجواب المشهور بأنهم لا يقولون بعدم علم الحكم في خصوص فعل فعل، وتجويز أن يكون غير الإباحة والحظر حتى يلزم من إثباتهما ولو بالدليل الإجمالي اجتماع المتنافيين، بل المقصود عدم العلم الحاصل بدليل دليل مخصوص مخصوص بكل فعل فعمدوا إلى دليل إجمالي شامل لكل فعل فوجدوه حاكماً بالإباحة أو الحظر، فحينئذ لا تناقض فتدبر (الثالث : التوقف) في الحكم بشيء من الأحكام (لأن ثمة حكماً معيناً من الخمسة، ولا يدرى أيها واقع) فيتوقف (أقول : هذا يقتضي الوقف في الخصوصية) أي الحكم في مخصوص لمخصوص (ولا ينافي ذلك الحكم) إجمالاً في كل فعل فتدبر وهذا شيء عجاب، فإن المصنف أورد على الأولين بعدم كفاية الإجمال، والتفصيل في عدم المنافاة، وها هنا حكم بعدم المنافاة لأجل الإجمال والتفصيل، إلا أن يقال : المراد أن الوقف في الخصوص لا المخصوصة لكل فعل فعل فاحتمل أن يكون في البعض جهة محسنة موجبة معلومة عند الله تعالى فيكون الحكم بحسبها الوجوب، ويرد الشرع بحسبها أن ورد كما ورد في صوم آخر رمضان المبارك، فيجتمع فيه الوجوب مع الحرمة أو الإباحة، ولا ينفع الإجمال والتفصيل، وبهذا ظهر اندفاع تقرير الجواب المذكور سابقاً، والمقصود من الإيراد هنا أن المفروض إنما هو عدم معرفة العلة المخصوصة المعينة في كل فعل فعل، ولا ينافي معرفة حكم مخصوص بفعل مطابقاً لما في نفس الأمر بالاستنباط على ضابطة كلية، لا أن يعلم حكم واحد شامل للكل كما في المذهبين الأولين حتى يلزم الخلف فتدبر وأنصف.
The second article on rulings: (43) He is punished for the action, or weakly without causing distress, but leaving the preferable. Thus it divides into the five well-known categories of obligation, recommendation, permission, prohibition, and dislike, and into what is not like that, i.e., where no aspect of good or bad is perceived. For them, before the law, there are three statements: permission, to achieve the wisdom of creation and repel futility; meaning if it were not permissible, the benefit of creation, which is the servant's benefit, would be lost, making it futile. And sometimes the implication is denied, i.e., the implication that lack of permission causes loss of the benefit of creation, because it is possible that the benefit is testing through requiring avoidance and prohibition, so as not to necessitate acting in the property of another, which is the Creator, without His permission. And it has passed with what is in it. And it is not objected to them that how can rational permission and prohibition be said when it is assumed that there is no ruling for reason in it? For saying both with this assumption is combining contradictories. That is because the assumption is that there is no detailed knowledge of the cause of the ruling in any particular act, but that does not contradict general knowledge of a cause encompassing all acts. I say: an objection is made against them that it then necessitates the permissibility of an act being described by two contradictory rulings in themselves. For if we assume lack of knowledge of the ruling in an act, it could be obligation, for example, and now you establish permission or prohibition. And general and detailed knowledge mentioned in the answer do not benefit then, because difference in cause does not remove contradiction, and here the generality is in the cause of knowing the ruling, not in the locus of the ruling. So contemplate. For it is possible to answer that this general ruling is like an erroneous ijtihadi ruling, so it must be acted upon until the sun of reality rises with the confirmation of the mission, so the permissibility of being described [by opposites] does not arise at all. Thus in the gloss. And the well-known answer can be directed: they do not say that there is no knowledge of the ruling in particular acts, and they do not deem it possible that it is other than permission or prohibition, so that from their establishment, even by general evidence, would necessitate combining contradictories. Rather, the intended meaning is the absence of knowledge obtained through a specific evidence specific to each act, so they resorted to a general evidence encompassing all acts and found it ruling by permission or prohibition. Then there is no contradiction, so ponder. The third: suspension in ruling anything from the rulings, because there is a specific ruling from the five, and it is not known which one applies, so he suspends. I say: this necessitates suspension in specificity, i.e., the ruling in a specific for a specific, and that does not contradict the ruling in general for every act, so ponder. And this is a wonder, for the author objected to the first two with the insufficiency of generality and detail in the absence of contradiction, and here he ruled with the absence of contradiction due to generality and detail, unless it is said that the intended meaning is that suspension in the specific does not contradict the general ruling, even if in the manner of error in ijtihad. And the most correct in the explanation is to say: the insufficiency of generality and detail there is because the adherents of the first two schools ruled by permission or prohibition absolutely, and it is assumed that there is no knowledge of the specific aspect for each act. So it is possible that in some acts there is an improving aspect necessitating [obligation], known to God, so the ruling according to it would be obligation, and the law may come according to it, as came in fasting the last day of Ramadan the blessed. So obligation would combine with prohibition or permission, and generality and detail do not benefit. And by this the dismissal of the previously mentioned answer becomes apparent. And the purpose of the objection here is that the assumption is only the lack of knowledge of the specific designated cause for each act, and that does not contradict knowledge of a specific ruling for an act corresponding to what is in reality through deduction on a universal principle, not that a single ruling encompassing all is known, as in the first two schools, so that inconsistency would arise. So ponder and be fair.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a dispute among usul scholars about the status of actions before revelation (shar). The author presents three positions: (1) ibaha (permissibility) — actions are inherently permissible to avoid futility in creation; (2) hazr (prohibition) — actions are prohibited because they are acts in the property of the Creator without permission; (3) tawaqquf (suspension) — we withhold judgment because a specific ruling exists but is unknown. The commentator objects to the first two positions by arguing that they lead to contradiction: if reason cannot know the detailed ruling, how can it claim permissibility or prohibition for all actions? He defends tawaqquf, but also provides possible reconciliations for the first two positions, such as appealing to a general evidence or treating the ruling as tentative (like ijtihad). Ultimately, the text clarifies that the real issue is the lack of knowledge of the specific 'illa (cause) for each act, which does not preclude deriving a specific ruling through deduction from a universal principle—this is distinct from claiming a blanket ruling for all acts. The discussion illustrates the Hanafi usul method of careful analysis of the epistemological grounds for rulings.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibāḥa): The opinion that before revelation, actions are inherently permissible (mubah) as the default ruling.
حظر (ḥaẓr): The opinion that before revelation, actions are inherently prohibited (haram) because they involve the property of the Creator without permission.
توقف (tawaqquf): The opinion that before revelation, we suspend judgment on the ruling of actions; a specific ruling exists but is unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the main objection against the first two opinions (ibaha and hazr) regarding pre-revelation rulings?
A) They contradict the principle that reason cannot know rulings.
B) They lead to the possibility of an act being described by two contradictory rulings in reality.
C) They are based on weak evidence from revelation.
D) They ignore the fact that actions are inherently neutral.

Key Takeaway
Before revelation, actions have a specific ruling known to Allah; tawaqquf avoids the logical pitfalls of assuming a blanket permissibility or prohibition.

Hanafi Classification of Hasan (Good) Acts: Intrinsic vs Extrinsic Goodness
Classifying hasan (good) acts into hasan li nafsihi and hasan li ghayrihi with subdivisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hasan li nafsihi and hasan li ghayrihi, identify the subdivisions of each, and apply the classification to fiqh examples.

Core Idea
Goodness in actions is either intrinsic or acquired through an external intermediary.

The Hanafis, through induction, classified all good actions into two fundamental categories: those that are good in themselves (ḥasan li nafsihi) and those that are good due to an external factor (ḥasan li ghayrihi). This division is not merely theoretical; it helps us understand why certain acts, like faith, are always good, while others, like prayer, can lose their apparent goodness in specific circumstances, and why acts like jihad, which involve killing, are considered good in the Shari‘a. In this lesson, we will unpack this taxonomy and explore its subcategories with concrete examples from worship and social transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Primary Division
The text begins by dividing actions that are ḥasan (good) into two main types: (1) ḥasan li nafsihi (good in itself) and (2) ḥasan li ghayrihi (good due to another). Note that this classification is based on the source of goodness—whether it is intrinsic or derived.
This is a unique Hanafi contribution to uṣūl al-fiqh.
2
Analyze the Subcategories of Ḥasan li Nafsihi
Within ḥasan li nafsihi, the author distinguishes two subtypes: (a) those whose goodness never ceases, like īmān (faith), because it is necessitated by the essence without any additional condition; (b) those whose goodness can cease due to a condition, like ṣalāh (prayer), which is good but may become 'bad' in disliked times because of a prevailing accidental badness (qabḥ).
The author then addresses a potential objection: the intrinsic goodness of prayer remains even in disliked times; only the accidental badness overcomes. A stronger proof is the case of a menstruating woman, whose prayer is intrinsically bad.
3
Understand the Objection and Refinement
Consider the objection: does prayer in disliked times lose its goodness? The author argues that it does not lose intrinsic goodness; rather, accidental badness overcomes. To prove true cessation, the author offers the example of a menstruating woman: her prayer is bad in itself, so it is not obligatory and no makeup is required. This contrasts with faith, which never ceases from a believer (though it may be absent from children and the insane due to incapacity, not badness).
This step sharpens the definition of 'cessation of goodness'.
4
Examine the Subcategories of Ḥasan li Ghayrihi
The second main division, ḥasan li ghayrihi, is further split: (a) acts where the intermediary (wāsiṭa) is not volitional for the servant, so the act becomes good purely as worship—examples: zakāt, ṣawm, ḥajj, where the intermediary (need of the poor, rebellious soul, the House) is extrinsic; (b) acts where the intermediary is volitional and fulfilled through the act—like jihād, ḥadd punishments, and janāzah prayer. These acts are intrinsically neutral or bad but become good because they serve a good end.
The author notes a possible objection: perhaps jihād is good in itself, but the classification still stands for other examples.
5
Apply to Fiqh Examples
Practice identifying the category of various acts: Is the goodness intrinsic or extrinsic? For example, giving charity is ḥasan li nafsihi if done sincerely, but becomes ḥasan li ghayrihi if done to show off? No, that would be bad. But the text focuses on acts like jihād: killing disbelievers is not intrinsically good but becomes good because it demolishes disbelief. This step solidifies understanding.
Refer to the key terms: wāsiṭa fī al-thubūt vs. wāsiṭa fī al-‘urūḍ.

بشرط زائد أو يقبل) حسنه السقوط لأجل اقتضاء الحسن بشرط زائد ممكن الزوال ( كالصلاة) وقد منعت في الأوقات المكروهة) فسقط حسنها في هذه الأوقات وللمناقش أن يناقش بأنه لم يسقط حسنها الذاتي في هذه الأوقات، بل غلب القبح العارض، وهو لا ينافي بقاء الحسن الذاتي، ولذا لو أدى الصلاة فيها كانت صحيحة كالنفل وعصر اليوم وعدم جواز صلاة الصبح لأمر آخر لا لبطلان الحسن الذاتي، فالأولى أن يستدل بسقوطها عن الحائض، فإن طاغية اقتضت أن يكون قهرها بمنع شهواتها الثلاث حسناً وهو الصوم، والبيت اقتضت أن يكون تعظيمها على الوجه المخصوص حسناً، وهذه كلها عبادات خالصة لله تعالى لا دخل لهذه الوسائط في العبادة (أو غير ملحق بالأول بعبادة الجماد كالثالث، لكنها حسنت (بواسطة) هدم (الكفر) وإعلاء كلمة الله وهو حسن بالذات، وبحسنه حسن تعذيب الكفار بالقتل والنهب وبه يتأدى هدم الكفر وإعلاء الكلمة الإلهية (و) بواسطة (المعصية) أي الزجر عليها لينزجر الناس عنها، والزجر عن المعصية حسن لنفسه وبحسنه حسن تعذيب العباد الفساق بإقامة الحدود، وهذا الزجر يتأدى بنفس إقامة الحدود (و) بواسطة تعظيم إسلام الميت فإن تعظيمه كان حسناً بنفسه، وبحسنه A note: The mukallaf (the Hanafis divided the good action, through induction, into what is good in itself, i.e., without an intermediary in its establishment. So either its goodness does not accept cessation, because it is necessitated by the essence without considering any other condition, like faith, for its goodness is required without an additional condition; or its goodness accepts cessation because the goodness is necessitated by a condition that may cease, like prayer, which is prohibited in disliked times, so its goodness ceases in those times. But a challenger may argue that its intrinsic goodness does not cease in those times; rather, the accidental badness overcomes, which does not negate the persistence of intrinsic goodness. Hence, if one performs the prayer at those times, it is valid, like supererogatory prayer and the afternoon prayer of the same day. The impermissibility of the dawn prayer is due to another matter, not the invalidity of intrinsic goodness. So it is better to prove cessation by the menstruating woman, for her prayer is bad in itself, therefore it is not obligatory upon her, and thus she does not have to make it up. If you say: faith is also absent from the non-rational child and the insane, so it is like prayer? I say: it ceases only due to impossibility, not due to badness and cessation of its goodness. And the intended meaning by 'does not cease' is that it does not cease from the believers; faith never ceases from them, unlike prayer, because the menstruating woman is obligated, so understand. And into what is good due to another, by that other being an intermediary in the establishment. And it either joins the first, i.e., what is good in itself, because this intermediary is not an intermediary in the accidental occurrence, so the goodness is accidental to the act itself. And it, i.e., what joins the first, is only in that which the servant has no choice in, so this intermediary is not a volitional act fit to be described as good, so it is only an intermediary in the establishment of goodness, like zakat, fasting, and hajj: they were legislated in consideration of need, the soul, and the House. And the servant has no choice in these. This: the need of the poor necessitated that its fulfillment from the rich out of their surplus wealth is good, i.e., zakat; the soul when it is rebellious necessitated that its subjugation by preventing its three desires is good, i.e., fasting; and the House necessitated that its glorification in the specific manner is good. And all these are pure acts of worship for God, these intermediaries have no part in the worship. Or it does not join the first because the other is an intermediary in the accidental occurrence. This section divides into two parts: first, that this other is fulfilled by performing this good, like jihad, the hadd punishment, and janazah prayer. For they are in themselves torture of the servants of God, as in the first two, or imitation of the worship of inanimate objects, as in the third, but they are made good through the intermediary of demolishing disbelief and raising the word of God, which is good in itself, and by its goodness the torture of disbelievers by killing and plundering becomes good, and by it the demolition of disbelief and raising the divine word is fulfilled. And through the intermediary of disobedience, i.e., deterring from it so that people refrain from it, and deterring from disobedience is good in itself, and by its goodness the torture of sinful servants by establishing the hadd punishments becomes good, and this deterrence is fulfilled by the very establishment of the hadd. And through the intermediary of honoring the Islam of the deceased, for honoring him was good in itself, and by its goodness supplication becomes good. This construction... and it might be objected that torturing disbelievers and fighting them has no badness in it, rather it could be good in itself. And...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a classification of good acts (hasan) according to the Hanafi school, based on induction (istiqra). The Hanafis divide hasan into two main categories: (1) hasan li nafsihi (good in itself) — acts whose goodness is intrinsic, not dependent on an external factor. This type is subdivided into: (a) those whose goodness never ceases, like iman (faith), because its goodness flows from the essence of the act itself without any extra condition; (b) those whose goodness can cease because it depends on a condition that may disappear, like prayer, which is good but its goodness is suspended in disliked times (e.g., after 'Asr) due to a prevailing badness (qabh), though the intrinsic goodness remains (as the prayer is still valid if performed). The commentator suggests a better proof of cessation is the case of a menstruating woman: her prayer is intrinsically bad, so it is not obligatory and no makeup is required. Faith, however, never ceases from a believer, though it may be absent from children and the insane due to incapacity, not due to badness. (2) hasan li ghayrihi (good due to another) — acts that are good not in themselves but because of an external factor. This type is further divided into: (a) acts whose goodness derives from an intermediary that is not volitional for the servant, like zakat, fasting, and hajj, where the intermediary (e.g., the need of the poor) is extrinsic but the act becomes good as a pure act of worship; (b) acts whose goodness derives from an intermediary that is volitional and the intermediary is fulfilled through the act, such as jihad (which involves killing disbelievers), hadd punishments (which involve harming people), and janazah prayer (which involves supplication for the deceased). These acts are intrinsically neutral or even bad (torture, imitation of inanimate worship), but they become good because they serve a good end: demolishing disbelief, deterring sin, or honoring a Muslim. The commentator ends by noting a possible objection: killing disbelievers might be considered good in itself, not requiring an intermediary.

Key Terms
حسن لنفسه (ḥasan li nafsihi): An act that is good in itself, without any external intermediary. Its goodness is intrinsic.
حسن لغيره (ḥasan li ghayrihi): An act that is good due to an external factor, either as an intermediary in establishment or in accidental occurrence.
واسطة في الثبوت (wāsiṭa fī al-thubūt): An intermediary that is instrumental in establishing the goodness of the act, where the act itself does not have choice over that intermediary.
واسطة في العروض (wāsiṭa fī al-ʿurūḍ): An intermediary that is the locus for the accidental attribution of goodness, where the act's goodness is realized through that intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī classification, why is the prayer of a menstruating woman considered to have ceased in goodness (sāqiṭ al-ḥusn)?
A) Because menstruation is a disliked time similar to the disliked times for prayer.
B) Because her prayer is intrinsically bad (qabīḥ li dhātihā) and therefore not obligatory.
C) Because she is not a mukallaf during menstruation.
D) Because prayer is not legislated for women.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafī classification of ḥasan into li nafsihi and li ghayrihi, with further subdivisions, provides a nuanced framework for understanding the moral and legal value of actions, especially those involving prima facie negative aspects like jihād and ḥadd punishments.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
15 min
The Hanafi Taxonomy of Good and Bad Actions
Classification of actions by moral quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any action according to the Hanafi taxonomy of hasan and qabih, distinguishing intrinsic from acquired moral qualities and explain the Hanafi response to the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold that actions have intrinsic goodness or badness, not merely by divine command.

This lesson explores the Hanafi division of actions into hasan (good) and qabih (bad) as presented in Fawatih al-Rahamut. The passage uses induction (istiqra) to establish a sophisticated taxonomy: actions can be good or bad in themselves (li nafsihi) or for another (li ghayrihi), with further subdivisions based on whether the quality is permanent or contingent. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping Hanafi usul al-fiqh and its departure from Ash'ari occasionalism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Read the beginning of the passage where the Hanafis divide hasan into hasan li nafsihi and hasan li ghayrihi. Note that the classification is based on induction (istiqra).
2
Subdivide hasan li nafsihi
Within hasan li nafsihi, identify two subtypes: those that never lose their goodness (like iman) and those that can (like salat). Understand the reasoning: iman's goodness is intrinsic without condition, while salat's goodness depends on a condition that can cease.
The author clarifies that for salat, the intrinsic goodness persists but is overcome by accidental badness; this is a key nuance.
3
Analyze hasan li ghayrihi
This category is further divided based on whether the intermediary is in thubut (establishment) or 'urud (occurrence). For thubut, the intermediary is outside the servant's choice (e.g., need for zakat). For 'urud, the intermediary is a goal accomplished by the act (e.g., jihad destroys disbelief) or not accomplished (e.g., walking to Jumu'ah).
Examples include zakat, fasting, Hajj for thubut; jihad, hadd, funeral prayer for 'urud (accomplished); walking to Jumu'ah for non-accomplished.
4
Apply the same pattern to qabih
The author states that the categories of qabih follow the same pattern: qabih li nafsihi (e.g., shirk, zina) that never loses badness; qabih li nafsihi that can lose badness (e.g., eating carrion in necessity); qabih li ghayrihi (accomplished, e.g., fasting on Eid; non-accomplished, e.g., selling during adhan).
Note the special case of ghasp (usurpation) which is qabih li ghayrihi but the intermediary is disregarded, so it becomes qabih li nafsihi.
5
Understand the Hanafi-Ash'ari dispute
The passage refutes the Ash'ari view that goodness is solely by command. The Hanafi response: obeying Allah is good in itself (shukr al-mun'im), and commanded acts have an additional goodness without negating intrinsic goodness. The thamarah (practical consequence): if Allah commanded shirk, on Ash'ari view it would become good, which Hanafis deny.
This is a central ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh with major theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه المكلف، (الحنفية قسموا الفعل الحسن (بالاستقراء إلى ما هو حسن لنفسه) أي من غير واسطة في الثبوت فإما لا يقبل) حسنه (السقوط) لأجل كونه مقتضى الذات بلا اعتبار أمر آخر (كالإيمان) فإنه يقتضي حسنه لا بشرط زائد أو يقبل) حسنه السقوط لأجل اقتضاء الحسن بشرط زائد ممكن الزوال ( كالصلاة) وقد منعت في الأوقات المكروهة) فسقط حسنها في هذه الأوقات وللمناقش أن يناقش بأنه لم يسقط حسنها الذاتي في هذه الأوقات، بل غلب القبح العارض، وهو لا ينافي بقاء الحسن الذاتي، ولذا لو أدى الصلاة فيها كانت صحيحة كالنفل وعصر اليوم وعدم جواز صلاة الصبح لأمر آخر لا لبطلان الحسن الذاتي، فالأولى أن يستدل بسقوطها عن الحائض، فإن عدم السقوط عن والإيمان لم يسقط عنه بحال بخلاف الصلاة، لأن الحائض مكلفة فافهم (وإلى ما هو حسن (لغيره) بأن يكون هذا الغير واسطة في الثبوت وهو إما ملحق بالأول) أي بما هو حسن لكون هذه الواسطة واسطة لا في العروض فالحسن عارض للفعل بالذات (وهو) أي الملحق بالأول، إنما يكون (فيما) أي الغير الذي هو الواسطة (لا) اختيار للعبد فيه فحينئذ لا يكون هذا الغير فعلاً اختيارياً صالحاً لأن يتصف بالحسن، فيكون واسطة في ثبوت الحسن فقط كالزكاة والصوم والحج شرعت نظراً إلى الحاجة والنفس والبيت) ولا اختيار للعبد فيه، هذا فحاجة الفقير اقتضت أن يكون دفعها من الأغنياء من قليل فاضل مالهم حسناً وهو الزكاة، والنفس لما كانت طاغية اقتضت أن يكون قهرها بمنع شهواتها الثلاث حسناً وهو الصوم، والبيت اقتضت أن يكون تعظيمها على الوجه المخصوص حسناً، وهذه كلها عبادات خالصة لله تعالى لا دخل لهذه الوسائط في العبادة (أو غير ملحق بالأول لكون الغير واسطة في العروض، وهذا القسم منقسم إلى قسمين : الأول : أن يكون هذا الغير يتأدى بأداء هذا الحسن كالجهاد والحد وصلاة الجنازة فإنها في أنفسها تعذيب عباد الله تعالى كما في الأولين، أو التشبه بعبادة الجماد كالثالث، لكنها حسنت (بواسطة) هدم (الكفر) وإعلاء كلمة الله وهو حسن بالذات، وبحسنه حسن تعذيب الكفار بالقتل والنهب وبه يتأدى هدم الكفر وإعلاء الكلمة الإلهية (و) بواسطة (المعصية) أي الزجر عليها لينزجر الناس عنها، والزجر عن المعصية حسن لنفسه وبحسنه حسن تعذيب العباد الفساق بإقامة الحدود، وهذا الزجر يتأدى بنفس إقامة الحدود (و) بواسطة تعظيم إسلام الميت فإن تعظيمه كان حسناً بنفسه، وبحسنه حسن الدعاء، هذا الصنع من وربما يورد أن تعذيب الكفار والقتال معهم لا قبح فيه، بل يجوز كونه حسناً بالذات، وأي دليل على خلافه؟ نعم مطلق التعذيب لا حسن فيه، لكنه غير الجهاد وكذا صلاة الجنازة ذكر الله تعالى وعبادة له مع الدعاء، فيجوز أن يكون حسنه بالذات وليس شبيهاً بعبادة غير الله تعالى كما في «الحج»، نعم الكفر وإسلام الميت وسائط في الثبوت كالبيت في الحج، هذا : والظاهر أن الجهاد لا يصلح أن يلحق بالأول، ألا ترى أن القتال معهم يسقط بأدنى شبهة، والحسن بالذات لا يسقط عن الذمة بالشبهات بل يجب معها للاحتياط كما لا يخفى على الناظر في الفقه وأما صلاة الجنازة فلما سقط بفعل البعض علمنا أن ليس المقصود إتعاب البدن بذكر الله، بل قضاء حاجة الميت، فيكون حسنها لأجله، وبهذا يخرج الجواب عن الجهاد أيضاً فتدبر وأنصف والثاني: أن لا يتأدى هذا الغير بأداء هذا الحسن. كالسعي إلى الجمعة، فإنه حسن بحسن صلاة الجمعة ولا تتأدى بالسعي فقط، وربما يمثل بالوضوء، فإنه حسن بحسن الصلاة لأجل كونه شرطاً، وفيه شائبة من الخفاء فإن الوضوء بما هو طهارة حسن، وإن كان له حسن آخر من جهة حسن مشروطة، ألا ترى أن الشرع ندب الدوام على الطهارة، والمندوب حسن وليس ندبها لإقامة الصلاة، فإن من أوقات مندوبية الطهارة وقت الخطبة وسائر الأوقات المكروهة فتدبر وليعلم أن جميع المأمورات فيها حسن آخر ثبت بكونه مأموراً به، ولا ينافي الحسن الثابت قبل الأمر فالإيمان مع كونه حسناً كونه حسناً في نفسه حسن لكونه مأموراً به، ولا تظنن أن هذا يؤول إلى مذهب الأشعري من أن الحسن يثبت بالأمر، فإنا نقول : إن أداء مأمور الله حسن في نفسه فإنه من قبيل شكر المنعم، وأداء هذه العبادات من أفراد أداء المأمور به، فيكون حسنه بحسنه لا أن الشرع جعله حسناً وكان إتيانها وإتيان المنهيات في أنفسهما متساويين، وأعاذنا الله تعالى عن هذا القول وهكذا أقسام القبيح) فالقبيح قبيح لعينه لا يحتمل السقوط، كقبح الشرك وسائر العقائد الباطلة والزنا أو يحتمل السقوط، كأكل الميتة سقط قبحه في «المخمصة»، وقبيح لغيره يتأدى بارتكاب هذا القبيح، كصوم يوم العيد قبيح، لأجل كونه إعراضاً عن ضيافة الله تعالى، وبارتكاب الصوم يرتكب الإعراض، أو لا يتأدى، كالبيع وقت النداء قبيح لإفضائه إلى فوات الجمعة، وأما القبيح لغيره الذي يكون فيه واسطة في الثبوت مهدرة لم أر بيانه في كلام القوم، وإن كان فمثاله الغصب، فإنه إنما حرم لتعلق حق الغير، لكن هذه الواسطة مهدرة، فصار الغصب قبيحاً بالذات
Reminder: the obligated person. The Hanafis divided the good action (by induction) into what is good in itself, i.e., without an intermediary in its establishment: either it does not accept falling off, because it is a requirement of the essence without consideration of another command (like faith), for its goodness is necessitated without an additional condition; or it accepts falling off, because its goodness is necessitated by an additional condition that can cease (like prayer), and it has been prohibited in disliked times, so its goodness falls off in these times. However, one may object that its intrinsic goodness does not fall off in these times, rather the accidental badness overcomes, which does not negate the persistence of intrinsic goodness; hence if one prays in those times, it is valid, like supererogatory prayer and the afternoon prayer on the day of Jumu'ah, and the impermissibility of morning prayer is for another reason, not because of the nullification of intrinsic goodness. It is better to use as evidence its falling off from the menstruating woman, for her prayer is bad in itself, so it is not obligatory upon her, and thus no make-up is required. If you say: faith also falls off from the child, the non-rational, and the insane, so it is like prayer? I say: it falls off due to incapability, not due to badness and falling off of its goodness. What is meant by 'no falling off' is that it does not fall off from one who is obligated; faith never falls off from him, unlike prayer, because the menstruating woman is obligated, so understand. And (they divided it into) what is good for another, i.e., where this 'other' is an intermediary in the establishment. This is either annexed to the first, i.e., to what is good in itself, because this intermediary is not an intermediary in the occurrence, so the goodness is an attribute of the act essentially: it occurs in what the servant has no choice over; then this other is not a voluntary act worthy of being described as good, so it is only an intermediary in the establishment of goodness, like zakat, fasting, and Hajj, legislated with regard to need, the soul, and the House. The servant has no choice over this. So the need of the poor required that its fulfillment by the rich from the surplus of their wealth be good, which is zakat; the soul, being rebellious, required that its subjugation by preventing its three desires be good, which is fasting; and the House required that its glorification in a specific manner be good. All these are pure acts of worship for Allah; these intermediaries have no role in the worship. Or it is not annexed to the first, because the other is an intermediary in the occurrence. This section is divided into two parts: First, that this other is accomplished by performing this good, like jihad, the prescribed punishment, and the funeral prayer. For they are in themselves a punishment of Allah's servants (as in the first two) or imitation of worship of inanimate objects (as the third), but they become good through the intermediary of destroying disbelief and exalting the word of Allah, which is good in itself, and through its goodness, the punishment of disbelievers by killing and plunder becomes good, and by this the destruction of disbelief and exaltation of the divine word is accomplished; and through the intermediary of disobedience, i.e., deterrence from it so that people desist, and deterrence from disobedience is good in itself, and through its goodness, the punishment of disobedient servants by executing prescribed punishments becomes good, and this deterrence is accomplished by the very execution of the prescribed punishments; and through the intermediary of honoring the death of a Muslim, for honoring him is good in itself, and through its goodness, supplication becomes good. One might object that torturing disbelievers and fighting them has no badness; rather it may be good in itself, and what is the evidence to the contrary? Yes, absolute torture has no goodness, but this is not jihad. Similarly, the funeral prayer is a remembrance of Allah and worship of Him along with supplication, so it may be good in itself, and it is not similar to worship of other than Allah, as in Hajj. Indeed, disbelief and the death of a Muslim are intermediaries in the establishment, like the House in Hajj. However, it seems that jihad cannot be annexed to the first; do you not see that fighting them falls off with the slightest doubt, and the good in itself does not fall off from responsibility due to doubts, rather it becomes obligatory with caution, as is evident to one who looks into fiqh. As for the funeral prayer, since it falls off when performed by some, we know that the purpose is not to exhaust the body by remembrance of Allah, but to fulfill the need of the deceased, so its goodness is for his sake. By this, the objection regarding jihad is also answered. Reflect and be fair. Second: that this other is not accomplished by performing this good, like walking to Friday prayer, for it is good by the goodness of Friday prayer, but it is not accomplished by walking only. Perhaps one may exemplify with ablution, for it is good by the goodness of prayer because it is a condition; but there is an aspect of obscurity, for ablution as purification is good, although it has another goodness from the perspective of the goodness of that which is conditioned by it. Do you not see that the Law recommends persistence in purification, and the recommended is good, and it is not recommended for establishing prayer, for among the times when purification is recommended is during the sermon and other disliked times. So reflect. And let it be known that all commanded actions have another goodness established by being commanded, which does not negate the goodness established before the command. So faith, while being good in itself, is also good because it is commanded. Do not think that this leads to the Ash'ari doctrine that goodness is established by command; for we say: performing what Allah commands is good in itself, for it is a form of thanking the Benefactor, and performing these acts of worship are instances of performing what is commanded, so their goodness is by its goodness, not that the Law made them good while doing them and doing prohibited acts were equal in themselves. Allah protect us from this statement. Similarly go the categories of badness: badness is bad in itself, not accepting falling off, like the badness of polytheism and other false beliefs and adultery; or it accepts falling off, like eating carrion whose badness falls off in forced necessity. Badness for another that is accomplished by committing this badness, like fasting on the day of Eid is bad because it is turning away from Allah's hospitality, and by fasting one commits turning away; or it is not accomplished, like selling at the time of the call to prayer, which is bad because it leads to missing Friday prayer. As for badness for another where the intermediary in the establishment is disregarded, I have not seen its exposition in the words of the scholars; but if there is one, its example is usurpation, for it is forbidden due to the attachment of another's right, but this intermediary is disregarded, so usurpation becomes bad in itself.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a classification of actions into good (hasan) and bad (qabih) according to the Hanafi school, based on induction (istiqra). The author first divides good actions into those that are good in themselves (hasan li nafsihi) and those that are good for another (hasan li ghayrihi). For good-in-itself actions, there are two subtypes: those that never lose their goodness (like iman) because their goodness is intrinsic without needing any condition, and those that can lose their goodness (like salat) because their goodness depends on a condition that can cease. The author notes a potential objection: even in disliked times, the intrinsic goodness remains but is overcome by accidental badness; he prefers the example of a menstruating woman to show that her prayer is inherently bad (not good), thus not obligatory and not made up. For good-for-other actions, the author further distinguishes between cases where the intermediary (ghayr) is something the servant has no choice over (like the need of the poor, rebellious soul, or the House), making these acts purely worship despite the intermediary, and cases where the intermediary is in the occurrence (fi 'urud). The latter includes actions like jihad, hadd, and funeral prayer, which appear bad in themselves (inflicting punishment or imitating corpse worship) but become good through their connection to destroying disbelief, deterring sin, or honoring a Muslim's death. The author then discusses whether these can be considered good-in-itself, concluding that jihad cannot because it falls off with mere doubt, unlike truly inherent goodness, and funeral prayer also falls off when performed by others, indicating its goodness is for the deceased. The second subtype of good-for-other is when the intermediary is not accomplished by the action itself, like walking to Friday prayer (good) but not accomplishing Friday prayer itself. The author analogizes with wudu, which has intrinsic goodness as purification but also goodness as a condition for prayer, though its recommendation persists even outside prayer times. Finally, the author stresses that all commanded actions have an additional goodness from being commanded, but this does not imply Ash'ari subjectivism; rather, obeying the Benefactor is inherently good. The classification for bad actions follows the same pattern: bad-in-itself (e.g., shirk, zinā) that never loses badness; bad that can lose badness (e.g., eating carrion in necessity); bad-for-other with the badness accomplished by the act (e.g., fasting on Eid) or not accomplished (e.g., selling during Friday adhan); and a category of bad-for-other where the intermediary is disregarded, exemplified by ghasp (usurpation), which becomes bad in itself despite originally being bad for violating another's right.

Key Terms
حسن لنفسه (hasan li nafsihi): An action that is good in itself, without any external intermediary in the establishment of its goodness.
حسن لغيره (hasan li ghayrihi): An action that becomes good due to an external factor, which serves as an intermediary either in thubut or in 'urud.
قبيح لنفسه (qabih li nafsihi): An action that is bad in itself, never losing its badness.
قبيح لغيره (qabih li ghayrihi): An action that becomes bad due to an external factor, such as causing harm or leading to a prohibited outcome.
واسطة في الثبوت (wasita fi al-thubut): An intermediary that is part of the establishment (thubut) of the goodness or badness, not merely in its occurrence ('urud).
واسطة في العروض (wasita fi al-'urud): An intermediary that occurs (is accomplished) by the act itself, making the act good or bad because of its connection to that intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which of the following actions is an example of 'hasan li nafsihi' that can lose its goodness under certain circumstances?
A) Faith (iman)
B) Prayer (salat)
C) Zakat
D) Jihad

Key Takeaway
Hanafi ethics distinguishes intrinsic moral qualities from those acquired through external factors, affirming that some acts are inherently good or bad, while others derive their moral status from their connection to an intermediary—a framework that grounds the objectivity of moral values in Islamic law.

Does the Absolute Command Indicate Intrinsic Goodness?
Debating whether al-amr al-mutlaq implies hasan li nafsihi or li ghayrihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on al-amr al-mutlaq and evaluate the Hanafi tarjih for intrinsic goodness.

Core Idea
The absolute command indicates intrinsic goodness (hasan li nafsihi) per the Hanafi majority; the minority view is rejected.

This lesson explores a key dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: does a bare command automatically imply that the commanded act is intrinsically good (hasan li nafsihi) or only good for an external reason (hasan li ghayrihi)? The answer affects how we assess the praiseworthiness of commanded acts and shapes our understanding of divine commands. The passage clarifies that the authoritative Hanafi view, held by Shams al-A'imma and affirmed by the author of Asrar, is that the absolute command indicates intrinsic goodness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, note the two competing views: (1) The absolute command indicates hasan li nafsihi (intrinsic goodness), as held by Shams al-A'imma and the majority. (2) A contrary view, reported from an unknown source, claims it only indicates minimal goodness (hasan li ghayrihi) sufficient to repel necessity.
The contrary view is attributed to an unnamed scholar in al-Badi'.
2
Examine the correction by the author of Asrar
The author of Asrar al-Kitab al-Mubin corrects a misunderstanding: Shams al-A'imma did not claim that the commanded act never falls off (la yaqbal al-suqut); rather, he held that the absolute command indicates goodness in itself. The non-falling-off is a rare characteristic of some acts, not a general implication of the command.
This correction resolves a common misattribution.
3
Analyze the opposing argument
The opposing view argues that the absolute command establishes only the minimal goodness (adna) needed to fulfill the purpose (daf' al-darura). Intrinsic goodness requires an additional proof (dalil za'id). Identify this as a methodological difference in what the command itself conveys.
Consider whether the minimal goodness suffices for obligation or if more is needed.
4
Apply the Hanafi tarjih
The author of Asrar asserts, 'I know of no disagreement that the absolute command indicates goodness in itself.' This statement functions as a tarjih, indicating that the sound Hanafi position is the first view. Confirm that the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is hasan li nafsihi.
This tarjih is based on induction and the inherent praiseworthiness of faith.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
If the command indicates intrinsic goodness, then every commanded act is inherently praiseworthy regardless of performance. If only minimal goodness, then its praiseworthiness is contingent on external factors. This affects whether we can derive praise from the command alone in istinbat.
Practical effect: in deriving rulings, the first view gives stronger basis for commendation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر المطلق مجرداً عن القرينة هل للحسن لنفسه لا يقبل السقوط كما اختار شمس (الأئمة قال واقف أسرار الكتاب المبين قدس سره وأذاقنا الله تعالى ما أذاقه : إن هذه النسبة غلط فإنه ليس في كلامه إلا أنه يدل على الحسن لنفسه وهو الأظهر، كيف وعدم القبول للسقوط في بعض المأمورات أقل القليل فلا يجعل متبادراً (أو) للحسن (لغيره كما ذكر في البديع حكاية قول لا يدري قائله الثبوت الحسن في المأمور به اقتضاء فيثبت الأدنى الذي يكفي لدفع الضرورة، وأما كونه لنفسه فلا بد له من دليل زائد هذا وفي الأسرار لا أعلم خلافاً في أن الأمر المطلق يدل على الحسن لنفسه، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
The absolute command, stripped of context: does it indicate goodness in itself, not accepting falling off, as Shams al-A'imma chose? The author of Asrar al-Kitab al-Mubin (may Allah sanctify his soul and make us taste what He made him taste) said: 'This attribution is a mistake, for his statement only indicates that it denotes goodness in itself, and that is the more apparent. How so? For the non-acceptance of falling off in some commanded things is very little; thus it should not be made the primary understanding.' Or does it indicate goodness for another, as mentioned in al-Badi' in a quote whose source is unknown: 'The established goodness in the commanded thing is by way of entailment, so the lower level that suffices to repel necessity is established; as for it being in itself, it requires an additional proof.' And in al-Asrar: 'I know of no disagreement that the absolute command indicates goodness in itself.' And Allah knows the true reality.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul al-fiqh question: what does an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) indicate about the goodness of the commanded action? Shams al-A'imma (al-Halwani) held that it indicates hasan li nafsihi (intrinsic goodness) that does not accept falling off. However, the author of Asrar al-Kitab al-Mubin (likely a super-commentary) corrects an attribution: Shams al-A'imma's actual position is that the absolute command indicates goodness in itself, but that does not necessarily mean it never falls off—since some commanded actions do fall off, it's only a small minority that never fall off. Another view, reported in al-Badi' from an unknown source, claims that the absolute command only establishes a minimal goodness sufficient to fulfill necessity (e.g., repelling harm), not intrinsic goodness; for intrinsic goodness, an additional proof is required. The author of Asrar then asserts that in his work al-Asrar, he knows of no disagreement that the absolute command indicates intrinsic goodness. The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best,' indicating the preferred view is that the absolute command does indicate hasan li nafsihi.

Key Terms
الأمر المطلق (al-amr al-mutlaq): A command that is not accompanied by any contextual indication (qarina) specifying its meaning or effect.
الحسن لنفسه (al-hasan li nafsihi): The property of being good intrinsically, independent of any external factor or command.
الحسن لغيره (al-hasan li ghayrihi): Goodness that accrues to an action from an external source, such as a command or an end.
شمس الأئمة (Shams al-A'imma): Honorific for the Hanafi jurist al-Halwani, a prominent usuli from Samarqand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon position of Shams al-A'imma regarding the absolute command?
A) It indicates minimal goodness to repel necessity
B) It indicates goodness in itself
C) It requires additional proof for goodness
D) It indicates goodness that never falls off

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon position is that the absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) indicates intrinsic goodness (hasan li nafsihi) of the commanded act.

Categories of Goodness and Evil in Actions: Intrinsic and Derived
Classifying intrinsic and extrinsic goodness and evil with Hanafi defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intrinsic and derived goodness/evil in actions and explain the Hanafi position on moral value prior to revelation.

Core Idea
Intrinsic goodness/evil is inseparable from the act; derived goodness/evil depends on external factors.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut, the commentator expands on the classification of moral qualities of actions. He defines categories of good (al-husn) and evil (al-qubh), both intrinsic and extrinsic, and defends the Hanafi view that actions have intrinsic value independent of command. Understanding these categories is essential for grasping how Hanafi usul grounds moral obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall classification system
Read the passage to find how the author categorizes goodness (husn) and evil (qubh). Look for terms like 'bi al-dhat' (intrinsic) and 'li ghayrihi' (derived).
2
Explain the two subtypes of derived goodness
Note the distinction: some derived goods achieve the other good (e.g., walking to Friday prayer) while others do not (e.g., ablution as condition).
The author mentions ablution has an element of intrinsic goodness as purification.
3
Identify the three categories of evil
Find the three types: intrinsic that cannot drop (shirk), intrinsic that can drop under necessity (carrion), and derived (fasting on Eid, selling during adhan).
4
Understand the Hanafi-Ash'ari dispute
Locate the argument about whether command confers goodness or affirms pre-existing value. Note the author's defense of Shams al-A'imma.
The author insists that performing a commanded act is intrinsically good as gratitude.
5
Summarize the absolute command indication
The passage concludes that the absolute command indicates intrinsic goodness, supporting the Hanafi position.
Contrary to a claim attributed to Shams al-A'imma, the author affirms this is the apparent view.

Source Text

المقالة الثانية في الأحكام

دليل على خلافه؟ نعم مطلق التعذيب لا حسن فيه، لكنه غير الجهاد وكذا صلاة الجنازة ذكر الله تعالى وعبادة له مع الدعاء، فيجوز أن يكون حسنه بالذات وليس شبيهاً بعبادة غير الله تعالى كما في «الحج»، نعم الكفر وإسلام الميت وسائط في الثبوت كالبيت في الحج، هذا : والظاهر أن الجهاد لا يصلح أن يلحق بالأول، ألا ترى أن القتال معهم يسقط بأدنى شبهة، والحسن بالذات لا يسقط عن الذمة بالشبهات بل يجب معها للاحتياط كما لا يخفى على الناظر في الفقه وأما صلاة الجنازة فلما سقط بفعل البعض علمنا أن ليس المقصود إتعاب البدن بذكر الله، بل قضاء حاجة الميت، فيكون حسنها لأجله، وبهذا يخرج الجواب عن الجهاد أيضاً فتدبر وأنصف والثاني: أن لا يتأدى هذا الغير بأداء هذا الحسن. كالسعي إلى الجمعة، فإنه حسن بحسن صلاة الجمعة ولا تتأدى بالسعي فقط، وربما يمثل بالوضوء، فإنه حسن بحسن الصلاة لأجل كونه شرطاً، وفيه شائبة من الخفاء فإن الوضوء بما هو طهارة حسن، وإن كان له حسن آخر من جهة حسن مشروطة، ألا ترى أن الشرع ندب الدوام على الطهارة، والمندوب حسن وليس ندبها لإقامة الصلاة، فإن من أوقات مندوبية الطهارة وقت الخطبة وسائر الأوقات المكروهة فتدبر وليعلم أن جميع المأمورات فيها حسن آخر ثبت بكونه مأموراً به، ولا ينافي الحسن الثابت قبل الأمر فالإيمان مع كونه حسناً كونه حسناً في نفسه حسن لكونه مأموراً به، ولا تظنن أن هذا يؤول إلى مذهب الأشعري من أن الحسن يثبت بالأمر، فإنا نقول : إن أداء مأمور الله حسن في نفسه فإنه من قبيل شكر المنعم، وأداء هذه العبادات من أفراد أداء المأمور به، فيكون حسنه بحسنه لا أن الشرع جعله حسناً وكان إتيانها وإتيان المنهيات في أنفسهما متساويين، وأعاذنا الله تعالى عن هذا القول وهكذا أقسام القبيح) فالقبيح قبيح لعينه لا يحتمل السقوط، كقبح الشرك وسائر العقائد الباطلة والزنا أو يحتمل السقوط، كأكل الميتة سقط قبحه في «المخمصة»، وقبيح لغيره يتأدى بارتكاب هذا القبيح، كصوم يوم العيد قبيح، لأجل كونه إعراضاً عن ضيافة الله تعالى، وبارتكاب الصوم يرتكب الإعراض، أو لا يتأدى، كالبيع وقت النداء قبيح لإفضائه إلى فوات الجمعة، وأما القبيح لغيره الذي يكون فيه واسطة في الثبوت مهدرة لم أر بيانه في كلام القوم، وإن كان فمثاله الغصب، فإنه إنما حرم لتعلق حق الغير، لكن هذه الواسطة مهدرة، فصار الغصب قبيحاً بالذات (الأمر المطلق مجرداً عن القرينة هل للحسن لنفسه لا يقبل السقوط كما اختار شمس (الأئمة قال واقف أسرار الكتاب المبين قدس سره وأذاقنا الله تعالى ما أذاقه : إن هذه النسبة غلط فإنه ليس في كلامه إلا أنه يدل على الحسن لنفسه وهو الأظهر، كيف وعدم القبول للسقوط في بعض المأمورات أقل القليل فلا يجعل متبادراً (أو) للحسن (لغيره كما ذكر في البديع حكاية قول لا يدري قائله الثبوت الحسن في المأمور به اقتضاء فيثبت الأدنى الذي يكفي لدفع الضرورة، وأما كونه لنفسه فلا بد له من دليل زائد هذا وفي الأسرار لا أعلم خلافاً في أن الأمر المطلق يدل على الحسن لنفسه، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال .
The second article on rulings. Evidence against it? Yes, absolute torment has no goodness, but it is not jihad, and similarly funeral prayer is remembrance of Allah and worship with supplication, so it is possible that its goodness is intrinsic and not similar to worship of other than Allah as in Hajj. Yes, disbelief and the Islam of the deceased are intermediaries in existence like the Ka'bah in Hajj. This: It is apparent that jihad cannot be annexed to the first kind; do you not see that fighting with them drops by the slightest doubt, while intrinsic goodness does not drop from liability by doubts but rather is obligatory with them for precaution, as is clear to the student of fiqh. As for funeral prayer, since it drops by some performing it, we know that the intent is not to fatigue the body with remembrance of Allah, but to fulfill the need of the deceased, so its goodness is for his sake. By this, the answer for jihad also emerges, so ponder and be fair. The second: that this other is not achieved by performing this good, like walking to Friday prayer, for it is good by the goodness of Friday prayer and is not achieved by walking alone. Perhaps one may give the example of ablution, for it is good by the goodness of prayer for being a condition, but there is an element of subtlety: ablution as purification is good, although it has another goodness from the aspect of the goodness of what it conditions. Do you not see that the law encouraged continuity of purification, and the recommendatory is good, and its encouragement is not for performing prayer, for among the times for recommendatory purification is the time of the sermon and all disliked times, so ponder. And let it be known that all commanded acts have another goodness established by being commanded, and this does not negate the goodness established before the command. So faith, while being good in itself, is good for being commanded. Do not think that this leads to the Ash'ari doctrine that goodness is established by command, for we say: Performing what Allah commands is good in itself, for it is a kind of gratitude to the Benefactor, and performing these acts of worship are instances of performing what is commanded, so their goodness is due to its goodness, not that the law made them good and their commission and commission of prohibitions were equal in themselves. May Allah protect us from this statement. Likewise the divisions of evil: Evil is evil in itself and does not admit of dropping, like the evil of polytheism and other false beliefs and adultery, or admits of dropping, like eating carrion—its evil drops in dire hunger. Evil for another: it is achieved by committing this evil, like fasting on the day of Eid is evil for being a turning away from Allah's hospitality, and by committing the fast one commits turning away; or it is not achieved, like selling at the time of the call is evil for leading to missing Friday prayer. As for evil for another in which the intermediate in existence is disregarded, I have not seen its explanation in the words of the people, but if there is, its example is usurpation, for it is forbidden due to the involvement of another's right, but this intermediate is disregarded, so usurpation becomes evil in itself. The absolute command stripped of context: does it indicate goodness in itself without accepting dropping, as chosen by Shams al-A'imma? He said, 'The stander for the secrets of the clear book, may Allah sanctify his secret and make us taste what he tasted: This attribution is mistaken, for in his words there is only that it indicates goodness in itself, and that is more apparent. How so? Non-acceptance of dropping in some commanded acts is the least of the least, so it should not be made the immediate understanding. Or (indicates) goodness for another, as mentioned in al-Badi' as a narration whose speaker is unknown: Goodness in the commanded act is implication, so the lower {goodness} that suffices to repel necessity is established. As for its being in itself, it requires additional evidence. This is in al-Asrar: I know of no disagreement that the absolute command indicates goodness in itself. And Allah knows the reality of the situation.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' is a commentary on the matn 'Musallam al-Thubut'. It falls under the second article on rulings (ahkam) and focuses on categorizing the goodness (husn) and evil (qubh) of actions. The author first addresses potential objections to the classification, using examples of jihad and funeral prayer to argue that some actions may have intrinsic goodness while others derive it from external factors. He then distinguishes between two types of 'good for another': those where the derived action achieves the goodness (like walking to Friday prayer) and those where it does not fully achieve it (like ablution's role as a condition for prayer). Importantly, the author defends the Hanafi position that actions have intrinsic moral value before the command (contra the Ash'aris who hold that moral values are solely conferred by revelation). He illustrates with faith, which is inherently good yet also commanded. Next, he divides evil (qubh) into three categories: (1) intrinsically evil that cannot be removed (e.g., shirk, adultery), (2) intrinsically evil that may be removed under necessity (e.g., eating carrion in hunger), and (3) evil for another, which may or may not be achieved by committing the act (e.g., fasting on Eid is evil due to it being an expression of turning away from Allah's hospitality; selling during Friday call is evil because it leads to missing the prayer). The passage then discusses the absolute command without context—whether it indicates intrinsic goodness. The author sides with Shams al-A'imma that it does indicate intrinsic goodness, contradicting a claim attributed to him, and mentions that in 'al-Asrar' there is consensus on this. The discussion is foundational for understanding how Hanafi usul grounds moral categories and the role of divine command.

Key Terms
الحسن بالذات (al-ḥusn bi al-dhāt): Intrinsic goodness that does not accept dropping; the act is good in itself regardless of external factors.
القبيح لعينه (al-qubḥ li 'aynihi): Intrinsic evil that does not accept dropping; acts like shirk and adultery are evil in themselves.
الحسن لغيره (al-ḥusn li ghayrihi): Goodness derived from another act; the act is good because it leads to or is part of something else that is good.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following is an example of an act that is intrinsically evil (qabih li 'aynihi)?
A) Eating carrion in hunger
B) Selling during Friday call to prayer
C) Shirk (polytheism)
D) Fasting on the day of Eid

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic goodness/evil is inseparable from the act; derived goodness/evil depends on external factors, and the Hanafi school affirms that actions have real moral value prior to revelation.
