Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
8 min
The Hanafi Definition of Hukm: Requirement, Choice, and Posited Rulings
Defining hukm as divine speech relating to a mukallaf's action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hukm and classify whether a given divine address qualifies as a hukm.

Core Idea
The Hanafi hukm is Allah's speech demanding or permitting an action, excluding mere information.

This lesson dissects the foundational definition of 'hukm' (legal ruling) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. We will see how the Hanafi school refined the definition to exclude purely informative verses and to include both explicit and implicit demands. The passage also addresses whether posited rulings (ahkam wad'iyya) are part of the definition and engages with the Mu'tazili objection regarding the eternality of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the sentence: 'al-hukm huwa khitab Allah al-muta'alliq bi-fi'l mukallaf.' Note the key components: khitab Allah, fi'l mukallaf, and the additions of iqtida' and takhyir.
The definition is relational: Allah's speech regarding the action of a morally responsible person.
2
Understand the refinement against the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'ari incomplete definition (without iqtida'/takhyir) was criticized using verse 37:96. See how adding 'iqtida' aw takhyir' excludes such informative verses.
The verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' is not a ruling because it has no demand or choice.
3
Analyze the inclusion of posited rulings (ahkam wad'iyya)
Some Hanafis add 'aw wad'an' to include rulings like causality, condition, etc. Others argue that such rulings contain implicit iqtida'. Trace the arguments and the concept of 'iqtida' dimni'.
The debate shows the breadth of the definition; the author leans toward including wad'iyya under a broader iqtida'.
4
Evaluate the Mu'tazili objection on temporality of hukm
The Mu'tazila argue that if the divine address is eternal, the ruling must also be eternal. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the eternal kalam nafsi and the temporal hukm as a derivative relation.
Abrogation (naskh) proves that rulings can change, supporting the temporality of the hukm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: في الحكم وهو عندنا معشر أهل السنة خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل مكلف، المكلف أي جنس المكلف، فلا يختص الحد بالمتعلق بكل مكلف، وقد كان اكتفى في بعض كلمات الأشعرية على هذا، فورد عليهم النقض بنحو وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ [الصافات: 96] فزيد قوله : (اقتضاء) حتمياً أولاً (أو تخييراً فنحو : ﴿وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ليس منه لعدم الاقتضاء والتخيير فيه فلم يرد النقض، واعتذر أيضاً بأن الحيثية معتبرة، والمقصود خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف بما هو مكلف والآية ليست متعلقة بفعله بما هو وهو غير واف، إذ حينئذ تخرج الإباحة، لأنها غير متعلقة بفعله بما هو مكلف، إذ لا تكليف فيها فتدبر وكلمة أو ها هنا ليست للشك والإبهام بل للتنويع فلا يضر التعريف، والمراد بالفعل ما هو أعم من فعل القلب والجوارح فلا يخرج نحو وجوب الإيمان، نعم يرد عليه خروج نحو الإجماع حجة، إلا أن يقال : ليس بحكم إلا إذا أوّل بأن العمل بمقتضاه واجب، وحينئذ صار متعلقاً بفعل المكلف فتدبر وها هنا أبحاث الأول : أنه لا ينعكس) الحد (فإنه يخرج منه الأحكام الوضعية كالحكم بسببية الوقت للصلاة والبيع للملك وأمثالهما. (فمنهم من زاد) في الحد (أو وضعاً) فدخلت تلك الأحكام ومنهم من لم يزد، فتارة) لدفع هذا الإيراد (يمنع خروجها عن الحد ويدعي أن في الأحكام (الوضعية اقتضاء أيضاً (فإن الاقتضاء) المذكور في الحد (أعم من الصريحي والضمني) والوضعيات فيها اقتضاء ضمني، فإنه يفهم من سببية الوقت للصلاة أنها واجبة عنده وهكذا. فإن قيل : فحينئذ يدخل القصة، إذ فيه أيضاً اقتضاء ضمني، فإنه يفهم منه الاجتناب عن أمثال أفعالهم السيئة وإتيان أمثال الحسنة؟ أجاب قصة لا اقتضاء فيها فإنها بهذا الاعتبار اخبار محض فلا بقوله : ( والقصة من حيث هي تدخل، وإما باعتبار أنها يفهم منها إيجاب وحرمة نظراً إلى أن شرائع من قبلنا حجة واجبة العمل، فحكم داخل في الحكم وفيه نظر فإنه إن أريد بالاقتضاء الضمني الدلالة على الاقتضاء ولو التزاماً، فعدم كون القصة دالة عليه غير ظاهر، إذ يفهم من الألفاظ، وإن أريد الدلالة مطابقة أو تضمناً أو التزاماً مقصودة بالذات فكون الأحكام الوضعية بأسرها كذلك دالة على الاقتضاء على هذا النحو غير ظاهر ، فإن قوله : « لا يقبل الله الصلاة من غير طهور» لا يفهم منه بما هو هذا الكلام إلا الاشتراط، وأما اقتضاء وجوب الوضوء فلازم من جهة الصلاة فتأمل فيه فإنه موضع تأمل (وما في التحرير) مطابقاً لقول صدر الشريعة (إن الوضع مقدم عليه فإن وضع الشارع سببية الوقت موجب لوجوب الصلاة عنده، والموجب مقدم، فهما متغايران، فإدراج أحدهما في الآخر غير معقول وما في «التلويح» أن التغاير لا يضر أعمية الاقتضاء، بل التغاير بين الأعم والأخص ضروري ساقط، إذ المراد بالتغاير المباينة، والوضع مباين للاقتضاء موجب له (لا يضر ) لما نحن بصدده، (الصدق) الاقتضاء (الأعم) من الصريحي وغيره، وإن كان الصريحي مبايناً له ومتأخراً عنه (وتارة يمنع هذا المكتفي (كونها من المحدود، فإنا لا نسمي) الخطابات الوضعية (حكماً، وإن سمى غيرنا ولا مشاحة في الإصطلاح البحث الثاني من المعتزلة : أن الخطاب عندكم) معشر أهل السنة (أي الكلام النفسي قديم والحكم حادث لثبوت عدمه بالنسخ وما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه فما لم وجوب
The second chapter: On the ruling (hukm). It is, with us the people of the Sunnah, Allah's speech relating to the action of a legally responsible person (mukallaf). 'Mukallaf' means the genus of the mukallaf, so the definition is not restricted to what relates to every individual. It was sufficient in some Ash'ari statements to this effect, so they were refuted by [the verse] 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96). So the phrase 'requirement (iqtida') definitively or choice (takhyir)' was added. So [the verse] 'And Allah created you and what you do' is not included due to the lack of requirement and choice in it, thus the refutation does not apply. He also excused that the consideration is taken into account, and the intended meaning is Allah's speech relating to the action of the mukallaf insofar as he is a mukallaf, and the verse is not related to his action as such, and that is insufficient, for then permission (ibaha) would be excluded because it is not related to his action insofar as he is a mukallaf, since there is no obligation (taklif) in it. So ponder. And the word 'or' here is not for doubt and ambiguity but for division, so it does not harm the definition. And by 'action' is meant what includes both the action of the heart and limbs, so it does not exclude, for example, the obligation of faith. Yes, the definition is objected to for excluding, e.g., 'consensus is a proof', unless it is said that it is not a ruling unless interpreted that acting by it is obligatory, and then it becomes related to the action of the mukallaf. So ponder. Here there are investigations. The first: that the definition is not conversive, for it excludes posited rulings (al-ahkam al-wad'iyya) like the ruling of causality of time for prayer, and sale for ownership, and the like. Some have added 'or a posited (wad'an)' in the definition so that these rulings are included, and some did not add. Sometimes, to repel this objection, one prevents their exclusion from the definition and claims that in posited rulings there is also requirement (iqtida'), for the requirement mentioned in the definition is more general than explicit and implicit. And posited rulings have implicit requirement, for it is understood from the causality of time for prayer that prayer is obligatory at that time, and so on. If it is said: then the story (qissa) [of previous prophets] enters, for it also has implicit requirement, for it is understood from it avoidance of similar evil deeds and performance of similar good deeds? He answered: The story has no requirement, for it in this respect is mere information. So not by his saying: 'and the story as such enters'. But if it is considered that from it obligation and prohibition are understood due to the view that previous laws are a proof and obligatory to act upon, then it is a ruling included in the definition. And there is consideration in this. For if by implicit requirement is meant indication of requirement even by implication, then the fact that the story does not indicate it is not apparent, for it is understood from the words. And if by indication is meant primary indication by correspondence, inclusion, or necessary implication, then that all posited rulings are indicative of requirement in this manner is not apparent. For his saying: 'Allah does not accept prayer without purification' is not understood from this speech as such except the condition. As for the requirement of the obligation of ablution, it is necessary from the side of prayer. So ponder, for it is a place of pondering. And what is in al-Tahrir, consistent with the statement of Sadr al-Shari'ah, that the posited (wad') precedes it, for the Lawgiver's positing the causality of time necessitates the obligation of prayer at that time, and the necessitator precedes, so they are different, so inclusion of one in the other is not conceivable. And what is in al-Talwih that difference does not harm the generality of requirement, but the difference between the more general and the more specific is necessarily invalid, for by difference is meant separation, and the posited is separate from the requirement and necessitator of it, does not harm what we are dealing with? The truth of the requirement that is more general than explicit and other, even if the explicit is separate from it and subsequent to it. And sometimes this sufficient one prevents that they are part of the defined, for we do not call the posited addresses a ruling, even if others call them that, and there is no dispute over terminology. The second investigation from the Mu'tazila: That the address with you people of the Sunnah, i.e., the internal speech (kalam nafsi) is eternal, and the ruling is temporal because its non-existence is established by abrogation, and what is established as eternal is impossible to cease, so it has not... obligation.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' is a commentary on the second chapter of the matn 'Musallam al-Thubut', which deals with the definition of 'al-hukm' (legal ruling). The author begins by stating the Hanafi definition: Hukm is Allah's speech (khitab) relating to the action of a legally responsible person (mukallaf). He clarifies that 'mukallaf' refers to the genus, not each individual. He then explains that this definition was refined in response to an Ash'ari objection based on the Quranic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96), which if taken as a ruling would be problematic. So the Hanafis added that the address must include requirement (iqtida') or choice (takhyir), thus excluding informative verses. He further emphasizes that 'action' includes both heart and limb actions, so inward obligations like faith are included. The passage then addresses objections about the definition not covering 'posited rulings' (ahkam wad'iyya) such as causality, condition, etc. Some Hanafis add 'or a posited' to the definition; others argue that posited rulings have implicit requirement (iqtida' dimni) because understanding a cause implies an obligation. This leads to a discussion about stories of previous nations (qisas) – whether they contain implicit requirement. The author weighs the counterarguments. He then cites al-Tahrir (by Sadr al-Shari'ah) and al-Talwih (by al-Taftazani) on whether posited rulings are separate from requirement; the consensus is that they are distinct, but that does not preclude inclusion under a broader 'iqtida'. Finally, the passage introduces the Mu'tazili objection: the address (kalam nafsi) is eternal, but the ruling is temporal (proven by abrogation). The Hanafi response is not fully preserved here but is initiated. The passage thus systematically refines the definition of hukm, handling objections from Ash'aris, regarding the scope of 'iqtida', and from Mu'tazila regarding its temporality.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-ḥukm): خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف: Allah's speech relating to the action of a legally responsible person, with requirement (iqtida') or choice (takhyir) or positing (wad').
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍāʾ): Demand for an action (obligation or prohibition) or its choice (permission); can be explicit (sarih) or implicit (dimni).
الأحكام الوضعية (al-aḥkām al-waḍʿiyya): Posited rulings: those that establish a cause (sabab), condition (shart), or preventer (mani); e.g., time as cause for prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, why is the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' not considered a hukm?
A) It is a statement about creation, not an address to a mukallaf.
B) It lacks requirement (iqtida') and choice (takhyir).
C) It is abrogated by later verses.
D) It only applies to the actions of the heart, not the limbs.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hukm is precise: Allah's speech that includes a demand (iqtida') or choice (takhyir) for the action of a morally responsible person, thereby excluding mere information and enabling a coherent approach to abrogation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Including Wad'i Rulings in the Definition of Hukm
Critical refinement of hukm definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that wad'i rulings are included in the definition of hukm through implicit demand and evaluate the opposing view.

Core Idea
Wad'i rulings are included in hukm because they contain implicit demand even without explicit command.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut refines the definition of hukm by addressing the objection that wad'i (declaratory) rulings—such as causality, conditionality, and preventiveness—are excluded. The Hanafi position, defended by the commentator, argues that such rulings do contain iqtida' (demand) implicitly, thus falling under the standard definition. Understanding this debate clarifies the scope of shar'i rulings and the methodological principles of the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard definition of hukm and the wad'i objection
The lesson begins with the definition: 'خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاءً أو تخييراً'. The objection is that this excludes wad'i rulings, which do not contain explicit demand. Locate this objection in the text.
The Ash'ari verse objection regarding ﴿وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ﴾ is used to illustrate the need for 'iqtida'.
2
Understand the two Hanafi responses
Some Hanafis add 'أو وضعاً' (or declaratory) to the definition. Others argue that wad'i rulings already contain iqtida' implicitly (iqtida' dimni). Identify both approaches in the text.
The commentator leans toward the implicit demand approach.
3
Analyze the concept of implicit demand
Implicit demand means that from a wad'i ruling (e.g., causality of time for prayer) one understands the obligation of the act. The text uses the example: 'سببية الوقت للصلاة' implies 'الصلاة واجبة عند الوقت'.
The commentator distinguishes between explicit (sarihi) and implicit (dimni) demand.
4
Evaluate the objection regarding historical stories
An opponent argues that if wad'i rulings have implicit demand, then Qur'anic stories would also have it, wrongly making them rulings. The text refutes this by noting that stories are mere reports (ikhbar mahd) without demand.
The refinement shows that stories, unlike wad'i, do not indicate demand even implicitly.
5
Discuss the terminological dispute and conclusion
Some scholars deny calling wad'i rulings 'hukm' altogether. The commentator dismisses this as a mere terminological quibble. Understand that the substantive issue is whether wad'i rulings are covered by the definition's conditions.
The passage ends by citing differences between al-Tahrir and al-Talwih on the relationship between wad'i and iqtida'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكلف، المكلف أي جنس المكلف، فلا يختص الحد بالمتعلق بكل مكلف، وقد كان اكتفى في بعض كلمات الأشعرية على هذا، فورد عليهم النقض بنحو ﴿وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ﴾ [الصافات: 96] فزيد قوله : (اقتضاء) حتمياً أولاً (أو تخييراً فنحو : ﴿وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ﴾ ليس منه لعدم الاقتضاء والتخيير فيه فلم يرد النقض، واعتذر أيضاً بأن الحيثية معتبرة، والمقصود خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف بما هو مكلف والآية ليست متعلقة بفعله بما هو وهو غير واف، إذ حينئذ تخرج الإباحة، لأنها غير متعلقة بفعله بما هو مكلف، إذ لا تكليف، فيها فتدبر وكلمة أو ها هنا ليست للشك والإبهام بل للتنويع فلا يضر التعريف، والمراد بالفعل ما هو أعم من فعل القلب والجوارح فلا يخرج نحو وجوب الإيمان، نعم يرد عليه خروج نحو الإجماع حجة، إلا أن يقال : ليس بحكم إلا إذا أوّل بأن العمل بمقتضاه واجب، وحينئذ صار متعلقاً بفعل المكلف فتدبر وها هنا أبحاث الأول : أنه لا ينعكس) الحد (فإنه يخرج منه الأحكام الوضعية كالحكم بسببية الوقت للصلاة والبيع للملك وأمثالهما. (فمنهم من زاد) في الحد (أو وضعاً) فدخلت تلك الأحكام ومنهم من لم يزد، فتارة) لدفع هذا الإيراد (يمنع خروجها عن الحد ويدعي أن في الأحكام (الوضعية اقتضاء أيضاً (فإن الاقتضاء) المذكور في الحد (أعم من الصريحي والضمني) والوضعيات فيها اقتضاء ضمني، فإنه يفهم من سببية الوقت للصلاة أنها واجبة عنده وهكذا. فإن قيل : فحينئذ يدخل القصة، إذ فيه أيضاً اقتضاء ضمني، فإنه يفهم منه الاجتناب عن أمثال أفعالهم السيئة وإتيان أمثال الحسنة؟ أجاب قصة لا اقتضاء فيها فإنها بهذا الاعتبار اخبار محض فلا بقوله : ( والقصة من حيث هي تدخل، وإما باعتبار أنها يفهم منها إيجاب وحرمة نظراً إلى أن شرائع من قبلنا حجة واجبة العمل، فحكم داخل في الحكم وفيه ،نظر فإنه إن أريد بالاقتضاء الضمني الدلالة على الاقتضاء ولو التزاماً، فعدم كون القصة دالة عليه غير ظاهر، إذ يفهم من الألفاظ، وإن أريد الدلالة مطابقة أو تضمناً أو التزاماً مقصودة بالذات فكون الأحكام الوضعية بأسرها كذلك دالة على الاقتضاء على هذا النحو غير ظاهر ، فإن قوله : « لا يقبل الله الصلاة من غير طهور» لا يفهم منه بما هو هذا الكلام إلا الاشتراط، وأما اقتضاء وجوب الوضوء فلازم من جهة الصلاة فتأمل فيه فإنه موضع تأمل (وما في التحرير) مطابقاً لقول صدر الشريعة (إن الوضع مقدم عليه فإن وضع الشارع سببية الوقت موجب لوجوب الصلاة عنده، والموجب مقدم، فهما متغايران، فإدراج أحدهما في الآخر غير معقول وما في «التلويح» أن التغاير لا يضر أعمية الاقتضاء، بل التغاير بين الأعم والأخص ضروري ساقط، إذ المراد بالتغاير المباينة، والوضع مباين للاقتضاء موجب له (لا يضر ) لما نحن بصدده، (الصدق) الاقتضاء (الأعم) من الصريحي وغيره، وإن كان الصريحي مبايناً له ومتأخراً عنه (وتارة يمنع هذا المكتفي (كونها من المحدود، فإنا لا نسمي) الخطابات الوضعية (حكماً، وإن سمى غيرنا ولا مشاحة في الإصطلاح
Mukallaf, i.e., the genus of the mukallaf; so the definition is not specific to what relates to every mukallaf. The Ash‘aris relied on some of their words on this, but were refuted by verses like 'And Allah created you and what you do' [Saffat:96]. So (the definer) added the word 'demand' necessarily first or optionally. Thus the verse is not included due to lack of demand and option, so the refutation did not apply. They also excused that the aspect is considered, and the intent is the speech of Allah related to the act of the mukallaf as a mukallaf, and the verse is not related to his act as such—this is insufficient because then permission would be excluded, since it is not related to his act as a mukallaf as there is no obligation in it. So reflect. The word 'or' here is not for doubt or ambiguity but for division, so it does not harm the definition. The intended act is general including acts of heart and limbs, so the obligation of faith is not excluded. Yes, it is objected that consensus as evidence is excluded, unless it is said that it is not a ruling unless interpreted that acting upon it is obligatory, then it becomes related to the act of the mukallaf. So reflect. Here are discussions: First: that it does not reverse the definition, for it excludes declaratory rulings like the ruling of causality of time for prayer and sale for ownership and the like. Some added 'or declaratory' to the definition to include those rulings, and some did not add. Sometimes, to repel this objection, they deny its exclusion from the definition and claim that declaratory rulings also have demand, for the demand mentioned in the definition is more general than explicit and implicit, and declaratory rulings have implicit demand: from the causality of time for prayer it is understood that prayer is obligatory at that time, etc. If it is said: then stories would enter, for they also have implicit demand, as one understands avoidance of bad deeds and doing of good deeds from them? The answer: stories have no demand, for in this respect they are mere reports. So [the definer] says: the story as such does not enter, but if considered that it implies obligation and prohibition due to the view that laws before us are authoritative and obligatory to act upon, then it is a ruling that enters. And there is consideration: if implicit demand is intended as indicating demand even by entailment, then it is not obvious that stories do not indicate it, since it is understood from the words. If intended as indicative meaning (literal, by inclusion, or by intended entailment), then it is not obvious that all declaratory rulings indicate demand in this way. For the statement 'Allah does not accept prayer without purification' only implies condition; the demand for ablution is a necessary consequence of prayer. So reflect, for it is a place of reflection. What is in al-Tahrir, agreeing with Sadr al-Shari‘ah, that the declaratory is prior to it, for the Legislator's positing of causality of time necessitates obligation of prayer in his view, and the necessitator is prior, so they are distinct; incorporating one into the other is unreasonable. What is in al-Talwih that distinctness does not harm the generality of demand; rather, the opposition between the general and specific is necessarily invalid, for by distinctness is meant contradiction, while the declaratory is contradictory to demand but necessitates it. It does not harm what we are discussing; the truth of the more general demand (explicit and other) even if explicit is incompatible and later. Sometimes this objector denies that declaratory rulings are from the defined, saying 'we do not call declaratory speeches a ruling', even if others call them so, and there is no quibble over terminology.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of hukm (ruling), specifically addressing the objection that the definition excludes wad‘i (declaratory) rulings like causality. The commentator explains that those who add 'or declaratory' to the definition solve this, but another school argues that wad‘i rulings also contain iqtida’ (demand) implicitly: e.g., the causality of time for prayer implies the obligation of prayer. The objection that this would include historical stories is refuted by noting stories are pure reports without demand. The commentator also discusses the distinction between explicit and implicit demand and cites disagreements between al-Tahrir and al-Talwih. Ultimately, some deny that wad‘i rulings are called 'hukm' in their terminology.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاءً أو تخييراً
الوضعي (al-wad'i): الحكم الذي لا يتضمن تكليفاً مباشراً كالسببية والشرطية والمانعية
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): الطلب الحتمي أو التخييري, يتناول الصريح والضمني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what type of demand do wad'i rulings (like causality) contain?
A) Explicit demand (iqtida' sarihi)
B) Implicit demand (iqtida' dimni)
C) No demand at all
D) Only option (takhayyur)

Key Takeaway
Wad'i rulings are included under hukm because they contain implicit demand (iqtida' dimni), thus the definition of hukm is broader than explicit commands.

Children's Acts and the Scope of Hukm: A Hanafi-Shafi‘i Dispute
Responding to the objection that the definition excludes children's acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument that children's acts are subject to shar‘i rulings and identify the thamarah al-khilaf with the Shafi‘i position.

Core Idea
Shar‘i rulings apply to children's acts: their prayer is mandub, sale is valid, and financial rights are binding — despite absence of taklif.

The standard definition of hukm as 'Allah's speech related to the act of the mukallaf' seems to exclude children. But Hanafis argue that children's acts clearly have shar‘i effects: reward for prayer, validity of sale, and liability for damages. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut refines the definition by responding to a Shafi‘i objection, ultimately affirming that hukm applies broadly to the acts of servants, not just the mukallaf.

Guided Path
1
State the objection to the definition
Identify the challenge: the definition of hukm is criticized because children's acts (prayer, sale, liability) are subject to shar‘i rulings yet children are not mukallaf.
The objection is that the definition is 'refuted' (manqud) by these examples.
2
Present the Shafi‘i response
Some Shafi‘is argue that there is no khitab (divine speech) directed to the child, so his prayer is not mandub (merely encouraged by guardian), validity of sale is rational (not legal), and obligations fall on the guardian, not the child.
This response attempts to maintain the restrictive definition of hukm as speech to the mukallaf.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi commentator rejects each point: well-known hadiths confirm reward for children; validity of sale requires legal cause (sabab), which is established by the Lawgiver; and financial liability is imposed on the child's estate primarily.
The rebuttal relies on textual evidence (hadith) and the nature of legal effectiveness.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is whether a child's prayer is considered mandub (rewardable) and whether his sale is a legally binding transaction. Under the Hanafi view, the child's acts carry real shar‘i consequences.
This affects fiqh rulings on ibadat (e.g., reward for child's prayer) and muamalat (e.g., liability for damages).
5
Conclude with the correct definition
Sadr al-Shari‘ah's definition 'Allah's speech related to the act of the servant' is preferred because it does not restrict to mukallaf, accommodating evidence for children's acts.
The definition is inclusive, covering all acts that the Lawgiver addresses, directly or indirectly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث: الحد منقوض بأحكام أفعال الصبي من مندوبية صلاته وصحة بيعه ووجوب الحقوق المالية في ذمته أولاً) وإن كان يؤدي بالنائب الولي، وهي ليست متعلقة بفعل المكلف (وأجيب) في كتب بعض الشافعية (بأنه لا خطاب للصبي) أصلاً، فليست صلاته مندوبة وإنما للولي (التحريض) على الصلاة للاعتياد لا للثواب بل (وله) أي للولي الثواب وعليه (الأداء) أي أداء الحقوق من مال الصبي لا أن الحقوق تجب أوّلاً على الصبي (والصحة) أمر (عقلي) لا حكم شرعي لأنها تتم بالمطابقة) أي مطابقة الجزئي للحقيقة المعتبرة شرعاً ولا حاجة فيه إلى الخطاب (وفيه ما فيه) لأن القول بنفي الثواب عن الصبي بعيد جداً ومخالف للأحاديث المشهورة فصدق على صلاته حد المندوب فلا مجال لمنع مندوبية صلاته قال في الحاشية الأظهر أن ترتب الثواب لعله بجري عادة الله تعالى أن لا يضيع أجر من أحسن عملاً .انتهى ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا لا يصح من قبل الأشعري، إذ حسن ولا قبح من غير خطاب، فلو لم يكن حكم من الشرع فليس هذا العمل بحسن، وأما على رأينا وإن كان هناك حسن من دون ورود خطاب لكن إثابة الصبي ليس من هذا القبيل، فإنه قد ورد الخطاب النبوي والتقرير بإيصال الثواب على أعمال الصبيان، فإن قلت : لا هذا الخطاب حكماً ، إنما الحكم الخطاب المتعلق بفعل المكلف . قلت : هذا تحكم ظاهر لا يلتفت إليه فافهم وأما الحقوق المالية فلو لم تجب على الصبي كان الأخذ ظلماً، فإذن الحقوق المالية كضمان المتلفات يجب في ماله أولاً، ثم ينوب عنه الولي في أدائه، ولا نريد بصحة بيعه ما ذكر حتى تكون عقلية، بل إن بيعه نافذ إذن الولي، وهذا مع حكم شرعي ،البتة فإن معناه أن بيعه بعد الإذن سبب للملك ، كبيع المكلف، ولا يتحقق هذا إلا بعد اعتبار الشارع ذلك ، ولو أورد بدلها كحركة بيعه وعدم نفاذه عند عدم الإذن لكان أدفع للشغب، فإذا الحق ما قال صدر الشريعة رحمه الله : الصواب خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل العبد .
The third investigation: The definition is refuted by the rulings on a child's acts, such as the recommendability of his prayer, the validity of his sale, and the obligation of financial rights against his estate (even if performed through the guardian). These are not related to the act of a mukallaf. Some Shafi‘i books answered that there is no speech (khitab) to the child at all, so his prayer is not recommendable; rather, the guardian is encouraged to encourage the child to pray for habituation, not for reward; reward is for the guardian, and payment of rights is obligatory on the guardian from the child's wealth, not primarily on the child. Validity is a rational matter, not a shar‘i ruling, because it consists in conformity of the particular to the legally recognized reality, and there is no need for speech. But there is much in this: denying reward to the child is very far and contradicts well-known hadiths; so the definition of 'recommendable' applies to his prayer; there is no room to deny its recommendability. The gloss says: the apparent is that reward follows perhaps by Allah's custom not to waste the reward of one who does good. It ends. It is not hidden from you that this is not sound from the Ash‘ari perspective, because good and evil are not recognized without speech; if there is no ruling from the Law, then this act is not good. But in our view, even if there is good without speech, rewarding the child is not of this type, because prophetic speech and approval have come regarding giving reward for children's acts. If you say: this speech is not a ruling; the ruling is only speech related to the act of a mukallaf. I say: this is blatant arbitrariness; pay no attention. So understand. As for financial rights, if they were not obligatory on the child, taking would be injustice; so financial rights like liability for damages are incumbent on his estate primarily, then the guardian acts for him in payment. We do not mean by validity of his sale what was mentioned that it is rational; rather, his sale is effective with the guardian's permission, and this is definitely a shar‘i ruling; meaning that his sale after permission is a cause of ownership, like the sale of a mukallaf, and this is not realized except by the Law's consideration. If one were to object that his sale's movement and non-effect without permission would be a stronger repulsion of quarreling. So the truth is what Sadr al-Shari‘ah said: the correct definition is 'Allah's speech related to the act of the servant'.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This dispute passage addresses the objection that children's acts (prayer, sale, liability) are subject to shar‘i rulings, yet children are not mukallaf. The Shafi‘i response denies these are actual shar‘i rulings: the child's prayer is not recommendable (mandub), only encouraged by the guardian; validity of sale is rational (conformity), not legal; financial obligations are on the guardian. The Hanafi commentator rejects this, noting that hadiths confirm reward for children; validity of sale requires legal effectiveness; imposing liability on the child's estate is real. Therefore, the definition of hukm must be inclusive enough to cover children's acts, as Sadr al-Shari‘ah's definition 'speech of Allah related to the act of the servant' (without 'mukallaf') is correct.

Key Terms
المندوب (al-mandub): ما يثاب على فعله ولا يعاقب على تركه
الصحة (al-sihha): كون الفعل موافقاً للحقيقة المعتبرة شرعاً مؤثراً في أحكامه
السبب (al-sabab): ما يكون وصفاً ظاهراً منضبطاً يجعله الشارع علامة على الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On what basis does the Hanafi commentator argue that a child's sale is a shar‘i ruling rather than a rational matter?
A) The child's sale requires no conditions.
B) The sale is a cause (sabab) of ownership only after the Lawgiver's consideration.
C) The child can buy and sell without any legal effect.
D) The guardian's permission is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The definition of hukm must be inclusive enough to cover children's acts, as evidenced by hadith on reward and the legal causality of their actions.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Five Basic Ahkam: Demand and Preference
Divides rulings into five types based on the nature of Shar'i demand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories of hukm based on the type of demand (iqtida') and classify any given act accordingly.

Core Idea
The hukm is determined by the nature of the Shar'i demand: determined, preferential, or permissive.

In this passage, the author of Fawatih al-Rahamut presents the foundational classification of rulings from the perspective of the demand (iqtida') inherent in the ruling. He outlines five categories: obligation, recommendation, prohibition, dislike, and permissibility, based on whether the demand is determined or preferential, and whether it commands action or refraining. Understanding this basic taxonomy is essential before moving to the more refined Hanafi distinction based on evidence strength.

Guided Path
1
Understand iqtida'
Identify the demand inherent in the ruling: hatm (determined), tarjih (preferential), or permissive (no demand).
Demand is of the essence of the ruling.
2
Determine object: action vs refraining
Specify whether the demand applies to performing an action (fi'l) or refraining (kaf).
Refraining itself can be demanded.
3
Map to five categories
Combine demand type and object: hatm + action = ijab; hatm + refraining = tahrim; tarjih + action = nadb; tarjih + refraining = karahah; no demand = ibahah.
Author corrects that hatm for refraining is also obligation (wajib) in a sense.
4
Note the refinement
The initial formulation 'ghayr kaf' (other than refraining) is corrected; refraining is included.
Refraining from haram is still a determined demand.
5
Apply to example
Practice classifying texts by identifying the demand type and deriving the hukm.
Use Quran and hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم (الاقتضاء) الذي في الحكم إن كان حتماً لفعل غير كف فالإيجاب) أي فالحكم الإيجاب، وعلى هذا يلزم أن لا يكون الكف عن الحرام واجباً، ولا يصلح للدخول في باقي الأقسام فيختل الحصر، فالصواب أن لا يفيد بغير الكف (وهو نفس الأمر النفسي وهو ظاهر عند كون الأمر النفسي مدلول اللفظي (أو) إن كان (ترجيحاً) لفعل، (فالندب) أي فالحكم الندب (أو) إن كان (حتماً لكف الفعل فالتحريم) أي فالحكم التحريم بالقياس إلى المكفوف عنه وإن كان إيجاباً بالقياس إلى نفسه المطلوب فتدبر أحسن التدبر (أو) إن كان (ترجيحاً) لكف (فالكراهة) أي فالحكم الكراهة والتخيير (الإباحة أي الحكم بالتخيير الإباحة (والحنفية) لما وجدوا أحكام ما ثبت بدليل قطعي مخالفة لما ثبت بظني ( لاحظوا) في التقسيم (حال) الدال في الطلب الحتمي لأنه العمدة في الباب (فقالوا: إن ثبت الطلب الجازم بقطعي فالافتراض إن كان ذلك الطلب للفعل أو التحريم) إن كان ذلك للكف (أو) ثبت الطلب الجازم بظني (فالإيجاب إن كان ذلك الطلب للفعل وكراهة التحريم) إن كان ذلك للكف، فالأحكام إذن سبعة، فقد بان لك أن النزاع بيننا وبين الشافعية ليس إلا في التسمية لا في المعنى، فلا وجه لما شمر الذيل صاحب المحصول لإبطال قولنا ومن زعم من الشافعية أن النزاع معنوي في أن الافتراض في كلام الشارع على أيهما يحمل فقد غلط، كيف وإن النصوص كلها كانت قطعية في زمن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، والظن إنما نشأ من بعد ذلك الزمان،
Then, the demand (iqtida') in the ruling: if it is a determined demand for an action other than refraining, then it is obligation (ijab); i.e., the ruling is obligation. According to this, it would follow that refraining from the forbidden would not be obligatory, and it would not be suitable for entering the remaining categories, thus the enumeration would be compromised. The correct view is that it is not restricted to other than refraining. (And it is the intrinsic command; this is apparent when the intrinsic command is the meaning of the verbal command.) Or if it is a preference for an action, then it is recommendation (nadb); i.e., the ruling is recommendation. Or if it is a determined demand for refraining from an action, then it is prohibition (tahrim); i.e., the ruling is prohibition in relation to the act refrained from, even though it is obligation in relation to the required act itself—so reflect well. Or if it is a preference for refraining, then it is dislike (karahah); i.e., the ruling is dislike. And permissibility (ibahah) i.e., the ruling of choice is permissibility. The Hanafis, since they found that rulings established by decisive (qat'i) evidence differ from those established by probable (zanni) evidence, considered in the division the status of the evidence in the determined demand because it is the main pillar in this topic. So they said: If the determined demand is established by decisive evidence, then it is fard (obligatory-in-the-strong-sense) if that demand is for an action, or prohibition (tahrim) if it is for refraining. Or if the determined demand is established by probable evidence, then it is wajib (obligatory-in-the-general-sense) if that demand is for an action, and prohibition-akin-to-dislike (karahah tahrimiyyah) if for refraining. Thus the rulings are seven. So it has become clear to you that the dispute between us and the Shafi'is is only terminological, not in meaning. There is no justification for the author of al-Mahsul [al-Razi] to exert effort to refute our view. And whoever among the Shafi'is claims that the dispute is substantive—concerning whether fard (as used in the Lawgiver's speech) is to be interpreted as this or that—has erred. How so? Because all the texts were decisive at the time of the Prophet (PBUH); probability only arose after that time.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This is a definition passage. The author begins by presenting the five-fold division of ahkam based on the nature of the demand (iqtida') in the ruling: determined demand for an action = obligation (ijab); preference for action = recommendation (nadb); determined demand for refraining = prohibition (tahrim); preference for refraining = dislike (karahah); no demand = permissibility (ibahah). The author notes a refinement: the initial phrasing would wrongly exclude forbidding (haram) from being wajib (obligatory) in the sense of demanding refraining, so the correct formulation is not to restrict to 'other than refraining'. Then the Hanafi extension is explained: looking at the certainty (qat'i or zanni) of the evidence (dalil) that establishes the determined demand, they split the obligation category into fard (qat'i) and wajib (zanni), and split the prohibition category into tahrim (qat'i) and karahah tahrimiyyah (zanni). Thus Hanafis have seven categories: fard, wajib, nadb, haram, makruh tahrimi, makruh tanzihi, mubah. The author emphasizes that the disagreement with the Shafi'is, who generally maintain five categories (fard or wajib synonymously), is merely terminological—not substantive. He criticizes al-Razi's al-Mahsul for attempting to refute the Hanafi position, noting that at the Prophet's time all texts were decisive, and probability only emerged later; thus the distinction is valid for post-revelation jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): The demand inherent in the legal ruling, whether determined or preferential.
الحتم (al-ḥatm): Determined, decisive demand; contrasts with tarjih (preference).
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): Preference; a non-binding recommendation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the five-fold division in the passage, to which category does a 'determined demand for refraining' belong?
A) Ibahah
B) Nadb
C) Tahrim/Haram
D) Karahah

Key Takeaway
The five basic ahkam are defined by the type of demand (determined, preferential, none) and the object (action, refraining).

The Hanafi Seven and the Terminological Dispute
Expands the five rulings into seven by considering evidence certainty and addresses the intra-madhhab dispute with Shafi'is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi seven categories of rulings, explain the rationale for distinguishing fard from wajib, and describe the nature of the dispute with the Shafi'is as terminological.

Core Idea
Hanafis split obligation and prohibition each into two subtypes based on the certainty of the evidence, yielding seven distinct rulings.

Building on the five-fold classification, the Hanafi school introduces a crucial refinement: they distinguish between rulings established by decisive (qat'i) evidence and those established by probable (zanni) evidence. This creates two levels of obligation—fard and wajib—and two levels of prohibition—haram and makruh tahrimi. The passage explains that this expansion is not a disagreement in substance but in terminology, much of it rooted in the historical transmission of texts.

Guided Path
1
Recall the five basic categories
Review the five-fold division from the first part: ijab, nadb, tahrim, karahah, ibahah.
These are based solely on demand type.
2
Understand the Hanafi addition: evidence strength
Note that for determined demands (hatm), the Hanafis look at the certainty of the evidence (qat'i vs zanni).
This is the key innovation.
3
Map the seven categories
When hatm is for action: qat'i = fard, zanni = wajib. When hatm is for refraining: qat'i = haram, zanni = makruh tahrimi. Nadb and karahah remain; ibahah added. Total seven.
Makruh tanzihi is the karahah from preferential demand.
4
Analyze the dispute
Shafi'is do not split; they call both fard. The author argues the disagreement is terminological, not substantive.
The dispute arose from post-revelation transmission probability.
5
Apply the framework
Classify a sample ruling (e.g., witr prayer) as fard, wajib, haram, etc., based on evidence.
Consider the practical effect on fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم (الاقتضاء) الذي في الحكم إن كان حتماً لفعل غير كف فالإيجاب) أي فالحكم الإيجاب، وعلى هذا يلزم أن لا يكون الكف عن الحرام واجباً، ولا يصلح للدخول في باقي الأقسام فيختل الحصر، فالصواب أن لا يفيد بغير الكف (وهو نفس الأمر النفسي وهو ظاهر عند كون الأمر النفسي مدلول اللفظي (أو) إن كان (ترجيحاً) لفعل، (فالندب) أي فالحكم الندب (أو) إن كان (حتماً لكف الفعل فالتحريم) أي فالحكم التحريم بالقياس إلى المكفوف عنه وإن كان إيجاباً بالقياس إلى نفسه المطلوب فتدبر أحسن التدبر (أو) إن كان (ترجيحاً) لكف (فالكراهة) أي فالحكم الكراهة والتخيير (الإباحة أي الحكم بالتخيير الإباحة (والحنفية) لما وجدوا أحكام ما ثبت بدليل قطعي مخالفة لما ثبت بظني ( لاحظوا) في التقسيم (حال) الدال في الطلب الحتمي لأنه العمدة في الباب (فقالوا: إن ثبت الطلب الجازم بقطعي فالافتراض إن كان ذلك الطلب للفعل أو التحريم) إن كان ذلك للكف (أو) ثبت الطلب الجازم بظني (فالإيجاب إن كان ذلك الطلب للفعل وكراهة التحريم) إن كان ذلك للكف، فالأحكام إذن سبعة، فقد بان لك أن النزاع بيننا وبين الشافعية ليس إلا في التسمية لا في المعنى، فلا وجه لما شمر الذيل صاحب المحصول لإبطال قولنا ومن زعم من الشافعية أن النزاع معنوي في أن الافتراض في كلام الشارع على أيهما يحمل فقد غلط، كيف وإن النصوص كلها كانت قطعية في زمن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم، والظن إنما نشأ من بعد ذلك الزمان،
Then, the demand (iqtida') in the ruling: if it is a determined demand for an action other than refraining, then it is obligation (ijab); i.e., the ruling is obligation. According to this, it would follow that refraining from the forbidden would not be obligatory, and it would not be suitable for entering the remaining categories, thus the enumeration would be compromised. The correct view is that it is not restricted to other than refraining. (And it is the intrinsic command; this is apparent when the intrinsic command is the meaning of the verbal command.) Or if it is a preference for an action, then it is recommendation (nadb); i.e., the ruling is recommendation. Or if it is a determined demand for refraining from an action, then it is prohibition (tahrim); i.e., the ruling is prohibition in relation to the act refrained from, even though it is obligation in relation to the required act itself—so reflect well. Or if it is a preference for refraining, then it is dislike (karahah); i.e., the ruling is dislike. And permissibility (ibahah) i.e., the ruling of choice is permissibility. The Hanafis, since they found that rulings established by decisive (qat'i) evidence differ from those established by probable (zanni) evidence, considered in the division the status of the evidence in the determined demand because it is the main pillar in this topic. So they said: If the determined demand is established by decisive evidence, then it is fard (obligatory-in-the-strong-sense) if that demand is for an action, or prohibition (tahrim) if it is for refraining. Or if the determined demand is established by probable evidence, then it is wajib (obligatory-in-the-general-sense) if that demand is for an action, and prohibition-akin-to-dislike (karahah tahrimiyyah) if for refraining. Thus the rulings are seven. So it has become clear to you that the dispute between us and the Shafi'is is only terminological, not in meaning. There is no justification for the author of al-Mahsul [al-Razi] to exert effort to refute our view. And whoever among the Shafi'is claims that the dispute is substantive—concerning whether fard (as used in the Lawgiver's speech) is to be interpreted as this or that—has erred. How so? Because all the texts were decisive at the time of the Prophet (PBUH); probability only arose after that time.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This is a definition passage. The author begins by presenting the five-fold division of ahkam based on the nature of the demand (iqtida') in the ruling: determined demand for an action = obligation (ijab); preference for action = recommendation (nadb); determined demand for refraining = prohibition (tahrim); preference for refraining = dislike (karahah); no demand = permissibility (ibahah). The author notes a refinement: the initial phrasing would wrongly exclude forbidding (haram) from being wajib (obligatory) in the sense of demanding refraining, so the correct formulation is not to restrict to 'other than refraining'. Then the Hanafi extension is explained: looking at the certainty (qat'i or zanni) of the evidence (dalil) that establishes the determined demand, they split the obligation category into fard (qat'i) and wajib (zanni), and split the prohibition category into tahrim (qat'i) and karahah tahrimiyyah (zanni). Thus Hanafis have seven categories: fard, wajib, nadb, haram, makruh tahrimi, makruh tanzihi, mubah. The author emphasizes that the disagreement with the Shafi'is, who generally maintain five categories (fard or wajib synonymously), is merely terminological—not substantive. He criticizes al-Razi's al-Mahsul for attempting to refute the Hanafi position, noting that at the Prophet's time all texts were decisive, and probability only emerged later; thus the distinction is valid for post-revelation jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الافتراض (al-iftirāḍ): The legal category of obligation established by decisive (qat'i) evidence; equivalent to fard.
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The legal category of obligation established by probable (zanni) evidence; the more general term for obligation.
كراهة التحريم (karāhat al-taḥrīm): The category of prohibition based on zanni evidence; a lighter form than haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi seven-fold classification is a logical extension of the basic five, incorporating the probability of evidence, and the dispute with the Shafi'is is terminological, not substantive.

Types of Declaratory Ruling: Cause, Preventer, Condition
Classification of wad'i rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of wad'i rulings (sabab, mani', shart) in Hanafi usul and apply each to a fiqh example.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a cause triggers a ruling, a preventer blocks it, and a condition enables it.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut classifies the declaratory (wad'i) rulings that underpin Islamic legal theory. Understanding causation (sabab), prevention (mani'), and condition (shart) is essential for constructing sound legal arguments. We will walk through each type with its subtypes and concrete fiqh examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three wad'i categories
Identify sabab, mani', and shart from the passage. Notice that each is a ‘description’ (wasf) that the Lawgiver has linked to a ruling in a specific way.
These are not the same as the taklifi rulings (obligation, prohibition, etc.); they are the underlying building blocks.
2
Analyze the subtypes of sabab
A cause can be temporal (waqti), like noon for the obligation of prayer, or essential (ma'nawi), like intoxication for prohibition. Note the proof for each: Quran for noon, hadith for intoxication.
The existence of the cause does not always guarantee the ruling; preventers or missing conditions may intervene.
3
Evaluate the two types of mani'
A preventer can block the ruling itself (mani' al-hukm) while the cause remains intact (fatherhood in qisas), or it can block the effectiveness of the cause (mani' al-sabab) as debt blocks the nisab from being a cause for zakat.
This distinction is critical for correct legal reasoning.
4
Apply the condition subtypes
A condition may be attached to the ruling (shart al-hukm), like ability to deliver for sale validity, or to the cause (shart al-sabab), like purity for the cause of prayer (glorification).
The absence of a condition prevents the ruling or cause from being effective, but its presence does not necessitate the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأحكام، ولما فرغ عن تقسيم الاقتضاء والتخيير أن الخطاب التكليفي شرع في تقسيم الوضعي فقال : (ثم خطاب الوضع أصناف منها : الحكم على الوصف بالسببية) أي بكونه سبباً لحكم (وهي بالاستقراء وقتية إن كان السبب وقتاً كالدلوك) أي الزوال (لوجوب الصلاة) لقوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِمِ الصَّلَوَةَ لِدُلُوكِ الشَّمْسِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ۷۸] (ومعنوية) إن لم يكن وقتاً كالإسكار للتحريم) لقوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام : كل مسكر حرام» رواه مسلم (ومنها: الحكم بكونه مانعاً إما للحكم) فقط بقاء السبب على السببية كالأبوة في القصاص) فإنها مع منعت القصاص مع وجود السبب وهو القتل ظلماً أو للسبب كالدين في الزكاة فإنه منع النصاب عن كونه سبباً، فإن أداءه حاجة أصلية والنصاب صار مشغولاً، فلم يبق فاضلاً مغنياً حتى يكون مفضياً إلى جواب الإغناء، فقد اتضح الفرق بين هذا والأبوة، فإن الشرع جعل في باب الزكاة النصاب المغني سبباً، وأما القصاص فالسبب فيه القتل العمد العدوان، وإنما تخلف الحكم في البعض لمانع فافهم ومنها : الحكم بكونه شرطاً للحكم، كالقدرة على تسليم المبيع للبيع أي صحته وهي حكم (أو للسبب كالطهارة شرطت في الصلاة، وسببها تعظيم الباري تعالى وقد شرطت الطهارة فيها لأجله، لأن التعظيم يفقد مع فقدان الطهارة هذا
The second article on rulings. And when he finished the division of demand and choice that the obligating address is, he began to divide the declaratory address and said: 'Then the declaratory address has types, among them: the ruling that a description is a cause, i.e., being a cause for a ruling. And it, by induction, is temporal if the cause is a time, like the declining (of the sun) i.e., noon, for the obligation of prayer, due to His statement: 'Establish prayer at the declining of the sun' [al-Isra: 78]; and it is essential if it is not a time, like intoxication for prohibition, due to his statement, peace be upon him and his family and companions: 'Every intoxicant is unlawful' narrated by Muslim. And among them: the ruling that it is a preventer: either of the ruling, just keeping the cause on its causality, like fatherhood in retaliation, for it prevents retaliation while the cause exists, which is unjust murder; or of the cause, like debt in zakat, for it prevents the nisab from being a cause, because its payment is a primary need and the nisab becomes occupied, so it is not surplus satisfying so as to lead to the response of enrichment. Thus the difference between this and fatherhood has become clear, for the law made the enriching nisab a cause in the chapter of zakat; while for retaliation, the cause is intentional transgressive murder, and the ruling is only omitted in some cases due to a preventer, so understand. And among them: the ruling that it is a condition: either of the ruling, like the ability to deliver the sold item for the sale, i.e., its validity, which is a ruling; or of the cause, like purity is conditioned in prayer, and its cause is the glorification of Allah the Exalted, and purity has been conditioned in it for its sake, because glorification is lost with the loss of purity. This.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the types of declaratory (wad'i) rulings as classified in Hanafi usul. The author explains three main types: (1) Causality (sababiyya): a description is declared a cause of a ruling. Causes are either temporal, like the time of noon (zawal) which is the cause for the obligation of Zuhr prayer, supported by Quran; or essential, like intoxication which is the cause for prohibition, supported by hadith. (2) Preventer (mani'): a description that prevents a ruling or a cause. Two subtypes: (a) preventer of the ruling while the cause remains, like fatherhood in qisas: the cause (murder) exists but fatherhood prevents the ruling of retaliation; (b) preventer of the cause itself, like debt in zakat: debt prevents the nisab from being a sufficient cause for zakat because the nisab is occupied with a primary need. (3) Condition (shart): a description that is a condition. Two subtypes: (a) condition of the ruling, like the ability to deliver the sold item is a condition for the validity (ruling) of the sale; (b) condition of the cause, like purity (tahara) is a condition for the cause of prayer (glorification); without purity, the glorification is unattainable. The author emphasizes the practical difference between preventer of the ruling and preventer of the cause with clear fiqh examples.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause; a description that the Lawgiver has made a sign for the existence of a ruling.
مانع (mani'): A preventer; a description that prevents either a ruling or the effectiveness of a cause.
شرط (shart): A condition; something whose absence prevents the ruling or the cause, but whose presence does not necessitate the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi classification, how does a preventer (mani') of the ruling differ from a preventer of the cause?
A) There is no difference; both prevent the ruling.
B) A preventer of the ruling removes the cause itself, while a preventer of the cause only removes the ruling.
C) A preventer of the ruling keeps the cause intact but prevents the ruling from arising, while a preventer of the cause prevents the cause from being effective.
D) A preventer of the ruling applies only to conditions, not to causes.

Key Takeaway
Declaratory rulings in Hanafi usul classify legal causes, conditions, and preventers — each with distinct fiqh implications.

The Hanafi Definition of Wajib and the Issue of Punishment Desert
Definition of wajib with theological and legal refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of wajib, distinguish between rational and customary desert of punishment, and explain how the Hanafi school reconciles divine threat with pardon.

Core Idea
Wajib is what deserves punishment for abandonment; desert is customary, not rational.

In this passage, the author delves into the definition of wajib (obligatory act) with precision, addressing key theological and legal questions. The definition hinges on whether punishment is deserved rationally or by custom, a dispute between Mu'tazila and Ash'aris. The author also tackles the apparent conflict between God's threat of punishment and His pardon, offering a nuanced solution that preserves both divine truthfulness and mercy. Understanding this definition is foundational for Hanafi usul, as it frames the nature of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of wajib
Locate the statement 'ما استحق تاركه العقاب' (that whose abandoner deserves punishment). Note that the definition includes two possible bases: rational (istihqaq 'aqli) or customary (istihqaq 'adi).
The author does not prefer one over the other; both are presented.
2
Distinguish between rational and customary desert
Rational desert is held by the Mu'tazila (based on inherent good and evil). Customary desert is held by the Ash'aris (based on divine prescription). Recognize that the Hanafi position aligns with Ash'ari in denying rational desert.
This distinction affects the basis of obligation: reason vs. revelation.
3
Understand the addition 'in all of its time'
The phrase 'تاركه في جميع وقته' is added to include the expanded (musawwa') wajib, which has a broad time frame. The author deems it unnecessary, as the basic definition suffices.
This shows precision in defining scope.
4
Analyze the objection regarding punishing non-existence
An objection: punishing for non-existence is invalid because the act does not exist. The author counters by saying refraining from the act is a positive state that can be punished, so desert applies to the failure to perform.
This establishes that obligation is on the act, not its existence.
5
Examine the reconciliation of threat and pardon
The threat (wa'id) is a report (khabar), thus truthful. Pardon ('afw) does not break the threat because the threat is conditioned on the absence of pardon. The alternative view (threat as construction) is rejected as a departure from the literal meaning without cause.
This preserves both God's truthfulness and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن نشرع في مسائل الأحكام ولنقدم عليها تعريف الواجب لما فيه من الشغب، وإن كان علم سابقاً في ضمن التقسيم (وهو ما استحق تاركه العقاب استحقاقاً عقلياً) كما عليه قائلو الحسن والقبيح العقليين ( أو ) استحقاقاً عادياً) كما عليه الأشعرية، وزيد تاركه في جميع وقته ليدخل الموسع ، وقيل تاركه في جميع وقته بوجه ليدخل الموسع والكفائي ولا حاجة إليهما، ويكفي ما في المتن فتدبر، وما قيل إنه لا استحقاق العقاب بالعدم لأنه غير مقدور، وإن يصح ء أن أريد الكف عن الفعل للزم أن يكون التارك الغير الكاف لا يستحق العقاب، ففيه ما سيجيء عدم المقدور وإن كان في نفسه غير مقدور يوجب استحقاق العقاب فإن قيل : فعلى هذا يلزم عدم صحة العفو قلنا : كلا ( والعفو) لمستحق العقاب من الكرم فلا ينافي الاستحقاق والصلوح (وقيل: ما أوعد بالعقاب على تركه ولا يخرج العفو، لأن الخلف في الوعيد (جائز) يوعد بالعقاب ولا يأتي به فإن أهل العقول السليمة يعدونه فضلاً لا نقصاً، وهو مروي عن عبد الله بن عباس رضي الله عنهما (دون الوعد فإن الخلف فيه نقص مستحيل عليه سبحانه (ورد) هذا العذر بأن إيعاد الله تعالى خبر فهو صادق قطعاً لاستحالة الكذب هناك، واعتذر بأن كونه خبراً ممنوع بل هو إنشاء للتخويف، فلا بأس حينئذ في الخلف، ورده بقوله : ( وتجويز كونه إنشاء للتخويف كما قيل في حواشي ميرزاجان وغيرها (عدول عن الحقيقة بلا موجب) يلجيء إلى العدول وهو غير جائز على أن مثله يجري في الوعد) إذ يمكن أن يقال إنه لإنشاء الترغيب فيجوز فيه الخلف فينسد) باب المعاد هذا خلف (أقول) ثالثاً إنه (لو تم) تجويز الإنشائية لدل) على بطلان العفو مطلقاً لأنه التجاوز عمن يستحق المؤاخذة، وعلى هذا ليس المؤاخذة موعودة (والكلام) كان في خروجه بعد تسليم وجوده وإذا لم يتم العذر بتجويز الخلف فلا بد أن يقال) في العذر إن الإيعاد في كلامه تعالى مقيد بعدم العفو) فلا خلف ولا فيجوز أن
And now we proceed to the issues of rulings, and we preface them with the definition of wajib due to the controversy in it, although it was previously known in the division. (It is that whose abandoner deserves punishment, either by rational desert, as held by those who believe in rational good and evil, or by customary desert, as held by the Ash'aris. And it was added: 'its abandoner in all of its time' to include the expanded (musawwa'). And it was said: 'its abandoner in all of its time in a certain respect' to include the expanded and the collective (kifa'i), but there is no need for both, and what is in the text suffices, so reflect. And what was said that there is no desert of punishment for non-existence because it is not in one's power, if what is meant is refraining from the act, then it would follow that the one who does not refrain does not deserve punishment. In it is what will come: non-existence of the possible, even if it is in itself not possible, causes desert of punishment. If it is said: on this, the validity of pardon would be negated, we say: No! And pardon for the one who deserves punishment is from generosity, so it does not contradict desert and suitability. And it was said: 'What is threatened with punishment for its abandonment' and pardon does not exclude it, because breaking a threat is permissible; He threatens with punishment and does not inflict it, for people of sound intellect consider it a virtue, not a deficiency. And this is reported from Abdullah ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both). Unlike the promise, for breaking it is a deficiency and impossible for Him. This excuse was rebutted by saying: The threat of Allah is a report, so it is truthful definitively due to the impossibility of lying regarding Him. And it was excused by saying: It being a report is denied; rather, it is a construction for intimidation, so there is no problem in breaking it. And he rebutted with his statement: 'And allowing it to be a construction for intimidation, as said in the glosses of Mirzajan and others, is a departure from the literal meaning without cause, which compels departure, and it is not permissible. Moreover, the same would apply to the promise, for it could be said that it is for construction of encouragement, so breaking it would be permissible, thus the door of return would be closed.' This is a fallacy. I say thirdly: If the permission of construction were valid, it would indicate the invalidity of pardon absolutely, because it is forgiveness of one who deserves punishment, and on this, the punishment is not threatened. The discussion was about its exclusion after admitting its existence. And if the excuse by allowing the break is not valid, then it must be said in the excuse that the threat in the speech of Allah is conditioned by the non-existence of pardon, so there is no break and it is permissible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of wajib (obligatory) and addresses key objections. The author states that wajib is that which deserves punishment for its abandonment, whether by rational desert (as per the Mu'tazila) or by customary desert (as per the Ash'aris). The addition 'in all of its time' is to include the expanded (musawwa') wajib, but not strictly necessary. Then the text discusses two main issues: (1) The objection that punishing for non-existence is invalid because the act is not existent. The author argues that even non-existent acts can deserve punishment through the concept of 'refraining' from the act. (2) The issue of whether divine pardon contradicts the desert of punishment. The author defends that pardon does not negate desert; it is a generous act. Then there is a debate about whether the threat (wa'id) of punishment can be broken (khalaf) without contradiction. Some argue that threats are not promises, so breaking them is permissible. But the author rebuts that God's threat is a report (khabar) and thus truthful, and interpreting it as a construction (insha) for intimidation is a departure from the literal meaning without cause, which is not permissible. Moreover, if allowed, the same could be said for promises, breaking which is impossible for God. The passage ends with the author's own argument that the threat is conditioned on the absence of pardon, so no break occurs. This is a standard Hanafi position that maintains both the efficacy of divine pardon and the truthfulness of threats.

Key Terms
واجب (wajib): That whose abandoner deserves punishment (by reason or custom).
استحقاق عقلي (istihqaq 'aqli): Rational desert of punishment, based on the inherent goodness or evil of acts.
وعيد (wa'id): Threat of punishment for disobedience.
عفو ('afw): Pardon; exemption from deserved punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how is the divine threat (wa'id) of punishment reconciled with the possibility of pardon ('afw)?
A) Pardon is only for the unworthy, so it does not apply to those who deserve punishment.
B) The threat is a construction (insha) for intimidation, so breaking it is permissible.
C) The threat is a report (khabar) and thus truthful, but it is conditioned on the absence of pardon, so no contradiction.
D) The threat is not literal but metaphorical, so pardon can override it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of wajib centers on desert of punishment, which is customary (not rational), and divine pardon does not contradict the threat because the threat is conditioned on non-pardon.

Types of Declaratory Rulings: Cause, Preventer, Condition
Defining three types of wad'i rulings with evidence and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of declaratory rulings (sabab, mani', shart) and distinguish them using Hanafi usul criteria.

Core Idea
Causes trigger rulings, preventers block them, conditions enable them — each has a distinct legal role.

The Lawgiver addresses us not only through commands and prohibitions but also through declaratory rulings (ahkam wad'iya) that define the structure of legal obligations. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explains three fundamental categories: cause (sabab), preventer (mani'), and condition (shart). Understanding these is essential for correctly applying rulings to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the list: sabab (cause), mani' (preventer), shart (condition). Each is a distinct type of wad'i ruling.
2
Distinguish sabab from shart
A sabab triggers the ruling directly (e.g., time triggers prayer). A shart is a prerequisite for validity (e.g., purity for prayer). The cause may exist without the condition.
3
Analyze the two kinds of mani'
A preventer can block the ruling itself (like paternity blocking qisas) or block the cause from operating (like debt blocking nisab from being a cause for zakah).
Key: in blocking the ruling, the cause remains; in blocking the cause, the cause's effectiveness is removed.
4
Examine the dalil for each
The author cites Quran 17:78 for time as cause and the hadith 'every intoxicant is unlawful' for conceptual cause. The distinction between preventer types is illustrated with fiqh examples.
5
Apply the definitions to new cases
Given a new description (e.g., travel as cause for qasr), determine whether it is a sabab, mani', or shart based on the criteria learned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأحكام الغيوب، ولما فرغ عن تقسيم الاقتضاء والتخيير أن الخطاب التكليفي شرع في تقسيم الوضعي فقال : (ثم خطاب الوضع أصناف منها : الحكم على الوصف بالسببية) أي بكونه سبباً لحكم (وهي بالاستقراء وقتية إن كان السبب وقتاً كالدلوك) أي الزوال (لوجوب الصلاة) لقوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِمِ الصَّلَوَةَ لِدُلُوكِ الشَّمْسِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ۷۸] (ومعنوية) إن لم يكن وقتاً كالإسكار للتحريم) لقوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام : كل مسكر حرام» رواه مسلم (ومنها: الحكم بكونه مانعاً إما للحكم) فقط بقاء السبب على السببية كالأبوة في القصاص) فإنها مع منعت القصاص مع وجود السبب وهو القتل ظلماً أو للسبب كالدين في الزكاة فإنه منع النصاب عن كونه سبباً، فإن أداءه حاجة أصلية والنصاب صار مشغولاً، فلم يبق فاضلاً مغنياً حتى يكون مفضياً إلى جواب الإغناء، فقد اتضح الفرق بين هذا والأبوة، فإن الشرع جعل في باب الزكاة النصاب المغني سبباً، وأما القصاص فالسبب فيه القتل العمد العدوان، وإنما تخلف الحكم في البعض لمانع فافهم ومنها : الحكم بكونه شرطاً للحكم، كالقدرة على تسليم المبيع للبيع أي صحته وهي حكم (أو للسبب كالطهارة شرطت في الصلاة، وسببها تعظيم الباري تعالى وقد شرطت الطهارة فيها لأجله
The second discourse on rulings. Having finished the division of demand and choice in the taklifi address, he began dividing the wad'i address, saying: 'Then the wad'i address has types, among them: the judgment upon a description as being a cause' i.e., it being a cause for a ruling. 'And it is, by induction, temporal if the cause is a time, like the declining of the sun' i.e., its zenith 'for the obligation of prayer' due to His statement: 'Establish prayer at the declining of the sun' [17:78], 'and conceptual' if it is not a time, like intoxication for prohibition due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicant is unlawful' narrated by Muslim. 'And among them: the judgment of it being a preventer, either of the ruling' merely preserving the cause's causality, like paternity in retaliation for it prevents retaliation with the existence of the cause, which is unjust killing, 'or of the cause' like debt in zakah, for it prevents the nisab from being a cause, because fulfilling it is an original need and the nisab becomes occupied, so it no longer remains surplus and enriching such that it leads to the answer of enrichment. So the difference between this and paternity has become clear, for the Lawgiver in the chapter of zakah made the enriching nisab a cause, while in retaliation the cause is intentional killing with transgression, and the ruling is only postponed in some cases due to a preventer, so understand. 'And among them: the judgment of it being a condition for the ruling' like the ability to deliver the sold item for the sale, i.e., its validity, which is a ruling, 'or for the cause' like purity was made a condition in prayer, and its cause is magnifying the High God, and purity was made a condition therein for its sake, because magnification is lost with the loss of purity.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the three types of declaratory rulings (ahkam wad'iya): cause (sabab), preventer (mani'), and condition (shart). The author first explains the cause, giving examples: time as a cause for obligatory prayer (citing Quran 17:78), and intoxication as a cause for prohibition (citing the hadith 'every intoxicant is unlawful'). Causes are divided into temporal and conceptual. Then he explains the preventer, distinguishing between a preventer of the ruling itself (like paternity in qisas, which prevents retaliation despite the cause being present) and a preventer of the cause (like debt in zakah, which prevents the wealth from being a cause for zakah because it is needed for basic needs). Finally, he explains the condition, giving the example of the ability to deliver as a condition for the validity of a sale, and purity as a condition for the validity of prayer. The commentary emphasizes the Hanafi understanding that these categories are distinct and have specific legal effects.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A description that the Lawgiver has made a cause for a ruling, either temporal (like time for prayer) or conceptual (like intoxication for prohibition).
مانع (mani'): A preventer that either blocks the ruling itself while the cause remains (like paternity in qisas) or blocks the cause from operating (like debt in zakah).
شرط (shart): A condition that is required for the correctness of the ruling (like ability to deliver for sale) or for the correctness of the cause (like purity for prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a preventer (mani') in Hanafi fiqh?
A) The time of Zuhr for prayer
B) Fatherhood in qisas
C) Ability to deliver the sold item
D) Purity for prayer

Key Takeaway
Understanding the distinct roles of sabab, mani', and shart is essential for correctly deriving and applying rulings in Hanafi jurisprudence.

The Definition of Wajib and the Rational Basis of Punishment
Defining wajib and discussing the nature of punishment and pardon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of wajib, distinguishing between rational and conventional desert of punishment, and analyze the argument that Allah's threat of punishment can be broken through pardon.

Core Idea
Wajib is defined by desert of punishment, but pardon is possible without breaking Allah's truthfulness.

This passage tackles the foundational definition of wajib and then dives into a theological dispute: is the punishment for abandoning a wajib a rational necessity or a conventional decree? The answer shapes how we understand divine threats and the possibility of pardon. We will work through the Hanafi position that punishment is rationally deserved, yet pardon remains an act of generosity that does not undermine the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Define wajib and identify the two bases of punishment desert
The passage opens by defining wajib as an act which, if abandoned, merits punishment. It then presents two competing views: rational desert (istihqaq 'aqli, held by Mu'tazila and Hanafis) and conventional desert (istihqaq 'adi, held by Ash'aris). Identify the exact phrasing that signals each view.
The phrase 'استحقاقاً عقلياً' vs 'استحقاقاً عادياً' is the key distinction.
2
Analyze the debate on pardon and the threat (wa'id)
The text examines whether pardon contradicts the desert of punishment. Some argue that if Allah threatens punishment, then pardoning would be breaking the threat, which is impermissible. Others claim breaking a threat is permissible, citing a hadith from Ibn Abbas. Follow the arguments and counter-arguments: is the threat a report (khabar) or a creative speech (insha')?
Note the distinction between wa'd (promise) and wa'id (threat): promises must be kept, threats may be broken.
3
Evaluate the resolution: conditioning the threat on no pardon
The author ultimately resolves the issue by stating that the threat is conditioned upon no pardon, and this departure from the literal meaning is justified by the certain knowledge that Allah pardons major sinners among believers. Identify the two options for interpreting threats for non-polytheists: either conditioning or creative threat.
The passage concludes that for polytheists, threats remain literal; for others, they are reinterpreted to allow pardon.
4
Extract the Hanafi position on the matter
From the arguments and preferred resolution, deduce the Hanafi stance: punishment is deserved rationally, but pardon is a grace that does not negate the desert, and divine threats are either conditioned or creative to accommodate mercy.
The text sides with the rational view, as the possibility of pardon is an established certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

استحقاقاً عادياً) كما عليه الأشعرية، وزيد تاركه في جميع وقته ليدخل الموسع ، وقيل تاركه في جميع وقته بوجه ليدخل الموسع والكفائي ولا حاجة إليهما، ويكفي ما في المتن فتدبر، وما قيل إنه لا استحقاق العقاب بالعدم لأنه غير مقدور، وإن يصح ء أن أريد الكف عن الفعل للزم أن يكون التارك الغير الكاف لا يستحق العقاب، ففيه ما سيجيء عدم المقدور وإن كان في نفسه غير مقدور يوجب استحقاق العقاب فإن قيل : فعلى هذا يلزم عدم صحة العفو قلنا : كلا ( والعفو) لمستحق العقاب من الكرم فلا ينافي الاستحقاق والصلوح (وقيل: ما أوعد بالعقاب على تركه ولا يخرج العفو، لأن الخلف في الوعيد (جائز) يوعد بالعقاب ولا يأتي به فإن أهل العقول واعتذر بأن كونه خبراً ممنوع بل هو إنشاء للتخويف، فلا بأس حينئذ في الخلف، ورده بقوله : ( وتجويز كونه إنشاء للتخويف كما قيل في حواشي ميرزاجان وغيرها (عدول عن الحقيقة بلا موجب) يلجيء إلى العدول وهو غير جائز على أن مثله يجري في الوعد) إذ يمكن أن يقال إنه لإنشاء الترغيب فيجوز فيه الخلف فينسد) باب المعاد هذا خلف (أقول) ثالثاً إنه (لو تم) تجويز الإنشائية لدل) على بطلان العفو مطلقاً لأنه التجاوز عمن يستحق المؤاخذة، وعلى هذا ليس المؤاخذة موعودة (والكلام) كان في خروجه بعد تسليم وجوده وإذا لم يتم العذر بتجويز الخلف فلا بد أن يقال) في العذر إن الإيعاد في كلامه تعالى مقيد بعدم العفو) فلا خلف ولا الكبائر الغير المشركين ثبوتاً قطعياً جلياً، مثل الشمس على نصف النهار، فلا بد من العدول عن الظاهر في الوعيدات التي لغير الكفرة، فإما بالتقييد أو جعله لإنشاء التخويف، وأما الوعد فلا موجب فيه فيبقى على الحقيقة، وما قال : فليس بشيء، لأن التخويف لا يكون إلا على فعل قبيح موجب استحقاق الذم، فالتجاوز عن مستحق الذم والعقاب هو العفو، قيل في ترجيح الإضمار على التخويف بأن التخويف مخصوص بعصاة المؤمنين
