الواردة في حق المشركين
Now we begin the topics of rulings, and we present before them the definition of wajib due to the contention it contains, even though it was previously known within the division. (It is that the abandoner of which deserves punishment, a rational deserving) according to the proponents of rational good and evil, (or a conventional deserving) according to the Ash'aris. And 'its abandoner in all of its time' was added to include the broadened [obligation]. It was said: 'its abandoner in all of its time in a way' to include the broadened and the communal, but there is no need for both, and what is in the text is sufficient, so reflect. And what was said that there is no deserving of punishment for non-existence because it is not possible, but if abstinence from the act is intended, then the non-abstainer would not deserve punishment. It contains what will come: non-existence, even if in itself impossible, necessitates deserving punishment. If it is said: 'Thus the validity of pardon is impossible', we say: No. (And pardon) for the one deserving punishment is from generosity, so it does not contradict the deserving and appropriateness. (And it is said: 'It is that which punishment is threatened for abandoning it, and pardon is not excluded because breaking the threat is permissible. He threatens punishment and does not execute it, for people of sound intellect consider it a virtue not a deficiency, and it is narrated from Abdullah ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both), unlike the promise, for breaking it is a deficiency impossible for Him, exalted. This excuse was refuted by saying that Allah's threat is a report, so it is absolutely true due to the impossibility of lying there. He apologized by saying that it is not a report; rather it is a creative threat, so there is no harm then in breaking it. He refuted it by saying: (And considering it a creative threat, as mentioned in the margins of Mirzajan and others, is a departure from the literal meaning without a cause that necessitates departure, which is impermissible. Moreover, the same applies to the promise, for it could be said that it is for creative encouragement, so breaking it would be permissible, thus closing the door of return. This is absurd. I say thirdly: If the permissibility of creativity is accepted, it would indicate the absolute invalidity of pardon because it is overlooking the one deserving punishment. On this, the punishment is not promised. The discussion was about its exclusion after conceding its existence. And if the excuse by allowing breaking is not valid, then it must be said in the excuse that the threat in the speech of Allah is conditioned upon no pardon, so there is no breaking nor objection. You may turn it against him by saying that conditioning is a departure from the literal meaning without cause, and the same applies to the promise, so it would entail the permissibility of punishing the one promised paradise without account, as a possible occurrence. The truth is that the cause for departure is realized, which is the established permissibility of pardon for the major sinners who are not polytheists, an absolutely clear establishment like the sun at midday. So departure from the apparent meaning in threats that are for non-disbelievers is necessary, either by conditioning or by making it a creative threat. As for the promise, there is no cause for it, so it remains on its literal meaning. And what he said is nothing, because a threat is only for an evil act that necessitates deserving blame, so overlooking the one deserving blame and punishment is pardon. It was said in preferring ellipsis over threat that the threat is specific to sinful believers who are forgiven, while the text of the threat includes others, and there is no threat in their case. It is possible that one speech is a threat for some and a report for others. It is not unlikely that it be answered that there is threat for all, except that perhaps he may punish, for punishing the threatened is permissible, and perhaps he may pardon. Yes, the verses and hadith specific to polytheists are not interpreted as creative threat because there is no cause there. Hence, it occurred in the speech of the greatest Shaykh, the caliph of Allah on earths, may his secret be sanctified, that there is no real threat except the texts regarding polytheists.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a definition of wajib (obligatory) and a detailed dispute regarding the nature of punishment and pardon. It begins by defining wajib as that which the abandoner deserves punishment, with a debate between those who hold rational deserving (Mu'tazila, followed by Hanafis) and those who hold conventional deserving (Ash'aris). The author then discusses the issue of 'afw (pardon) and khulf al-wa'id (breaking a threat), whether Allah's threat of punishment can be broken. He presents arguments: some say breaking threat is permissible based on a narration from Ibn Abbas, but this is refuted by saying Allah's speech is reportative and therefore truthful. Others say it is a creative threat (insha), which would allow breaking, but that is rejected as a departure from the literal without cause. The preferred view is that the threat is conditioned upon no pardon, given the certain knowledge that Allah pardons major sinners among believers. The passage concludes that the threat is either conditioned or creative for non-polytheists, while promises remain literal. This reflects the Hanafi/Mu'tazili position that punishment is deserved rationally but pardon is possible through divine grace.

Key Terms
واجب (wajib): What the abandoner deserves punishment for, either rationally or conventionally.
عفو ('afw): Pardon from punishment despite the desert, an act of generosity that does not negate the desert.
خلف الوعيد (khulf al-wa'id): Breaking a threat by not executing the promised punishment; considered permissible if the threat is not a report.
استحقاق عقلي (istihqaq 'aqli): Rational desert: the act itself inherently merits reward or punishment, discernible by reason.
إنشاء تخويف (insha' takhwif): Creative threat: a speech act intending to deter, not report a fact. If broken, it is not a lie.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position as presented in the passage, how is the problem of Allah's threat of punishment reconciled with the possibility of pardon?
A) The threat is a report that must be literally fulfilled, so pardon is impossible.
B) The threat is a creative speech (insha') intended to deter, so breaking it is permissible without lying.
C) The threat is conditioned upon no pardon, or it is a creative threat for non-polytheists, justified by the certain knowledge that Allah pardons major sinners.
D) The threat applies only to polytheists; believers are never threatened.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, wajib is defined by rational desert of punishment, but divine mercy permits pardon without breaking the threat, achieved by conditioning or creative interpretation of the threat for non-polytheists.

Fard Kifayah: Definition, Naskh Objection, and the Three Views
Defining communal obligation and addressing key objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fard kifayah in Hanafi usul, explain why its dropping is not naskh, and identify the three views on who exactly is obligated.

Core Idea
Fard kifayah drops from all when some fulfill it — the purpose is achieved, not the command abrogated.

Communal obligations are duties required from the community as a whole. When some perform them, the obligation falls away from everyone, and only the doers are rewarded. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes this from abrogation (naskh) and examines who exactly is addressed by such commands. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut lays the foundation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the definition of al-wajib 'ala al-kifayah: an obligation which, if performed by some, absolves the rest from sin and punishment, but if neglected by all, makes everyone sinful.
The author uses 'wajib' broadly to include fard.
2
Analyze the naskh objection
Consider the objection: if an obligation drops without being performed by everyone, isn't that abrogation? Follow the Hanafi response: no, because abrogation nullifies the command permanently, whereas here the command remains valid but its purpose is fulfilled by the action of some, like jihad aiming to raise Allah's word.
The key: the cause (illa) of obligation disappears when the purpose is achieved.
3
Identify the three views on the addressee
The passage presents three opinions on who exactly the obligation falls on: (1) an indeterminate person (al-ba'd al-mubham), (2) a specific unknown individual (rejected), and (3) a specific group — those who witness the event, as in funeral prayer.
The third view is preferred by Hanafi scholars like the author of al-Hidayah.
4
Apply to funeral prayer
Use the example of funeral prayer: it becomes obligatory on those present (neighbors in the vicinity). If the nearest perform it, it drops from all; if they neglect, the distant ones must rise.
This illustrates the practical implications of the third view.
5
Examine the dalil
Look at the Quranic evidence: 'Prescribed for you is fighting' (2:216). The purpose is to raise Allah's word; if some fight, the obligation drops.
The dalil supports the concept of communal obligation and its mechanism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الواجب على الكفاية) أي الواجب الذي من شأنه أن يثاب الآتون ولا يعاقب التاركون إذا أتى به البعض، وإن لم يأت أحد يعاقب الكل (واجب) على الكل أي كل واحد والمصنف جرى في هذا الكتاب على إطلاق الواجب بحيث يشمل الفرض أيضاً ويسقط بفعل البعض فإن قيل : سقوط الواجب من غير أدائه نسخ له؟ قال : ولا) يلزم النسخ لأن سقوط الأمر قبل الأداء قد يكون لانتفاء علة الوجوب) وهو ليس بنسخ وهي حصول المقصود من إيجابه بإتيان ،واحد، وتحقيقه أن المقصود من الإيجاب، قد يكون إتعاب المكلف بالاشتغال به كما في الأركان الأربعة، وقد يكون المقصود شيئاً آخر يجب لأجله ما يحصل المقصود بحصوله، فإذا حصل المقصود لا يبقى الواجب واجباً كالجهاد، فإنه إنما وجب لإعلاء كلمة الله تعالى فإذا أتى به البعض حصل الإعلاء وسقط الوجوب وهذا بمراحل من النسخ (وقيل) الواجب على الكفاية واجب على البعض المبهم، وهو مختار صاحب المحصول، وأما القول بأنه واجب على واحد معين عند الله غير معلوم عندنا فلم يصدر ممن يعتد به وبطلانه ،بين فإنه يلزم أن لا يكون المكلف عالماً بما كلف به، ولا يصح من نية أداء الواجب، والقول بأنه واجب على البعض المعين وهم المشاهدون للشيء، كصلاة الجنازة فإنها تجب على من شاهدها شرح لقول الجمهور، فإنهم لا يقولون بوجوب صلاة الجنازة على كل أحد كيف وهذا تكليف بما لا يطاق، وقد صرح صاحب «الهداية» أن سبب وجوب صلاة الجنازة شهودها وقال صدر الشريعة في شرح الوقاية»: تصير صلاة الجنازة فرضاً على جيرانه دون من هو بعيد فإن أقام الأقربون كلهم أو بعضهم سقط عن الكل، وإن بلغ الأبعد أن الأقرب ضيع حقه فعلى الأبعد أن يقوم بها، فإن ترك الكل فكل من بلغ إليه خبر موته آثم فافهم لنا أوّلاً النصوص، كقوله تعالى: كتب عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِتَالُ [البقرة: ٢١٦] [يس: ٣٩]. وقوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام: «طلب
Issue: The communal obligation (al-wajib 'ala al-kifayah), i.e., the obligation that is such that those who perform it are rewarded and those who abandon it are not punished if some perform it, but if no one performs it, all are punished. (It is obligatory) upon all, i.e., every individual. And the author in this book uses 'wajib' in a general sense that includes fard as well, and it drops by the action of some. If it is said: 'The dropping of an obligation without performance is abrogation of it?', he said: No, abrogation does not necessarily follow, because the dropping of a command before performance may be due to the removal of the cause of obligation, which is not abrogation. That is the achievement of the purpose of its obligation by the action of one. Its verification is that the purpose of obligation may be to occupy the servant with the act, as in the four pillars, and may be something else for which it is obligated, which is achieved by its achievement. When the purpose is achieved, the obligation is no longer obligatory, like jihad, for it was obligated to raise the word of Allah, so when some perform it, raising is achieved and the obligation drops. This is far from abrogation. And it was said: The communal obligation is obligatory upon an indeterminate person (al-ba'd al-mubham), and this is the choice of the author of al-Mahsul. As for the saying that it is obligatory upon a specific individual known to Allah but unknown to us, it has not been issued by anyone reliable, and its falsehood is clear, for it would entail that the obligated person does not know what he is obligated to do, and the intention to perform the obligation would not be valid. And the saying that it is obligatory upon a specific group, namely those who witness the matter, like the funeral prayer, for it becomes obligatory upon those who witness it, that is an explanation of the majority view, for they do not say that the funeral prayer is obligatory upon everyone, how could that be, for it would be an impossible burden. And the author of al-Hidayah explicitly stated that the cause of the obligation of funeral prayer is witnessing it. And Sadr al-Shari'ah said in Sharh al-Wiqayah: The funeral prayer becomes fard upon its neighbors, not upon those far away. If the nearest ones, all or some, perform it, it drops from all. If the distant one learns that the nearest neglected his right, then the distant one must perform it. If all leave it, then everyone who hears of his death is sinful. Understand. First, the texts for us, like His statement: 'Prescribed for you is fighting' [2:216] ... and his statement, peace be upon him: 'Seeking' ...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of fard kifayah (communal obligation) within Hanafi usul. It explains that it is an obligation upon each individual, but if some perform it, the obligation drops from all. If none perform, all are sinful. The author addresses an objection that dropping an obligation without performance would be abrogation (naskh), and shows that it is not abrogation but rather the fulfillment of the purpose of the obligation (like raising Allah's word in jihad). The passage then discusses different views on who exactly the obligation falls upon: some say on an indeterminate person (al-ba'd al-mubham), others on a specific unknown individual (rejected), and others on those who witness the act (like funeral prayer). The latter view is explained with the example of funeral prayer: it is obligatory on those present (neighbors) according to al-Hidayah and Sadr al-Shari'ah. If the nearest neglect, the distant must perform. The texts cited include Quran 2:216 (jihad) and a hadith about 'seeking' (cut off). This shows the Hanafi approach to communal duties, emphasizing practicality and avoiding impossible burden.

Key Terms
الواجب على الكفاية (al-wajib 'ala al-kifayah): An obligation that is required from the community as a whole; if some perform it, the rest are absolved; if none perform, all are sinful.
فريضة الكفاية (faridat al-kifayah): Same as wajib kifayah, often called fard kifayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, the funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) becomes a fard kifayah first upon whom?
A) All Muslims worldwide
B) The immediate neighbors or those present
C) The imam of the local mosque
D) The family of the deceased only

Key Takeaway
Fard kifayah is a communal obligation that drops when the purpose is achieved; for funeral prayer, it falls first on those present, not on everyone.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
15 min
Definition of Fard Kifaya (Communal Obligation) and the Debate Over Its Addressee
Defining fard kifaya and debating its obligation on individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fard kifaya, explain why its falling away is not abrogation, and evaluate the dispute over whom the obligation addresses.

Core Idea
Fard kifaya is obligatory on every individual—but falls by the action of some, because its purpose is achieved.

In this passage, the author defines the communal obligation (wajib 'ala al-kifaya) and clarifies its operation: it is required of all responsible Muslims until some perform it, at which point the obligation falls from everyone without punishment. The passage then delves into the debate about whether the obligation is upon all individuals or upon an unspecified group, and defends the Hanafi position using textual evidence and logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the definition
Break down the phrase 'al-wajib 'ala al-kifayah' into its elements: what is the ruling for those who perform? For those who abandon when others have performed? For all if none perform?
Note that the author uses 'wajib' broadly to include fard.
2
Explain the non-abrogation argument
Understand why falling away without performance is not naskh. Identify the distinction between the cause (illa) of obligation being removed versus the ruling being changed.
The example of jihad: purpose is raising Allah's word; if achieved, cause vanishes.
3
Map the different positions on the addressee
List the three views: (1) obligatory on all (Hanafi), (2) obligatory on an unspecified group (al-Mahsul), (3) obligatory on a specific unknown individual (rejected). Note the majority view regarding witnesses for funeral prayer.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi proof from collective sin
Analyze the logic: if all are sinful when none perform, the obligation must have been on each individual. Consider the opponents' objection that collective sin does not necessitate individual obligation.
Follow the passage's reasoning about ithm al-kull being an individual of ithm al-ba'd.
5
Examine the counterarguments and responses
Review the opponents' arguments: falling by some (like other obligations would not), ambiguity in the responsible person. Then study the Hanafi responses including the guarantor analogy and refutation of the qiyas in the face of nass.
Pay attention to the distinction between direct and indirect sin.

والكل لا يستلزم الوجوب على القدر المشترك كيف والنصوص قاضية بالوجوب على الكل فلم لا يجوز أن يكون من خواص بعض الواجبات سقوطها بفعل واحد فافهم و(قلنا) ثانياً قياس الفارق إذ (تأثيم المبهم غير معقول بخلاف واحد مما صدق عليه البعض أو على البعض المبهم في أي فرد تحقق وعلى الأول : الوجوب على الكل، وإنما الاختلاف في التعبير، وعلى الثاني فتأثيم المبهم لازم قطعاً لأن الآثم لا يكون إلا تارك الواجب عليه، وها هنا التارك للواجب البعض المبهم فهو الآثم وهو غير معقول، لأنه حينئذ
Issue: The communal obligation (wajib 'ala al-kifaya) is the obligation such that those who perform it are rewarded and those who abandon it are not punished if some perform it, but if no one performs it, all are punished. It is obligatory (wajib) upon all, i.e., each individual. And the author in this book uses the term wajib broadly to include fard as well, and it falls away with the action of some. If it is said: The falling away of the obligation without its fulfillment is abrogation? He said: No, abrogation is not necessary because the falling away of the command before performance may be due to the removal of the cause of obligation—and that is not abrogation—and that is the attainment of the purpose of its obligation by the action of one. The reality is that the purpose of the obligation may be to tire the responsible person by engaging in it, as in the four pillars, or it may be something else for which it is obliged so that the purpose is achieved by its occurrence. When the purpose is achieved, the obligation no longer remains, like jihad, for it was obliged to raise the word of Allah Almighty; if some perform it, the raising occurs and the obligation falls away, and this is far from abrogation. And it was said: The communal obligation is obligatory upon an unspecified group (al-ba'd al-mubham), and this is the choice of the author of al-Mahsul. As for the saying that it is obligatory upon a specific individual known to Allah but unknown to us, that has not been issued by anyone reliable, and its falsehood is clear because it would necessitate that the responsible person not know what is required of him, and the intention of performing the obligation would not be valid. The saying that it is obligatory upon a specific group, i.e., those who witness the thing, like the funeral prayer, for it is obligatory upon those who witness it, is an explanation of the view of the majority, for they do not say that the funeral prayer is obligatory upon everyone, as that would be an impossible burden. And the author of al-Hidayah explicitly stated that the cause of obligation of the funeral prayer is witnessing it. And Sadr al-Shari'ah said in Sharh al-Wiqayah: The funeral prayer becomes fard upon the neighbors, not upon those far away; if all or some of the nearest perform it, it falls from all; if the far person learns that the nearest have neglected their duty, then the far person must perform it; if all abandon it, then everyone who hears of the death is sinful. Understand. For us, first, the texts, such as Allah's saying: 'Fighting is prescribed for you' (2:216) and 'Every innovation is misguidance' (?) and his saying, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions: 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim and Muslimah' reported by Imam Abu Hanifah, etc., so there is no reason to deviate from it. And for us, secondly, the sinning of all if they abandon it when they assume that others did not do it; if it were not obligatory upon them, they would not all sin. He said in the gloss: There is something in it, and perhaps the point is that the sinning of all does not necessitate obligation upon all; rather, the imputation of sin to all is because it is an individual of the group, as he resolved. And it may be said: Perhaps the sinning of all will come with obligation upon all as a whole, so it does not necessitate obligation upon each individual; the weakness of this claim is apparent, for the condition of collectivity in obligation is unintelligible, otherwise it would necessitate sin by the abandonment of some, which contradicts the communal obligation. Those who say obligation upon a group said: First, it falls by the action of some; if it were obligatory upon all, it would not fall by the action of some, like other acts of worship. We say: The entailment is not granted, for the purpose is the existence of the action in reality, and it has been found, so the cause of obligation remains no more, so it falls like what is upon two guarantors by the payment of one of them due to the attainment of the purpose, which is the satisfaction of the creditor's right. This is a support for the denial, so the criticism that there is no debt upon the two guarantors, rather they have the duty to demand, does not harm; for it suffices us in support that the demand falls from both by the payment of what they were demanded. Yes, if it were an analogy as understood from some Shafi'i books, it would harm, so understand. And they said: Secondly, the ambiguity in the responsible person (mukallaf) is like the ambiguity in the act (mukallaf bihi), and the obligation upon an ambiguous responsible person is valid for the attainment of the interest. We say: First, it is an analogy against a clear text, so it is not heard. And it has been established that the communal obligation falls by the action of all and some, so the responsible person is the common denominator, which is some, so no obstacle remains except ambiguity, and it is not an obstacle because ambiguity in the responsible person is like ambiguity in the act, and it does not prevent. Then the answer is that falling by the action of some and all does not entail obligation upon the common denominator; how can it be when texts require obligation upon all? So why should it not be a characteristic of some obligations that they fall by the action of one? So understand. And we say, secondly, an analogy of difference, since the imputation of sin to an ambiguous person is unintelligible, unlike the imputation of sin to specific persons for abandoning the ambiguous person. So ambiguity in the responsible person is with an obstacle unlike the act. It was said against it: Their view is that all sin due to the abandonment of some, so there is no imputation of sin to the ambiguous. We say: Rather it is necessary, since the abandonment of some first and directly entails the sin of some, for they are the abandoners of the obligation, although it leads to the sin of all secondly and indirectly due to the lack of priority of some over others. Even we say: The imputation of sin to all indirectly is also not valid unless it is obligatory upon all indirectly, and they do not hold that. So it necessitates the imputation of sin to the ambiguous. I say to refute it: The whole is an individual of the ambiguous group, for their meaning of 'some' includes whether it materializes in some or all, because if all perform it, they have performed what was obligatory upon them by agreement, so the sin of all is an individual of the sin of some, just as the action of all was an individual of the action of some. This kind of imputation of sin to the ambiguous is certainly intelligible because it does not negate actuality. Yes, the imputation of sin to the ambiguous that does not encompass the whole, i.e., insofar as it is ambiguous, is unintelligible, so reflect. And in it: First, the whole, although it is an individual of the some, the obligation is upon any single one to which 'some' applies, or upon the ambiguous some insofar as it is realized in any individual. On the first, the obligation is upon all, only the expression differs; on the second, the imputation of sin to the ambiguous is certainly necessary because the sinner is only the one who abandons what is obligatory upon him, and here the abandoner of the obligation is the ambiguous some, so it is the sinner, which is unintelligible because then...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines fard kifaya (communal obligation) as an obligation that is required of every individual, but if some perform it, the obligation falls from all without punishment for the rest. The author clarifies that if no one performs it, all are sinful. He responds to the objection that this falling away of obligation without performance would constitute abrogation (naskh). He explains that it is not abrogation because the purpose of the obligation (like jihad for exaltation of Allah's word) is achieved by the performance of some, so the cause (illa) of obligation disappears. This is fundamentally different from abrogation. The passage then discusses different opinions on whom the obligation actually addresses: some say it is obligatory upon an unspecified group (al-ba'd al-mubham), others say upon a specific individual unknown to us (rejected), and others say upon specific observers (e.g., funeral prayer). The author supports the Hanafi view that it is obligatory upon all, citing texts like the Quranic verse on fighting and the hadith on seeking knowledge. He refutes arguments that ambiguity in the responsible person invalidates the obligation, and uses the example of guarantors (kafilayn) to illustrate how a duty can fall by the action of one. The debate includes detailed logical arguments about the nature of sin (ithm) and obligation, ultimately defending the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الواجب على الكفاية (al-wājib ʿalā al-kifāyah): A type of obligation that, if performed by some, removes the obligation from all, but if abandoned by all, all are sinful.
الفرض (al-farḍ): An obligation proven by a definitive (qatʿī) evidence; the author uses 'wajib' to include both fard and other obligations.
البعض المبهم (al-baʿḍ al-mubham): An unspecified group; the view that the communal obligation addresses such an ambiguous group.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the falling away of a communal obligation without performance is not abrogation (naskh)?
A) Because the obligation is never truly binding on anyone.
B) Because the cause of the obligation disappears when its purpose is attained.
C) Because the texts that imposed it were abrogated by later verses.
D) Because the obligation only applies to a specific group of people.

Key Takeaway
Fard kifayah is a genuine obligation on each responsible individual, but its unique characteristic is that the obligation falls by the action of some because the purpose of the command is achieved.

The Core Dispute: Collective Obligation – Wajib on All or Some?
Dispute on the nature of collective obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on collective obligation and evaluate the opponents' arguments regarding the fall of obligation and the ambiguity of the obligated person.

Core Idea
Collective obligation is wajib on every individual but falls when performed by some, like a joint guarantee.

This lesson explores the foundational dispute over the nature of fard kifaya. The Hanafi school holds that the obligation is initially incumbent on every legally responsible person (الكل), while opponents argue it is only on an unspecified group (البعض). We will examine the two key arguments from the opponents and the Hanafi responses rooted in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its key proof
The Hanafi position states that collective obligation is wajib on every individual (الكل). The primary proof cited is that all sin if the act is abandoned, implying individual responsibility.
This proof is based on the assumption that sin requires a prior obligation on the sinner.
2
Analyze the opponent's first argument: fall of obligation by some
Opponents argue that if it were wajib on all, it would not fall by the action of some, like individual obligations (e.g., prayer). The Hanafi response: the purpose is the existence of the act; once it occurs, the cause of obligation ceases, similar to two guarantors where one pays the debt.
The analogy to joint guarantors is central to the Hanafi rebuttal.
3
Analyze the opponent's second argument: ambiguity in the obligated person
Opponents claim that ambiguity in the obligated person (البعض) is acceptable, just as ambiguity in the obligated act is acceptable. The Hanafi response: this is qiyas in the face of clear texts (nusus) that indicate obligation on all. Furthermore, making an ambiguous entity the subject of obligation leads to absurdity in punishment.
The Hanafis emphasize that texts clearly obligate every individual, so ambiguity is not allowed.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a collective obligation is neglected, all sin under the Hanafi view, but only an unidentifiable 'some' sins under the opposing view. This affects accountability and the necessity of communal effort.
The Hanafi view ensures that every individual feels personally responsible for fulfilling the obligation.

قال في التحرير يشكل) مسألة الوجوب على البعض بدليل السقوط بفعل البعض (بسقوط) صلاة الجنازة بفعل الصبي العاقل كما هو الأصح عند الشافعية لا وجوب عليه ولا يسقط الواجب إلا بأداء من وجب عليه أقول لا إشكال فإن ذلك) السقوط كسقوط الدين بأداء المتبرع مع أنه لا وجوب لأداء الدين عليه، والحاصل أنه ربما يكون المقصود من إيجاب شيء خروج الفعل المقصود منه في الوجود، فإن وجد بنفسه أو بأداء من وجوب عليه يسقط الوجوب، وهذا كما أنه متحقق في حقوق العباد فإن المقصود وصول الدين، مثلاً: فإن وجد الدائن مال المديون وأخذ بقدر دينه من غير إذنه، أو أدى المتبرع الدين سقط الوجوب عن ذمة المديون كذلك في حقوق الله تعالى التي يكون المقصود منها وقوع المصلحة دون إتعاب المكلف بالذات، بل الإتعاب لأجل وقوع الفعل فقط، فلو وقعت المصلحة بنفسها كما إذا أسلم الكفرة أو ماتوا أو اقتتلوا فيما بينهم وقتلوا جميعاً سقط الجهاد عن الذمة فما قيل إن هذا قياس الحقوق الإلهية عن الحقوق العبدية، وهو غير صحيح ساقط، فتدبر .
The second article on rulings. Knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim man and woman, narrated by Imam Abu Hanifa and others, so there is no reason to deviate from it. And we have, secondly: the sin of all if they leave it when they think that others did not do it. If it were not obligatory upon them, they would not all sin. He said in the Hashiyah: There is something in it, and perhaps its aspect is that the sinning of all does not necessitate obligation upon all, but rather the sinning of all because it is an individual of some [of them] as he resolved. And it may be said: Perhaps the sinning of all will come with the obligation upon all as a whole, so it does not necessitate obligation upon each individual. The claimant of this and its weakness is apparent, for stipulating collectivity in obligation is unreasonable, otherwise it would necessitate sin by leaving some, which contradicts collective obligation. Those who say obligation upon some said: Firstly, it falls by the action of some, and if it were obligatory upon all, it would not fall by the action of some like other acts of worship. We said: We do not accept the implication, because the intended is the existence of the act in reality, and it has existed, so the cause of obligation has ceased, so it falls like the falling of what is upon two guarantors by the payment of one of them because the purpose is achieved, which is the creditor's right. This is a basis for denial, so the discussion about it not being a debt on the guarantors but rather a claim upon them does not harm, for it suffices us in the basis the falling of the claim from them by the payment of what the claim was directed to them. Yes, if it were an analogy as understood from some Shafi'i books, it would harm, so understand. And they said, secondly: The ambiguity in the obligated person is like the ambiguity in the obligated act, and the obligation is on the ambiguous obligated person for the attainment of benefit through it. We said: Firstly, it is an analogy against the clear texts, so it is not heard, and it has been established that the collective obligation falls by the action of all and some, so the obligated is the common denominator, which is some. So no obstacle remains except ambiguity, and it is not an obstacle because ambiguity in the obligated person is like ambiguity in the obligated act, and it does not prevent. Then the answer: The falling by the action of some and all does not entail obligation upon the common denominator; how can it when texts decree obligation upon all? So why is it not permissible that it be a characteristic of some obligations that they fall by the action of one? Understand. And we said, secondly: It is an analogy of difference, because making the ambiguous sinful is unreasonable unlike making the determinate ones sinful for leaving the ambiguous. So the ambiguity of the obligated person is an obstacle without the obligated act. It was said against it: Their doctrine is that all sin due to leaving some, so there is no making the ambiguous sinful. We said: Rather it is necessary, because leaving some primarily and essentially necessitates the sin of some, for they are the abandoners of the obligation, even if it leads to the sin of all secondarily and accidentally due to lack of priority of some over others. Rather we say: Making all sinful accidentally is also not valid unless it is obligatory upon all accidentally, and they do not say that. So it necessitates making the ambiguous sinful. I say to refute it: The all are from the individual of the ambiguous some, because they mean by 'some' more general than whether it is realized in some or all, for if all perform it, they perform what is obligatory upon them unanimously, so the sin of all is an individual of the sin of some, just as the performance of all was an individual of the performance of some. This kind of making the ambiguous sinful is certainly reasonable because it does not contradict achievement. Yes, making the ambiguous sinful who is not combined with all, i.e., insofar as he is ambiguous, is unreasonable. So reflect. In it: Firstly, that all, even if it is an individual of some, the obligation is on any one of what is called some or on the ambiguous some in whichever individual it is realized. On the first: obligation is on all, and the difference is only in expression. On the second: making the ambiguous sinful is necessarily required because the sinner is only the one who leaves what is obligatory upon him. Here the leaver of the obligation is the ambiguous some, so he is the sinner, which is unreasonable because then the reckoning is directed essentially to him. So ponder. Secondly: This servant says that making the ambiguous sinful is false, because punishment is either on an ambiguous some in terms of ambiguity, or on a determinate some, or on all. The falsity of the first is necessary, and likewise the second because there is no priority of some, and likewise the third, and it is injustice, because each of them has the excuse that you obligated upon some and punished others besides me. And it cannot be said that all are punished due to lack of priority in it, because this is not better than pardoning all; rather pardoning them is better because His mercy preceded His wrath. And if it is said that the obligation is upon individuals of some and all are its individuals, then it comes back to what we said of obligation upon all. And the most complete knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. And they said, thirdly: Allah said: 'So why not go forth from each group among them a party to become learned in religion and to warn their people when they return to them' [Tawbah 122]. Meaning: Why does not a party from each group, whether one or more, go forth with the Messenger of Allah to become learned and warn their people after returning, or why does not a party go forth in expeditions while not everyone goes forth and others remain with the Messenger to become learned and warn their people who are with the expeditions when they return? He commanded some to go forth for learning or jihad, and both are collective obligations. We said: There is nothing in the noble verse indicating obligation upon some; rather it is an encouragement for some to go forth to gain the benefit of learning. And if we concede, we say: It is interpreted as falling by the action of some, combining the proofs—this proof and the proofs indicating obligation upon all from texts and others. Then he said in al-Tahrir: The issue of obligation upon some is problematic by the evidence of falling by the action of some, by the falling of the funeral prayer by the action of a sane boy as is the correct view among the Shafi'is—no obligation upon him, and the obligatory does not fall except by performance of the one upon whom it is obligatory. I say: There is no problem, for that falling is like the falling of a debt by the payment of a volunteer even though there is no obligation to pay the debt upon him. The conclusion: Sometimes the purpose of making something obligatory is the occurrence of the intended act in existence. If it occurs by itself or by performance of the one upon whom it is obligatory, the obligation falls. This is realized in the rights of servants, for the purpose is the arrival of the debt. For example, if the creditor finds the debtor's property and takes the amount of his debt without his permission, or a volunteer pays the debt, the obligation falls from the debtor's liability. Similarly in the rights of Allah where the purpose is the occurrence of the benefit without wearying the obligated person essentially; rather the wearying is only for the occurrence of the act. If the benefit occurs by itself—as if the disbelievers become Muslim or die or fight among themselves and kill each other—jihad falls from liability. So what is said, that this is analogy of divine rights to human rights, is incorrect and dropped. So ponder.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dispute on the nature of collective obligation (wajib kifa'i). The author defends the Hanafi position that such obligations are wajib on every individual (الكل) but fall when performed by some. Opponents argue it is wajib only on some vague 'some' (البعض). The Hanafi proof includes: sin of all for abandonment (showing it is obligatory on all), necessity of performance (like loan guarantee), and textual evidence (Quran 9:122) which the Hanafis interpret as encouraging some to act, not limiting obligation. The author also refutes the Shafi'i objection about funeral prayer by a boy, showing that obligation can fall by voluntary performance if the purpose is achieved. The passage concludes that the analogy to human rights is valid and the objection is dropped.

Key Terms
الوجوب الكفائي (al-wujub al-kifa'i): An obligation that is initially incumbent on every individual but falls if performed by a sufficient number.
الكل (al-kull): Every individual member of the community, i.e., all legally responsible persons.
البعض (al-ba'd): An unspecified number (some) of the community, vague and indeterminate.
الكفيلان (al-kafilān): Two joint guarantors/sureties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what is the correct understanding of collective obligation?
A) It is obligatory on every individual and falls only when all perform it.
B) It is obligatory on every individual but falls when performed by a sufficient number.
C) It is obligatory only on an ambiguous 'some' of the community.
D) It is obligatory on the community as a whole, not on individuals.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, collective obligation is wajib on every individual, not on an ambiguous group, ensuring clear personal responsibility.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

The Optional Obligatory (al-Wajib al-Mukhayyar): Definition and Critique
Definition and critique of al-wajib al-mukhayyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-wajib al-mukhayyar, identify its components, and evaluate the opposing view that all alternatives are individually obligatory.

Core Idea
The wajib mukhayyar obligates a choice among alternatives; performing one fulfills the duty, not all.

In this passage, the author defines a special type of obligation: the optional obligatory (wajib mukhayyar). It is a genuine obligation to choose one of several specified alternatives, like the expiation options for breaking an oath. Some scholars claim that each alternative is individually wajib, but the author critiques this view as inconsistent. We will dissect the definition and examine the critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term and Its Hukm
The term is al-wajib al-mukhayyar. Note that it is a wajib (obligation) but with a choice. State the hukm: it is wajib to perform one of the alternatives.
Do not confuse with mubah (permissible) – it is truly obligatory to choose.
2
List the Components of the Definition
Using the legal definition dissector, identify the mahall (mukallaf), sabab (cause like kaffara), shart (choose from set), mani (not doing any or impossibility), and athar (sin removed by one).
The definition is from the Hanafi perspective.
3
Understand the Opposing View
The passage mentions a view that all alternatives are wajib, and performing one satisfies the obligation, but doing all brings reward for multiple obligations.
This view is attributed to some scholars, not a specific madhhab.
4
Analyze the Author's Critique
The author says this view is not consistent (ghayr muttad). Why? Because sometimes the alternatives cannot be performed together, as in the example of appointing one of two qualified candidates for the caliphate—you cannot appoint both. So the view that all are wajib cannot be universally true.
The critique uses a counterexample from siyasa shar'iyya.
5
Draw the Conclusion
The Hanafi position is that only one alternative is required. The obligation is to choose and perform one; performing extra is not wajib and may be rewarded but not as an obligation.
This definition is operative in rulings on kaffara and similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [إيجاب الواجب المخير ] مع أنه وجوب إيجاب أمر من أمور معلومة صحيح) وواقع (وهو الواجب المخير) إصطلاحاً (كخصال الكفارة، وقيل فيها إيجاب) بالجميع، ويسقط بفعل البعض، فلو أتى) المكلف بالجميع يستحق ثواب واجبات على هذا الرأي، لأنه آت بواجبات (أقول) هذا غير مطرد، إذ (ذلك فرع جواز اجتماع الجميع، وقد لا يجوز كنصب أحد المستعدين للإمامة الكبرى فإنه واجب،
Issue: [The obligation of the optional obligatory]—with the meaning that it is an obligation to make one among known things obligatory—is sound and real. It is the optional obligatory in terminology, like the expiation options. And it was said in it: it is an obligation of all, and it falls by doing some. So if the obligated person does all, he deserves the reward of obligations according to this view, because he performed obligations. (I say): This is not consistent, because that is a branch of the permissibility of gathering all, and it may not be permissible, like appointing one of the two qualified for the great imamate, for it is obligatory.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the optional obligatory (wajib mukhayyar). It is a genuine obligation to choose among specified alternatives (e.g., expiation options: freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding the poor). Some scholars hold that all alternatives are individually obligatory, and the obligation falls by performing any one. If someone performs all, they receive reward for multiple obligations. The author (the muhaqqiq) critiques this: it is not a consistent rule, because sometimes the alternatives cannot all be performed together (e.g., appointing one of two qualified candidates for caliph—only one can be chosen, but it is wajib to appoint one, not both). Thus, the view that all are wajib is problematic.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير (al-wajib al-mukhayyar): An obligatory act where the mukallaf has a choice among specified alternatives; fulfilling one fulfills the obligation.
خصال الكفارة (khisal al-kaffara): The prescribed expiation options: freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
الإمامة الكبرى (al-imama al-kubra): The supreme leadership/caliphate; only one can be appointed at a time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person performs all the expiation options (free slave, fast, feed poor) for one kaffara?
A) It is invalid because only one is required.
B) According to one view, he deserves reward for multiple obligations, but the author says this rule is not consistent because sometimes the alternatives cannot all be done.
C) He must have intended them all as one obligation.
D) He sins for doing extra acts.

Key Takeaway
Al-wajib al-mukhayyar is a genuine obligation to choose among specified alternatives; it is not required to perform all of them.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
12 min
The Nature of Obligation in Takhyir (Choice-Based Commands)
Analyzing the dispute on the nature of wajib in takhyir commands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four views on the nature of wajib in takhyir and evaluate the Hanafi position that the wajib is a mubham (unspecified entity) using the arguments from this passage.

Core Idea
When the Lawgiver commands one of several alternatives, the wajib is the common element — a single unspecified entity that any alternative fulfills.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a foundational usul dispute: what exactly is wajib when the Lawgiver says 'do one of these things'? The Hanafi school argues that the wajib is the concept of 'one of them' (al-mubham), not the whole set nor a predetermined specific. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping how obligation operates in choice-based commands like kafara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four views
List the four positions discussed: the whole set is wajib (al-kull), a single specific varying wajib (mu'ayyan mukhtalif), a single specific non-varying wajib (mu'ayyan ghayr mukhtalif), and the Hanafi view: a single unspecified wajib (mubham).
These are clearly delineated in the passage with arguments and refutations.
2
Follow the Hanafi critique of the first three views
Note the objections raised: doing the whole set would incur sin; the varying specific depends on unknown future action; the non-varying specific is unknown until performance. The Hanafi refutes each.
The criticism of 'the whole set' includes the argument that extra acts beyond one are not obligatory.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof relies on rational possibility (jawaz 'aql) and definitive textual indication (nas qati) from Quran 5:89. The text argues that all obligations demand only the common element (al-qadr al-mushtarak).
Example: prayer demands the common element among all valid prayer instances.
4
Respond to the opponents' counterarguments
The passage shows Hanafi responses to claims that an unspecified wajib is impossible or unknown. The response: it is known conceptually (ma'lum min haythu huwa wajib) though not as a specific individual.
Knowledge of the specific follows the act, not precedes obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: in kafara, any one of the three acts fulfills the obligation; doing all three means only one wajib is fulfilled, the rest are nafl.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from those requiring all or a predetermined specific.

The second article on rulings. ... (the whole passage is translated in the commentary, but here is a condensed literal for the first part): 'And making the whole obligatory is forbidden, so how can one who comes with the whole deserve the reward of obligatory acts? Rather he deserves sin. Perhaps the one who says this only speaks of rewards whenever possible. Then it is apparent that the dispute is only about what is commanded as a choice among known things. Based on this, the objection would only apply if the command were established in this manner, otherwise not. Then this possibility is not well-known. As-Sayyid said: some Mu'tazila said it. No, said in al-Hashiya: they are considered among their famous ones: the obligatory is the whole as a substitute, and this is exactly our madhhab, so there is no substantive dispute. Some made the dispute conceptual and formulated this madhhab as the obligatory being the whole, then they refuted by saying if so, abandoning some would incur sin. And it was said: the obligatory is a single specific thing with Him (Exalted), ambiguous to us. That specific thing is what one does, so the obligatory varies; whoever frees a slave, freeing is obligatory upon him, whoever feeds or clothes, that is obligatory. It was refuted: obligation must be before the act; perhaps he meant knowledge of obligation must be before the act, otherwise this speaker holds obligation only at the moment of compliance, since compliance without knowledge of obligation is inconceivable, for it is performing the obligatory as obligatory and with intention in most obligations, especially in expiation. In al-Hashiya: obligation is a demand, and it comes before the demanded; specification even in God's knowledge only occurs after existence because knowledge follows the known. This shows that specific demand before existence is inconceivable. God knows things before they exist as they will exist, so He knows each specific act the mukallaf will do, so demand can attach to it. The truth is what we said before. Understand. And it was said: the obligatory is one specific thing that does not vary, but it is discharged by doing it and also by doing the other, because the other substitutes for it. We say: rational possibility is established; how so? All obligations only demand the common element; prayer only demands the common element among the particular prayers occurring in each part of its time; denying this is stubbornness. And the text indicates it with definitive indication, so it must be accepted. Those who say the obligatory is the whole said in denying choice: "and it was restricted because some of their evidences only give this much." In al-Hashiya: this restriction indicates there is no choice at all, unlike the other two opposing madhhabs, for in one there is choice in the act, in the other in discharge. Reducing all to denial of choice as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar is not free from criticism. Perhaps the point is that choice in discharge exists even in the view that the whole is obligatory, for it is discharged by doing the whole or some part, but the intended choice is choice in performing the obligation within any particularity one wishes. This is only realized on the chosen view, not the other madhhabs. For in the second the obligatory is the whole; in the third it is the one specific thing that varies per person; in the fourth it is the specific thing. The first: the one unspecified; the unspecified is unknown and impossible to occur, so one cannot be obliged with it because obligation is impossible. This can also refute the differing specific and the non-differing specific because it is unknown, so obligation with it is invalid. We say: we do not accept that the unspecified is unknown; rather it is known insofar as it is obligatory, and it is the concept of one of three, even if unknown as a specific, but it is not obligatory in that respect. This concept occurs by the occurrence of each, so its impossibility is denied; it would only be impossible if one were obliged to bring about an unspecified concrete instance. They said, secondly, the one is obligatory and choice is in it according to you; that the obligatory is one of them and choice in it are contradictory. We say: the ambiguous obligatory and the choice among the specified individuals is permissible because the locus of obligation is different from the locus of choice, like the obligation of one of two contradictories; otherwise both could cease while each is possible. They said, thirdly, obligation on all in the chosen is like obligation on all in sufficiency obligations, so the obligatory there is the whole as it was on all. We say, first, this is reasoning in opposition to definitive text; second, it is analogy with a difference, for making sinful one unspecified individual is inconceivable, so it was not made obligatory on one unspecified but on all, unlike making sinful by abandoning one specific, which is conceivable. Those who say the obligatory is specific said: God knows what one will do, so that is the obligatory... We say: yes, it is obligatory, but because it is one of them, not specifically. Those who say the obligatory is one specific that does not vary said: first, the commanded act must be known, otherwise the command is invalid; so it is specific with Him because ambiguity does not exist in the non-existent. We say: He knows it as He has obligated it, i.e., the concept of one of them; there is no ambiguity in it, only in its individuals, for knowledge follows the known. They said, second, if the mukallaf performs the whole, how is compliance? Either by the whole, then the whole is obligatory, or by each unit, leading to multiple final causes, which is absurd, or by one unspecified, which does not exist, so the specific is determined. I say in answer: I choose the first, and it follows that the whole is obligatory; compliance by the whole is only because the whole is an individual of one of them and the obligation exists in it. But it would follow that the whole is obligatory only if the whole were not a substitute. Here the whole is a substitute, so the set as a set is not obligatory. Do you not see that absence of a part is a complete cause for absence of the whole; if two parts are absent, the set of absences is the complete cause. Similarly, the real obligatory is one unspecified; when one specific is found, it is obligatory for containing it; when the whole is found, it is obligatory for containing it. It can be established by choosing the last option and denying that the one unspecified is non-existent; rather it exists within the whole, and compliance is by it. This is more apparent. And in al-Minhaj, he answered that compliance is by each and there is no harm in multiple final causes, for those are legal identifiers, not real causes, so no inconsistency. But that is clearly questionable because these identifiers have the same status as rational causes, so impossibility there implies impossibility here. This contradicts what the author will verify about allowing multiple causes in qiyas. The correct answer is that compliance is an existing thing, so it must have a cause of existence, and it is not a legal cause but rational; the Lawgiver merely made the concept of differing burden consideration, and this concept occurs by the occurrence of each, so its impossibility is denied; it would only be impossible if one were obliged to bring about an unspecified concrete instance.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the nature of obligation (wujub) when the Lawgiver commands one of several alternatives (takhyir), as in the expiation for breaking an oath (kafara: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them). The author presents and refutes four main views:
1. The wajib is the whole set (al-kull) — all three acts.
2. The wajib is a single specific entity that varies per person (mu'ayyan mukhtalif).
3. The wajib is a single specific entity that does not vary (mu'ayyan ghayr mukhtalif).
4. The Hanafi position: the wajib is a single unspecified entity (mubham) — the concept of 'one of them', and any alternative fulfills it.

The passage begins by criticizing the view that the whole set is obligatory, arguing that then doing all would be a sin (since extra acts would be unnecessary). It then explains that the true Hanafi view is that the wajib is the common element (al-qadr al-mushtarak) among the alternatives. The discussion includes counterarguments about knowledge, ambiguity, possibility, and compliance. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the unspecified entity is known as a concept, and the choice exists among the specified individuals (al-mu'ayyanat). The passage also addresses analogies to sufficiency obligations (fard kifaya) and the issue of multiple causes (ta'addud al-'ilal). Ultimately, the correct view per the Hanafi school is that the wajib is one unspecified act, and the mukallaf has choice in performing any of the specified alternatives.

Key Terms
الوجوب المخير (al-wujub al-mukhayyar): Obligation that can be fulfilled by any one of several specified alternatives; the wajib is the common element (concept of one of them).
المبهم (al-mubham): The unspecified entity; it is known as a concept but not as a particular individual.
المعين (al-mu'ayyan): The specific individual among the alternatives; it becomes the wajib after performance but not beforehand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the correct nature of the wajib in a takhyir command (e.g., the three alternatives for kafara)?
A) The whole set of alternatives is wajib.
B) A single specific alternative is wajib, which varies per person.
C) A single unspecified entity is wajib, and any alternative fulfills it.
D) No specific obligation exists; it is a recommendation.

Key Takeaway
In a takhyir command, the wajib is a single unspecified entity (mubham), and any specified alternative fulfills it; the mukallaf has genuine choice.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Time-Bound Obligations: Cause, Vessel, and Condition
Defining time-bound obligations and the roles of time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a wajib as muwaqqat or ghayr muwaqqat and identify the three roles of time in a muwaqqat obligation.

Core Idea
Time in the Law can be a cause, vessel, and condition for an obligation—yet the performed act is not the same as the performance.

This passage introduces a foundational classification in Hanafi usul: the division of wajib into muwaqqat (time-bound) and ghayr muwaqqat (non-time-bound). For each time-bound obligation, the time itself plays three distinct roles: it causes the obligation (sabab), contains the act (zarf), and conditions its validity (shart). The author then refines a subtle point: the contained act (the performed prayer) is not identical to the conditioned (the performance), though some texts equate them. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for deriving rulings about qada, tardiness, and excused omissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
Locate the first sentence: the obligatory act is divided into time-bound (muwaqqat) and non-time-bound (ghayr muwaqqat). Muwaqqat is defined as having a set time for performance prescribed by the Law.
Note that the term 'wajib' here is used broadly, encompassing what Hanafis sometimes separate as fard.
2
Analyze the temporal elements of a muwaqqat
For time-bound obligations, the time can be expansive (musawwa') or restrictive (mudayyiq). The passage focuses on expansive time. Identify the three functions of expansive time: cause of obligation (sabab), vessel containing the act (zarf), and condition for valid performance (shart).
The example of prayer time illustrates all three: it causes the obligation, holds the prayer and other acts, and is a condition for the prayer to be performed properly.
3
Distinguish between the act and the performance
The passage emphasizes that the contained act (al-mazruf, i.e., the performed prayer) is not identical to the conditioned (al-mashrut, i.e., the performance). Performance (ada') is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari), while the performed act is concrete. This distinction avoids confusion in rulings about when an act is considered performed.
Some texts, like al-Tahrir, conflate the two; the author refutes that by noting that the performance is a mental attribute with no external existence.
4
Evaluate the counter-argument from al-Tahrir
The passage quotes a view from al-Tahrir that equates 'performance' with 'the performed act'. The author responds by clarifying that 'performance' refers to the act of the agent (fi'l al-fa'il), which is mental and not concrete. Even mental occurrences can be conditions, so the distinction stands.
This refinement ensures that the rulings about time as condition remain consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تقسيم) الواجب إن كان لأدائه وقت مقدر شرعاً فمؤقت وإلا فغير مؤقت و(الوقت في الموقت إما أن يفضل) عن الواجب فيسمى ظرفاً وموسعاً) والمشهور أن الموسع اسم للواجب (كوقت الصلاة، وهو سبب للوجوب) لإضافة الصلاة إليه وهي تكرر بتكرر الوقت، وهذا آية السببية (وظرف للمؤدي فإنه يسعه ويسع غيره وشرط للأداء ، وهو) أي كونه شرطاً للأداء (الحكم في كل واجب موقت وليس المظروف عين المشروط لأن المشروط الأداء والمظروف الصلاة المؤداة والأداء غير المؤدى، وما في «التحرير» المراد بالأداء الفعل المفعول فيتحدان) أي المشروط والمؤدى المظروف (لا) المراد (فعل الفاعل) وهو الأداء الأنه اعتباري لا وجود له فمندفع : لأن الحادث وإن كان اعتبارياً يصلح للمشروطية
A division: The obligatory act – if there is a set time for its performance in the Law, it is time-bound; otherwise, it is non-time-bound. And the time in the time-bound is either surplus to the act, so it is called a vessel and expansive, and it is well-known that the expansive is a name for the obligatory, like the time of prayer, which is a cause for the obligation because prayer is added to it and it repeats with the repetition of time, and this is a sign of causality. And it is a vessel for the performed act, for it contains it and contains other than it, and it is a condition for performance. And it – i.e., its being a condition for performance – is the ruling in every time-bound obligatory act. And the contained is not the same as the conditioned because the conditioned is the performance and the contained is the performed prayer, and the performance is other than the performed. And what is in al-Tahrir: 'By performance is meant the performed act, so they become identical' – no, what is meant is the act of the agent, which is performance, for it is a mental consideration and has no existence, so it is repelled, because the occurrence, even if mental, is suitable for being a condition.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and classification. The author divides obligatory acts (wajib) into two categories: those with a designated time (muwaqqat) and those without (ghayr muwaqqat). For time-bound obligations, he further distinguishes two types of time: expansive (musawwa') and restrictive (mudayyiq). Expansive time is larger than the act itself and serves as a vessel (zarf), cause (sabab), and condition (shart). The example given is the time of prayer, which is a cause for obligation and contains both the prayer and other acts. The author then clarifies a subtle point: the contained act (the performed prayer) is not identical to the conditioned (the performance), though some texts equate them. In Hanafi usul, performance (ada') is a mental attribute, not a concrete action, but it can still serve as a condition. This distinction is important for understanding how time conditions obligations.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wajib): An act that is obligatory, whose omission is punishable
المؤقت (al-muwaqqat): An obligatory act with a specified time for performance
الموسع (al-musawwa'): Expansive time: time that exceeds the act and accommodates other acts
الظرف (al-zarf): Vessel: the time that contains the act
المظروف (al-mazruf): The contained act, e.g., the actual prayer performed
الأداء (al-ada'): Performance: the act of fulfilling the obligation, considered as a mental attribute of the agent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, what is a 'muwaqqat' wajib?
A) An obligatory act that must be performed immediately without delay
B) An obligatory act for which a specific time period is set by the Law
C) An obligatory act that can be performed at any time
D) An obligatory act that expiates a sin

Key Takeaway
The time of a muwaqqat wajib serves as cause, vessel, and condition, and the performed act must not be conflated with the performance.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Criterion (Mi'yar): Distinguishing Divine and Human Obligations
Explaining how the criterion applies to vow, Ramadan, and pilgrimage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of al-mi'yar to distinguish between divine obligations (like Ramadan) and human vows (like nathr), and evaluate the two Hanafi views on Hajj's time regarding immediacy and intention.

Core Idea
The criterion (mi'yar) defines a time that holds only one obligation, but divine and human obligations treat time differently.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi usul concept of al-mi'yar (the criterion) and its counterpart al-zarf (the container). These categories shape how obligations occupy time. We will see why Ramadan – a divine obligation – is a strict criterion, while a vowed fast originally occupies a container that becomes criterion by the vow. Then we tackle Hajj, which shares features of both, and examine the internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on whether its obligation is immediate or broad. This distinction is key for deriving correct rulings on intention and validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of time containers
Recognize al-mi'yar (criterion) as a time that can only accommodate one obligatory act of its type, and al-zarf (container) as a time that can accommodate multiple. The passage uses these to analyze obligations.
The criterion is like the month of Ramadan; the container is like the general days of the year before any obligation is assigned.
2
Apply the distinction to Ramadan vs. vow
Note that Ramadan is a divine obligation; its time is fixed as a criterion from the start, so any valid intention yields the obligatory fast. A vowed fast, however, was originally a container (any day could hold various fasts). The vow turns that day into a criterion for the vowed act, canceling the possibility of other intentions (like nafl or qada).
The text explicitly says: 'by the obligation of Allah, the fast of Ramadan becomes with the description of obligatory, so it cannot be described with another description. As for the vowed, it becomes specific by his vow.'
3
Examine Hajj's dual nature
Understand why Hajj has aspects of both: it can only be performed once per year (criterion-like), yet its overall time is one's entire life (container-like). The author mentions two similarities: it resembles the criterion because one year cannot hold more than one Hajj, and it resembles a container because life can hold multiple Hajj obligations (if missed and made up).
The text states: 'Hajj has two similarities: similarity to the criterion and similarity to the container, for it does not accommodate in one year except one.'
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf holds that obligation is immediate due to possibility of death, so the first year becomes the specific time (criterion). Muhammad argues that life is like the time of Zuhr prayer – broad until the end. The passage reconciles by stating that Hajj's ruling is between the two: it is performed with absolute intention, not purely criterion nor purely container.
The text: 'this aspect is only valid according to the opinion of Imam Abu Yusuf... As for the opinion of Imam Muhammad... the relation of life to it is like the relation of the time of Zuhr to it.'
5
Derive the Hanafi ruling on intention for Hajj
Apply the usul rule: because Hajj shares both features, its obligatory duty is performed with an absolute intention (just like a criterion), but it also counts as supererogatory if one intends it (like a container). The ruling is not reversed because supererogatoriness contradicts obligatory-ness.
The conclusion: 'We said: Its obligatory duty is performed with an absolute intention, and it counts as supererogatory if one intends it.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

النية وبنية النفل إلا في رواية غير مختارة لأنه كان للناذر في ذلك اليوم صوم واحد، وكان له أن يصفه بصفة النفلية ،وغيرها ولما نذر به صار واجباً فلا يتصف بالنفلية، ولا يصح غيره حتى يتصف بالنفلية فهذا
